Qabd Before Iftiraq in Salam with Ancillary Contracts
Rules of qabd and iftiraq in salam with ancillary contracts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that qabd (receipt) must occur before iftiraq (separation of original parties) in salam contracts with kafalah, hawala, and rahn, and determine the validity of the ancillary contracts accordingly.

Core Idea
In salam, only the original parties' separation matters for qabd; sureties and transferees are irrelevant.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq clarifies a critical condition for salam contracts when combined with kafalah (surety), hawala (transfer of debt), or rahn (pawn). While qabd (receipt of capital) is required, it does not have to occur in the same majlis (session) as the contract; but it must happen before the two original contracting parties physically separate. This lesson dissects the rulings and reasoning, emphasizing that the presence or absence of ancillary parties does not affect the condition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core condition
The author notes that the base text's phrase 'before separation' indicates that qabd must occur before the original parties separate, but not necessarily in the same majlis. Confirm this distinction.
The key is the moment of iftiraq, not the spatial continuity of the session.
2
Recognize what does not break the condition
From Al-Bazzaziyya, examples show that staying until night, traveling a farsakh, or sleeping do not constitute separation. Understand that separation is physical departure of the original parties.
If the seller enters a house to fetch the capital, visibility matters: hidden = invalid, visible = valid.
3
Apply to kafalah and hawala
When kafalah or hawala is attached to salam, receipt by the muslam ilayh from the surety or transferee completes the contract only if the original parties are still together. Separation of the original parties before qabd voids both the salam and the ancillary contract.
The contract hinges on the original parties, not on the ancillary participants.
4
Analyze the rahn exception
For rahn, the rules differ: if the pawn is destroyed in the majlis with value ≥ capital, the contract is fully completed; if less, partially; if not destroyed and they separate, salam is invalid and the pawn must be returned.
Rahn is treated as a form of constructive receipt until separation.
5
Synthesize the underlying principle
The reasoning is that qabd is a right of the contract, and the contract exists between the original parties. Therefore, only their majlis and separation are considered. This talil (legal reasoning) ensures consistency across ancillary contracts.
Document the authority weight: from Al-Bada'i, a Zahir al-Riwayah source.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ودل قوله «قبل الافتراق دون أن يقول في المجلس على أن القبض في المجلس ليس بشرط . وفي البزازية : وإن مكثا إلى الليل أو سافرا فرسخاً أو أكثر ثم سلم جاز، وإن نام أحدهما أو ناما لم تكن فرقة، ولو أسلم عشرة في كر ولم تكن الدراهم عنده فدخل المنزل ليخرجه إن توارى عن المسلم إليه بطل، وإن بحيث يراه لا وصحت الكفالة والحوالة والارتهان برأس مال المسلم اهـ. وفي البدائع : ثم إذا جازت الحوالة والكفالة فإن قبض المسلم إليه رأس المال من المحتال عليه أو الكفيل أو من رب السلم فقد تم العقد بينهما إذا كانا في المجلس، سواء بقي الحويل أو الكفيل أو افترقا بعد أن كان العاقدان في المجلس. وإن افترق العاقدان بأنفسهما قبل القبض بطل السلم وبطلت الحوالة والكفالة وإن بقي المحال عليه والكفيل في المجلس، والعبرة لبقاء العاقدين وافتراقهما لا لبقاء الحويل والكفيل وافتراقهما لأن القبض من حقوق العقد وقيام العقد بالعاقدين فكان المعتبر بمجلسهما، وعلى هذا الكفالة والحوالة ببدل الصرف. وأما الرهن برأس المال فإن هلك الرهن في المجلس وقيمته مثل رأس المال أو أكثر العقد بينهما، وإن كانت قيمته أقل من رأس المال تم العقد بقدره ويبطل في الباقي، وإن لم يهلك الرهن حتى افترقا بطل السلم لحصول الافتراق لا عن قبض وعليه رد الرهن على صاحبه، وكذا الحكم في بدل الصرف اهـ.
And his statement 'before separation' without saying 'in the majlis' indicates that receipt in the majlis is not a condition. In Al-Bazzaziyya: If they remain until night or travel a farsakh or more and then deliver, it is valid. If one of them sleeps or both sleep, it is not a separation. And if one gives salam for ten kurr while the dirhams are not with him, and he enters the house to bring them out, if he conceals himself from the muslam ilayh, it is invalid; if he is visible, it is valid. And the surety, hawala, and pawn with the capital of the muslam are valid. End. In Al-Bada'i: Then, when hawala and kafalah are valid, if the muslam ilayh receives the capital from the person transferred to (muhtal alayh) or the surety or from the rabb al-salam, the contract between them is completed if they were in the majlis, whether the muhtil or kafil remains or separates after the two contracting parties were in the majlis. If the two contracting parties themselves separate before receipt, the salam is invalidated along with the hawala and kafalah even if the muhtal alayh and kafil remain in the majlis. The consideration is the remaining and separation of the two contracting parties, not the remaining of the muhtil and kafil, because receipt is one of the rights of the contract and the contract is established by the two contracting parties; therefore the consideration is their majlis. Similarly for kafalah and hawala with the currency exchange. As for pawn (rahn) with the capital: if the pawn is destroyed in the majlis and its value equals the capital or more, the contract between them is completed; if the value is less than the capital, the contract is completed for that amount and invalid for the remainder. If the pawn is not destroyed until they separate, the salam is invalidated due to separation without receipt, and the pawn must be returned to its owner. Similarly for the currency exchange. End.
Source pages p. 272

Commentary
This passage clarifies the role of qabd (receipt) and iftiraq (separation) in salam transactions when kafalah (surety), hawala (transfer of debt), and rahn (pawn) are involved. The author first notes that the phrase 'before separation' in the base text implies that receipt does not have to occur in the same majlis (session); the key is that receipt must occur before the contracting parties physically separate. The Bazzaziyya cites examples: staying until night or traveling a farsakh and then delivering is valid, and sleeping does not constitute separation. If the person giving salam enters a house to bring the capital, if he hides from view, it invalidates the contract; if he remains visible, it is valid. Then, from Al-Bada'i, the author explains that once kafalah or hawala is valid, receipt of the capital by the muslam ilayh from the surety or transferee completes the contract only if the original contracting parties are still in the majlis. Separation of the original parties before receipt nullifies both the salam and the ancillary contracts. The reasoning is that qabd is a right of the contract, and the contract hinges on the original parties. For rahn, the rule is different: if the pawn is destroyed in the majlis with value equal to or exceeding the capital, the contract is complete; if less, only partial completion; if the pawn is not destroyed and they separate, the salam is invalidated because separation occurred without receipt. These rules ensure that the essential condition of qabd before iftiraq is maintained.

Key Terms
القبض (qabd): Physical or constructive receipt of the capital (ra's al-mal) in a salam contract.
الافتراق (iftiraq): Physical separation of the two original contracting parties after the contract.
الكفالة (kafalah): Surety, a contract guaranteeing a debt or obligation.
الحوالة (hawala): Transfer of debt from one person to another.
الرهن (rahn): Pawn or collateral given as security for a debt.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a salam contract with hawala, if the original parties separate before the muslam ilayh receives the capital from the muhtal alayh, what is the status of the salam?
A) The salam is valid if the muhtal alayh remains in the majlis
B) The salam becomes fasid (invalid)
C) The salam is suspended until receipt occurs
D) The salam remains valid but the hawala becomes invalid

Key Takeaway
In salam with ancillary contracts, the condition of qabd before iftiraq is strictly with respect to the original contracting parties, not the sureties or transferees.

Void Conditions in Emirate (Imarah): Appointment Remains Valid
Ruling on a void condition in the appointment of an emir.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state that a void condition in the appointment of an emir does not invalidate the appointment and explain the basis in Hanafi fiqh.

Core Idea
A condition unrelated to emirate is void, but the appointment stands valid.

When a caliph appoints an emir with a condition like 'you must ride', the condition is void because it is extraneous to the office. However, the appointment itself remains valid. This lesson explores the ruling and its underlying principle: the primary contract is not nullified by an invalid condition that does not affect its essence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the void condition
The caliph appoints an emir with a condition (e.g., 'on condition that you ride'). Recognize that the condition is not integral to the duties of emirate.
The condition is unrelated to governance.
2
Determine the ruling on the condition
According to the text, the condition is void (fasad) because it is not part of the emirate contract.
3
Determine the effect on the appointment
The appointment (emirate) is still valid; the void condition is ignored and does not nullify it.
4
Understand the rationale
The condition is extraneous to the essence of the appointment. Hanafi fiqh upholds the validity of the primary contract while discarding the void stipulation.
This is analogous to void conditions in other contracts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (والامارة) بأن قال الخليفة وليتك إمارة الشام مثلاً على أن تركب فهذا الشرط فاسد ولا تبطل أمريته بهذا. والإمارة مصدر كالإمرة بالكسر . يقال فلان أمر وأمر عليه إذا كان والياً وقد كان سوقه أي أنه يجرب والتأمير تولية الإمارة يقال هو أمير مؤمر وتأمر عليهم أي تسلط؛ كذا في الصحاح. وفي صحيح البخاري إنكم ستحرصون على الإمارة وستكون ندامة. قوله : (والكفالة بأن قال كفلت غريمك إن أقرضتني، كذا ذكره العيني. وهو مثال لتعليقها بالشرط. وفي البزازية : لو قال كفلت به على أنه متى طولبت به أو كلما طولبت به فلي أجل شهر ،صحت، فإذا طالبه به فله أجل شهر من وقت المطالبة الأولى، فإذا تم الشهر من المطالبة الأولى لزم التسليم ولا يكون للمطالبة الثانية تأجيل اهـ. ثم قال : كفل على أنه بالخيار عشرة أيام أو أكثر يصح بخلاف البيع لأن مبناها على التوسع اهـ.
His statement: (And emirate) that he said: The caliph said, 'I appoint you as emir of Syria, for example, on condition that you ride' – this condition is void and his emirate is not invalidated by it. And 'imarah' is a verbal noun like 'imrah' with kasra. It is said: 'fulan amara wa amara 'alayhi' if he is a governor, and 'qad kana sawqahu' meaning he is experienced? And 'ta'mir' is the act of appointing as emir; it is said 'huwa amir mu'ammar' and 'ta'mara 'alayhim' meaning he dominates; thus in al-Sihah. And in Sahih al-Bukhari: 'You will be eager for emirate, and it will be a regret.' His statement: (And suretyship) that he said: I guarantee your debtor if you lend to me – thus mentioned by al-'Ayni. And it is an example of suspending it on a condition. And in al-Bazzaziyya: If he said, 'I guarantee him on condition that whenever I am demanded, or every time I am demanded, I have a month's grace,' it is valid. So when he demands, he has a month from the first demand; when the month from the first demand ends, delivery is obligatory, and the second demand does not grant a further delay. End. Then he said: He guaranteed with the condition that he has an option for ten days or more – it is valid, unlike a sale, because suretyship is based on leniency. End.
Source pages p. 315

Commentary
This passage covers two topics: emirate (government appointment) and suretyship (kafalah). For emirate, an example of a void condition is the caliph saying 'I appoint you emir on condition that you ride' – the condition is void because it is unrelated to the office, but the appointment itself remains valid. The commentator gives lexical notes. For suretyship, the matn phrase 'والكفالة' is explained. An example of suspending suretyship on a condition is 'I guarantee your debtor if you lend to me' – this is a valid suspension. Then from al-Bazzaziyya: if the guarantor says 'I guarantee him on condition that whenever I am demanded, I have a month's grace,' this is valid; the grace period starts from the first demand. Also, a condition giving the guarantor an option for ten days is valid in suretyship, unlike sale, because suretyship is based on leniency (tawassu'). This shows that conditions that delay or give options are acceptable in kafalah.

Key Terms
الإمارة (al-imarah): Emirate; governorship or leadership of a region.
الشرط الفاسد (al-shart al-fasid): A void condition that does not invalidate the contract.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the imarah example, what is the ruling on the condition 'that you ride'?
A) Valid and binding.
B) Void and invalidates the appointment.
C) Void but the appointment remains valid.
D) The condition is obligatory.

Key Takeaway
A void condition in an appointment does not nullify the appointment; the contract stands.

Kafalah — Surety

Valid Conditions in Suretyship (Kafalah): Grace Period and Option
Rulings on valid conditions in suretyship (kafalah).

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify valid conditions in a suretyship contract (kafalah) such as grace periods and option clauses, and explain why they are permissible due to the leniency principle.

Core Idea
In kafalah, conditions that delay payment or grant an option are valid because suretyship is based on leniency.

Unlike sale, suretyship in Hanafi fiqh is a contract of leniency (tawassu'), allowing more flexibility. This lesson examines conditions like grace periods and options, which are valid in kafalah but would be problematic in sale. We draw from the examples in al-Bazzaziyya.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the condition
The guarantor says: 'I guarantee your debtor on condition that whenever I am demanded, I have a month's grace.' Recognize that this condition modifies the timing of payment.
Condition is a grace period.
2
Determine the ruling on the condition
According to al-Bazzaziyya, the condition is valid (sahih) because kafalah is based on leniency (tawassu').
3
Understand the implementation
The grace period runs from the first demand only. Subsequent demands do not renew the grace period.
The month is a one-time deferral.
4
Compare with sale (bai')
An option condition (e.g., ten days to cancel) is valid in kafalah but not in sale, because sale is strict (gharar avoidance).
This highlights the leniency of kafalah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (والامارة) بأن قال الخليفة وليتك إمارة الشام مثلاً على أن تركب فهذا الشرط فاسد ولا تبطل أمريته بهذا. والإمارة مصدر كالإمرة بالكسر . يقال فلان أمر وأمر عليه إذا كان والياً وقد كان سوقه أي أنه يجرب والتأمير تولية الإمارة يقال هو أمير مؤمر وتأمر عليهم أي تسلط؛ كذا في الصحاح. وفي صحيح البخاري إنكم ستحرصون على الإمارة وستكون ندامة. قوله : (والكفالة بأن قال كفلت غريمك إن أقرضتني، كذا ذكره العيني. وهو مثال لتعليقها بالشرط. وفي البزازية : لو قال كفلت به على أنه متى طولبت به أو كلما طولبت به فلي أجل شهر ،صحت، فإذا طالبه به فله أجل شهر من وقت المطالبة الأولى، فإذا تم الشهر من المطالبة الأولى لزم التسليم ولا يكون للمطالبة الثانية تأجيل اهـ. ثم قال : كفل على أنه بالخيار عشرة أيام أو أكثر يصح بخلاف البيع لأن مبناها على التوسع اهـ.
His statement: (And emirate) that he said: The caliph said, 'I appoint you as emir of Syria, for example, on condition that you ride' – this condition is void and his emirate is not invalidated by it. And 'imarah' is a verbal noun like 'imrah' with kasra. It is said: 'fulan amara wa amara 'alayhi' if he is a governor, and 'qad kana sawqahu' meaning he is experienced? And 'ta'mir' is the act of appointing as emir; it is said 'huwa amir mu'ammar' and 'ta'mara 'alayhim' meaning he dominates; thus in al-Sihah. And in Sahih al-Bukhari: 'You will be eager for emirate, and it will be a regret.' His statement: (And suretyship) that he said: I guarantee your debtor if you lend to me – thus mentioned by al-'Ayni. And it is an example of suspending it on a condition. And in al-Bazzaziyya: If he said, 'I guarantee him on condition that whenever I am demanded, or every time I am demanded, I have a month's grace,' it is valid. So when he demands, he has a month from the first demand; when the month from the first demand ends, delivery is obligatory, and the second demand does not grant a further delay. End. Then he said: He guaranteed with the condition that he has an option for ten days or more – it is valid, unlike a sale, because suretyship is based on leniency. End.

Commentary
This passage covers two topics: emirate (government appointment) and suretyship (kafalah). For emirate, an example of a void condition is the caliph saying 'I appoint you emir on condition that you ride' – the condition is void because it is unrelated to the office, but the appointment itself remains valid. Key Terms
الكفالة (al-kafalah): Suretyship; a contract where one party guarantees the debt or obligation of another.
التوسع (al-tawassu'): Leniency; the principle that allows flexibility in kafalah conditions.

Kafalah — Surety

Conditions in Suretyship: The Principle of Flexibility
Rulings on conditions in suretyship contracts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on conditions in suretyship and distinguish it from sale using the principle of tawassu'.

Core Idea
Suretyship is flexible: conditions like deferment and option are valid, unlike in sale.

In this lesson, we examine the Hanafi position on conditions in suretyship (kafalah). The key principle is that suretyship is based on flexibility (tawassu'), allowing conditions that would void a sale. We will explore the rulings on deferment and option, and the requirement that conditions be suitable.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is suretyship (kafalah) with additional conditions. The ruling is that such conditions are valid (sahih) due to the flexible nature of suretyship.
Conditions that would be void in sale are often valid here.
2
Extract the principle
The passage states that suretyship 'is based on flexibility' (مبناها على التوسع). This is the legal principle that justifies the validity of conditions.
Flexibility means the contract can accommodate extra stipulations.
3
Apply to examples
Examples from the text: (1) Deferment: surety stipulates a month's deferment from demand; (2) Option: surety stipulates a ten-day option period. Both are valid.
The deferment starts from the first demand, and subsequent demands do not renew it.
4
Distinguish from sale
Sale contracts are strict; conditions like option or deferment that cause uncertainty (gharar) are void. Suretyship, being a gratuitous guarantee, allows more flexibility.
This distinction is key in Hanafi fiqh.
5
Note the condition's suitability
The passage mentions that conditions must be 'suitable' (ملائم). Void conditions that are unsuitable do not attach; the suretyship either remains valid without the condition or becomes a mere promise.
Suitability is determined by custom and the nature of the contract.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (والكفالة بأن قال كفلت غريمك إن أقرضتني، كذا ذكره العيني. وهو مثال لتعليقها بالشرط. وفي البزازية : لو قال كفلت به على أنه متى طولبت به أو كلما طولبت به فلي أجل شهر ، صحت، فإذا طالبه به فله أجل شهر من وقت المطالبة الأولى، فإذا تم الشهر من المطالبة الأولى لزم التسليم ولا يكون للمطالبة الثانية تأجيل اهـ. ثم قال : كفل على أنه بالخيار عشرة أيام أو أكثر يصح بخلاف البيع لأن مبناها على التوسع اهـ. وأما تعليقها بالشرط فسيأتي أنه يصح بشرط ملائم. وفي البزازية من البيوع وتعليق الكفالة إن متعارفاً يوم ثلاثة؛ كذا بخط بعض الفضلاء قوله : (والدليل عليه ما في بيوع الذخيرة الخ) قال في النهر : والذي ينبغي حمل ما في الذخيرة على إحدى الروايتين من أنهما لو ألحقا به شرطاً فاسداً لا يلتحق، وعلى أنه لا يلتحق بقي مجرد وعد لا يلزم الوفاء به والله تعالى الموفق .اهـ. فتأمل. قوله: (ويصح تعليقه بالشرط أي تعليق العزل لا القضاء لأن ما ذكره عن البزازية لا يدل عليه ولا تدل عليه العبارة الثانية، نعم سيذكر المؤلف عن الشارح الزيلعي جواز تعليق القضاء والإمارة. قوله : (ومنه (۱) رواه البخاري في كتاب الأحكام باب .. النسائي في كتاب البيعة باب ٣٩. أحمد في مسنده (٢/ .(EEA
His saying: (And suretyship) that he says, 'I am surety for your debtor if you lend me,' as mentioned by al-Ayni. This is an example of suspending it upon a condition. And in al-Bazzaziyyah: If he says, 'I am surety for him on condition that whenever I am demanded or every time I am demanded, I have a month's deferment,' it is valid. When he is demanded, he has a month from the first demand; when the month from the first demand ends, delivery becomes necessary, and there is no deferment for the second demand. Then he said: He became surety on condition that he has an option for ten days or more; it is valid, unlike sale, because suretyship is based on flexibility. As for suspending it upon a condition, it will come that it is valid with a suitable condition. And in al-Bazzaziyyah from the sales: 'and suspending suretyship if it is customary for three days'; as written by some virtuous people. His saying: (And the evidence for it is what is in Buyu' al-Dhakhirah...) He said in al-Nahr: What is appropriate is to interpret what is in al-Dhakhirah according to one of two narrations: that if they attach a void condition to it, it does not attach, and that if it does not attach, it remains a mere promise that does not obligate fulfillment. And Allah is the Grantor of success. So reflect. His saying: (And its suspension upon a condition is valid) meaning suspension of removal, not of judgeship, because what he mentioned from al-Bazzaziyyah does not indicate it, nor does the second expression. But the author will mention from al-Zayla'i the permissibility of suspending judgeship and governorship. His saying: (And from it) [narrated by Bukhari...]
Source pages p. 315

Commentary
This passage discusses conditions in suretyship (kafalah). The author gives an example of suspending suretyship on a condition: 'I am surety for your debtor if you lend me' (this is a conditional offer). From al-Bazzaziyyah: if a surety stipulates that whenever he is demanded, he has a month's respite, this is valid; the month starts from the first demand, and after that month, he must pay. Also, a surety may stipulate an option period (e.g., ten days) to decide, which is valid because suretyship is based on flexibility (tawassu'), unlike sale. The author also mentions that suspending suretyship on a condition is valid if the condition is suitable (mulaim). There is an evidence from al-Dhakhirah about void conditions not attaching, which was covered in the first lesson. The text then clarifies that the phrase 'its suspension upon a condition is valid' refers to suspension of removal (of the surety), not of the judgeship, but later the author will mention that judgeship and governorship can also be suspended. The passage ends with a hadith reference.

Key Terms
كفالة (kafalah): Suretyship; a guarantee to fulfill an obligation if the principal fails.
توسع (tawassu'): Flexibility; the principle that suretyship is more accommodating than sale.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In suretyship, if the surety stipulates an option period of ten days, what is the ruling?
A) The condition is void because it resembles sale conditions.
B) The condition is valid because suretyship is based on flexibility.
C) The suretyship becomes void due to the condition.
D) The option is valid only if the creditor agrees.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, conditions in suretyship are broadly valid due to the principle of flexibility (tawassu'), distinguishing it from the strictness of sale.

Kafalah — Surety
beginner

The Definition and Linguistic Analysis of Kafala
Begins the chapter on Kafala with its legal and linguistic definitions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi legal definition of kafala, identify its key components, and distinguish it from daman linguistically.

Core Idea
Kafala joins one liability to another for claim — not a transfer or mere promise.

This lesson introduces the foundational definition of kafala (guarantee) as formulated in the Hanafi school. The passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq dissects both the linguistic roots and the precise legal meaning, clarifying how the contract operates. Understanding this definition is essential before studying the rulings and conditions of kafala.

Guided Path
1
Locate the legal definition
Find the sentence that gives the definitive legal meaning of kafala. The author states it clearly with the phrase 'هي ضم ذمة إلى ذمة في المطالبة'.
This definition is the core of the chapter.
2
Break down the components
Analyze each part: 'ضم' (joining), 'ذمة' (liability), 'في المطالبة' (for the purpose of claim). Understand that the joining is of two liabilities (original debtor and guarantor) for the creditor's right to demand.
The word 'مطالبة' restricts the joining to the context of claim.
3
Distinguish kafala from daman
Note the author's warning: although some derive daman from damm (joining), the roots are different. Kafala comes from k-f-l, daman from d-m-n. This affects the precise technical usage.
In common speech they may overlap, but in fiqh the distinction is maintained.
4
Understand 'dhimmah'
Define dhimmah as the legal liability or capacity that every human possesses at birth. It is the 'place' where obligations reside. The joining of dhimmahs attaches the guarantor's liability to the original debtor's.
Dhimmah is a key concept in Hanafi legal theory.
5
Recognize the scope of validity
The text states kafala is valid 'for a person' (surety for appearance) and 'for property'. This indicates that kafala covers both the guarantee of a person (kafala bi al-nafs) and guarantee of debt (kafala bi al-mal).
Other conditions and rulings are covered in subsequent lessons.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الكفالة هي ضم ذمة إلى ذمة في المطالبة وتصح بالنفس وإن تعددت بكفلت بنفسه وبما عبر به كتاب الكفالة ذكرها عقب البيوع لأنها غالباً تكون بالثمن أو بالمبيع، ومناسبتها للصرف لأنها تكون آخراً عند الرجوع معاوضة عما يثبت في الذمة من الأثمان، وقدمه عليها لأنه من البيوع والكلام فيها في عشرة مواضع: الأول في معناها لغة قال في المصباح : كفلت بالمال وبالنفس كفلاً من باب قتل وكفولاً أيضاً والاسم الكفالة، وحكى أبو زيد سماعاً من العرب من بابي تعب وقرب، وحكى ابن القطاع كفلت وكفلت به وعنه إذا تحملت به، ويتعدى إلى مفعول ثان بالتضعيف والهمزة فيحذف الحرف فيهما وقد يثبت مع المثقل . قال ابن الانباري : تكفلت بالمال التزمت به وألزمته نفسي وقال أبو زيد تحملت به وقال في المجمع، كفلت به كفلة وكفلت عنه بالمال لغريمه حقوق بينهما، وكفلت الرجل والصغير من باب قتل كفالة أيضاً علته وقت به، ويتعدى بالتضعيف إلى مفعول ثان يقال كفلت زيداً الصغير. والفاعل كفالة المال كفيل به للرجل والمرأة. وقال ابن الأعرابي : وكافل أيضاً مثل ضمين وضامن . وفرق الليث بينهما فقال : الكفيل الضامن والكافل هو الذي يعول إنساناً وينفق عليه . والكفل وزان حمل الضعف من الأجر أو الإثم والكَفَل بفتحتين العجز اهـ. وفي المغرب : الكفيل الضامن وتركيبه دال على الضم والتضمن والكفالة ضم ذمة إلى ذمة في حق المطالبة اهـ. الثاني في معناها شرعاً قد اختلف فيه وقد أشار إلى الأصح بقوله : (هي ضم ذمة إلى ذمة في المطالبة الضم الجمع ومن الفقهاء من جعل الضمان مشتقاً من الضم وهو غلط الاشتقاق لأن نون الضمان أصلية والضم لا نون فيه فهما مادتان مختلفتان ؛ كذا في المصباح. والذمة العهد والأمان والضمان وقولهم في ذمتي كذا أي في ضماني، والجمع ذمم مثل سدرة وسدر ؛ كذا في المصباح. وقال الأصوليون: إن الآدمي يولد وله ذمة صالحة للوجوب عن من جهة كتاب الكفالة قوله : (ثم رأيت صاحب البدائع الخ) قال الغزي قلت: ورأيت بخط قديم على حاشية شرح
Book of Kafala (Guarantee). It is joining a liability to another liability for the purpose of claim. It is valid for a person (surety for the appearance) even if multiple; by saying 'I stand surety for him' and by similar expressions. [The author] placed this book after the book of sales (buyu') because kafala often involves the price or the sold commodity, and its connection to sarf (exchange) is that it occurs later upon recourse as a compensation for what is established in the liability from the prices, and he gave it precedence because it is part of sales. The discussion is in ten topics: The first is its linguistic meaning. It is said in al-Misbah: Kafala with property and with a person, kafalan from the pattern of qatala, and kafoolan also, and the noun is al-kafala. Abu Zayd narrated hearing from the Arabs from the patterns of ta'iba and qaruba. Ibn al-Qatta' narrated: Kafala and kafala bihi, meaning he undertook it. It takes a second object by tashdeed and hamza, and the preposition may be dropped or retained. Ibn al-Anbari said: Takafaltu bil-mal: I undertook it and obligated it upon myself. Abu Zayd said: Tahammaltu bihi. It is said in al-Majma': Kafaltu bihi kafalan, and kafaltu 'anhu bil-mal li-gharimihi huququn baynahuma. And kafaltu al-rajula wa al-sagheera from the pattern of qatala, kafalatan also, meaning I took care of him and fed him. It takes a second object by tashdeed: kafaltu Zaydan al-sagheera. The active participle is kafil al-mal, kafeel for the man and woman. Ibn al-A'rabi said: Also kafil like dameen and damen. Al-Layth distinguished between them: al-kafeel is the guarantor, and al-kafil is the one who supports and spends on someone. And al-kifl (like himl) is the double of reward or sin; al-kafal (with two fathas) is the hip. End. In al-Mughrib: al-kafeel is the guarantor, and its root indicates joining and inclusion. And al-kafala is joining a liability to a liability in the right of claim. End. The second topic: its legal meaning; there is disagreement. The author pointed to the correct view with his statement: 'It is joining a liability to a liability for the purpose of claim.' Al-damm (joining) means combining. Some jurists derived daman (guarantee) from damm (joining), which is an etymological error because the letter noon in al-daman is original, while al-damm has no noon; they are two different roots. As in al-Misbah. Al-dhimmah is covenant, trust, and guarantee. Their statement: 'in my dhimmah so and so' means 'in my guarantee.' The plural is dhimmam like sidrah and sidr. As in al-Misbah. The usooliyyun said: A human being is born with a dhimmah capable of obligation from the aspect of... Book of Kafala: He said: 'Then I saw the author of al-Bada'i' etc.' al-Ghazzi said: I said: And I saw in an old manuscript on the margin of the commentary...
Source pages p. 341

Commentary
This passage opens the chapter on Kafala (guarantee/surety) in al-Bahr al-Ra'iq. The author first provides a concise legal definition: kafala is the joining of one liability (dhimmah) to another liability for the purpose of claim. This means the guarantor's liability is attached to the original debtor's liability so that the creditor can claim from either. The definition uses the phrase 'in the right of claim' because kafala primarily concerns the right to demand payment or performance. The author then explains why this chapter follows the book of sales: since guarantees often involve the price in a sale or the sold item. The connection to sarf (exchange) is also noted because guarantees may arise later as compensation for what is owed. The discussion is structured into ten topics. The first topic is the linguistic meaning of kafala, quoting various authorities like al-Misbah, Abu Zayd, Ibn al-Qatta', Ibn al-Anbari, al-Majma', and al-Mughrib. Key distinctions are made: 'kafeel' is the guarantor (al-damin), while 'kafil' is the caretaker or supporter (one who provides for another). The root indicates joining and inclusion. The second topic is the legal meaning; the author clarifies that the correct definition is joining one liability to another. A caution is given against deriving 'daman' from 'damm' (joining) because the word 'daman' contains a noon, while 'damm' does not; they are separate roots. The passage then defines 'dhimmah' as a legal capacity or liability, and mentions that a human is born with a dhimmah capable of bearing obligations. The text ends abruptly mid-sentence with a quote from al-Ghazzi about a marginal note in a commentary.

Key Terms
كفالة (kafala): A contract of suretyship/guarantee; joining one liability (dhimmah) to another for the purpose of claim.
ذمة (dhimmah): Legal liability or capacity; a trust or covenant that can bear obligations. Every human is born with a dhimmah.
ضمان (daman): Guarantee; often used synonymously with kafala, but the text notes they are not etymologically from the same root. Daman derives from the root d-m-n, while kafala from k-f-l.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) The transfer of debt from one person to another.
B) The joining of one liability to another for the purpose of claim.
C) The guarantee to pay a debt on behalf of another without recourse.
D) A promise to donate money in the future.

Key Takeaway
Kafala is the joining of one liability to another for the purpose of claim, distinct from daman in linguistic root and legal precision.

Kafalah — Surety
intermediate
12 min
Definition of Kafalah and the Claim vs Debt Dispute
Defines kafalah and debates join in claim vs debt

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi legal definition of kafalah and explain why the relied-upon view limits it to joining dhimmas in the claim (matalabah) rather than the debt.

Core Idea
Kafalah joins dhimmas for claim, not debt—the guarantor is not a co-debtor.

The chapter on kafalah (suretyship) begins with a precise definition: it is joining a dhimma to a dhimma in the claim. This seemingly simple statement conceals a significant intra-Hanafi dispute about whether the guarantor (kafil) becomes liable for the debt itself or only for being pursued by the creditor. The preferred Hanafi position, supported by analogies from agency and trusteeship, is that the debt remains solely on the principal; the guarantor merely provides an additional source of demand for the creditor. Understanding this distinction is essential for knowing the practical consequences, such as what happens when the guarantor dies or when the creditor releases the principal.

Guided Path
1
Identify the linguistic and legal definitions
First, read the passage's linguistic analysis of kafalah, noting the key distinction between 'kafil' (guarantor) and 'kafil' (supporter). Then focus on the legal definition: 'ضم ذمة إلى ذمة في المطالبة' — joining a dhimma to a dhimma in the claim. Understand that 'dhimma' is the legal capacity for obligation, and 'matalabah' is the right to demand payment or performance.
The linguistic analysis helps clarify that kafalah derives from inclusion/encompassment, not from the word 'daman' which has a different root.
2
Locate the central dispute
The passage states that some Hanafi scholars (mashayikh) hold that kafalah joins dhimmas in the debt itself, i.e., the debt is also established in the kafil's dhimma. However, the author indicates the chosen view (al-mukhtar) is that it is only in the claim. Identify the arguments for both sides presented in the text.
Al-Mabsut does not prefer one view, but later authorities (Fath al-Qadir, Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq) adopt the claim-only view.
3
Analyze the Hanafi proof for the claim-only view
The passage provides analogies: agents, trustees, guardians, and masters of authorized slaves are all demanded for payment or return even though nothing is in their dhimma. This shows that being subject to a claim does not require being a debtor. Also, if kafalah created a debt, releasing the principal should release the kafil, but the opposite is true: releasing the principal releases the kafil, but releasing the kafil does not release the principal.
The text also rebuts the opposing argument from gift of debt (hiba), explaining it as a procedural validation.
4
Identify the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
The passage explicitly states the benefit of the preferred view: if the kafil dies after the principal becomes insolvent, the creditor cannot take from the kafil's estate on the claim-only view, but could on the debt view. Additionally, if the creditor releases the principal, the kafil is released (on the preferred view), but not vice versa.
This practical outcome shows that the claim-only view protects the kafil's heirs from an unintended debt burden.
5
State the mufta bihi position and its authority
The relied-upon position in the Hanafi school is that kafalah is joining dhimmas in the claim only. This is the view of the author of Kanz al-Daqa'iq and is confirmed by the commentator in Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq as 'al-mukhtar'. It is from Zahir al-Riwayah and adopted by the majority of later scholars.
This is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf, not a dispute between madhhabs, though the Shafi'i school similarly holds a debt-joining view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الكفالة

Chapter of Kafalah. It is joining a dhimma to a dhimma in the claim. It is valid for the person even if multiple, [e.g.] "I guarantee him by his person and by what is expressed by it." Chapter of Kafalah. He mentioned it after sales because it is often for the price or the sold item, and its suitability for exchange because it occurs at the end upon return as compensation for what is established in the dhimma of prices, and he gave precedence to it because it is from sales, and the discussion about it is in ten places. First: its meaning linguistically. He said in al-Misbah: Kafaltu bi-al-mal and bi-al-nafs kafalan from the verb qatala and kafwlan also, and the noun is kafalah. Abu Zayd narrated hearing from the Arabs from the two patterns of ta'iba and qaruba. Ibn al-Qatta' narrated kafaltu and kafaltu bihi and 'anhu meaning I undertook it. It becomes transitive to a second object by tashdid and hamza, dropping the preposition in both, and sometimes it is retained with the heavy form. Ibn al-Anbari said: Takaffaltu bi-al-mal: I undertook it and made it binding on myself. Abu Zayd said: Tahammaltu bihi. He said in al-Mujamma': Kafaltu bihi kafalah and kafaltu 'anhu bi-al-mal for his creditor, rights between them, and kafaltu al-rajul and al-saghir from the verb qatala kafalahan also: I took care of him and protected him. It becomes transitive by tashdid to a second object; one says kafaltu Zaydan al-saghir. The agent of kafalah of property is kafil bihi for the man and the woman. Ibn al-A'rabi said: And kafil also like damin and daman. And al-Layth distinguished between them saying: al-kafil is the guarantor, and al-kafil is the one who supports a person and spends on him. Al-kifl (like himl) is the double of reward or sin, and al-kafal (with two fatha) is the inability. End. And in al-Mughrib: al-kafil is the guarantor, and its composition indicates inclusion and encompassment, and kafalah is joining a dhimma to a dhimma in the right of claim. End. Second: its meaning in Sacred Law. There is disagreement about it, and he pointed to the soundest view by his saying: (It is joining a dhimma to a dhimma in the claim). Al-damm is gathering. Some jurists derived daman from damm, but that is an error in derivation because the nun of daman is original and damm has no nun, so they are two different roots. Thus in al-Misbah. Al-dhimmah is covenant, trust, and guarantee. Their saying "in my dhimmah so-and-so" means in my guarantee. The plural is dhimam like sidrah and sidr. Thus in al-Misbah. The usuliyyun said: The human is born with a dhimmah capable of obligation for and against him. In al-Tahrir: al-dhimmah is a legal description by which one has eligibility for what is for and against him. Fakhr al-Islam interpreted it as the self and the neck that has a covenant, and the meaning is that it is the covenant. Their saying "in his dhimmah" means in his self by virtue of its covenant, by way of naming the state and intending the place. End. Al-matalabah is from talabtuhu mutalabatan and tilaban from the verb qatala. Thus in al-Misbah. The upshot: The kafil and the makful 'anhu become claimants for the makful lahu, whether what is sought from one is the same as what is sought from the other as in kafalah bi-al-mal, or not as in kafalah bi-al-nafs, for what is sought from the principal is money and from the kafil is the production of the person. The word mutalabah in its absolute sense encompasses both. This is according to some. And Muskayn asserted that what is sought from both is one thing: the surrender of the person, for what is required of him is the surrender of himself, and the kafil has undertaken it. It was restricted to mutalabah to rebut the one who said it is joining in the debt, so the debt is established in the dhimmah of the kafil without falling from the principal. And in al-Mabsut, neither of the two views is preferred over the other. What is thought to be a preventing factor from the necessity of one debt becoming two debts according to this view in al-Mabsut is that there is no preventing factor because it is not collected except from one of them, like the usurper with the usurper's usurper, for each is liable for the value, and the owner's right is only in one value because he only collects from one of them, and his choice to hold one liable frees the other. Similarly here, but here it is by possession, no. Among what indicates the establishment of the debt in the dhimmah of the kafil is that if he gifted the debt to the kafil, it is valid, and the kafil would then claim it from the principal, whereas a gift of debt from someone other than the debtor is not valid. He repelled [this argument] by [saying] that it is by his mere choice. The conclusion: Establishing the debt in the dhimmah is a legal consideration, so it is permissible to consider one thing in two dhimmas. It is only impossible for an actual entity to be established in one time in two real directions. But the chosen view is what the author mentioned: that it is regarding mere claim, not the debt, because considering it in two dhimmas, even if possible legally, does not necessitate ruling on every possibility except with a cause. No, because assurance is obtained by the claim, and that is a necessitating cause. And there is no need for establishing the consideration of the debt in the dhimmah like the agent for purchase is demanded for the price while it is in the dhimmah of the principal; thus in Fath al-Qadir. Similarly the executor, guardian, and supervisor are demanded for what must be paid while nothing is in their dhimmas. Similarly, every trustee is demanded to return the trust while nothing is in his dhimmah. Similarly, the master of an authorized slave in debt is demanded to sell him or ransom him while no debt is upon him. As for the answer regarding hibah and ibra', we consider it in the ruling of two debts to validate the act of the right-holder, and that is in his presence. Before that, there is no necessity or motive for it. Al-Majma' of Ibn Malik: The benefit of kafalah being joining of dhimma to dhimma in the claim, not in the debt, according to the sounder view, is that if the kafil dies after it becomes impossible to collect from the principal, the claimant takes the debt from the estate of the kafil according to some mashayikh because he is a debtor according to their view, but he does not take according to the soundest view because the claimant's right against the kafil is only in the claim. Similarly, if the claimant releases the principal, the kafil is released without the opposite. This is according to the correct view. As for some mashayikh, neither the kafil nor the principal is released by the claimant's release.
Source pages p. 341

Commentary
This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq, a commentary on Kanz al-Daqa'iq, opens the chapter of kafalah (suretyship). It first provides a linguistic definition of kafalah, citing several Arabic lexicons (al-Misbah, Abu Zayd, Ibn al-Qatta', etc.) and distinguishes between kafil (guarantor) and kafil (supporter). The legal definition is given as 'joining a dhimma to a dhimma in the claim' (ضم ذمة إلى ذمة في المطالبة). The author then clarifies that dhimma is a legal capacity for obligation. The central discussion is whether kafalah joins the two dhimmas in the debt itself or only in the claim (matalabah). The text presents arguments for both sides: some say the debt transfers to the kafil's dhimma, supporting this with analogies to usurpers and the validity of gifting the debt to the kafil. The preferred view (al-mukhtar) is that kafalah only joins dhimmas in the claim, not the debt, meaning the kafil is merely demanded to produce the principal or pay if the principal fails. This is supported by analogies with agents, trustees, and guardians who are demanded for what is not in their dhimmas. The practical consequence (thamarah) is that if the kafil dies after the principal becomes insolvent, the claimant does not take from the kafil's estate (on the soundest view), and releasing the principal releases the kafil but not vice versa. This is an intra-Hanafi dispute, with some mashayikh holding the debt-based view, but the relied-upon position is the claim-only view.

Key Terms
الكفالة (al-kafalah): A contract by which a person (kafil) joins his dhimma to another's dhimma in the claim, guaranteeing the appearance or debt of the principal.
الذمة (al-dhimmah): A legal attribute by which a person has capacity for obligation; it is the covenant or guarantee that makes one liable for rights and duties.
المطالبة (al-matalabah): The claim or demand; the right to seek fulfillment from either the principal or the guarantor. It is the key element in kafalah according to the preferred view.
الكفيل (al-kafil): The guarantor or surety; the person who joins his dhimma to the principal's dhimma in the claim.
المكفول عنه (al-makful 'anhu): The principal debtor or person for whom the guarantee is made; the one on whose behalf the kafil acts.
المكفول له (al-makful lahu): The creditor or beneficiary of the guarantee; the one to whom the claim is owed.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the preferred Hanafi view, what is the practical consequence if the creditor releases the principal from the debt?
A) The kafil remains liable because his debt is separate.
B) The kafil is also released from the guarantee.
C) The kafil becomes the sole debtor.
D) The guarantee is unaffected and the kafil must still pay.

Key Takeaway
Kafalah joins dhimmas in the claim only, not in the debt; thus the kafil is not a co-debtor, and the practical effects (estate liability, release) follow accordingly.

Kafalah — Surety

The Legal Nature of Kafalah: Debt or Demand?
Intra-Hanafi dispute on the legal nature of kafalah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two Hanafi views on the nature of kafalah and apply the mufta bihi view to determine the ruling when a surety swears he has no debt.

Core Idea
Kafalah in Hanafi is inclusion in demand, not debt—the surety is not a debtor but subject to claim.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explores a fundamental dispute within the Hanafi school: when a person becomes a surety (kafil), does he become a co-debtor, or does he merely become liable to be demanded? The answer affects practical rulings, such as whether a surety breaks his oath if he swears he has no debt. We will trace the arguments, identify the practical consequence, and determine the relied-upon position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two competing definitions of kafalah
From the passage, extract the definitions: inclusion in the debt (ضم في الدين) and inclusion in the demand (ضم في المطالبة). Note which scholars support each view.
The passage mentions the Shaykh Imam (likely Abu Hanifa) as supporting the debt view, and Abu Yusuf as supporting the demand view.
2
Locate the textual proofs for each definition
Find in the text the reasoning behind each view. For the debt view, the claim that debt is an action; for the demand view, the argument that the debt view fails to cover kafalah for the person (kafalah bi'l-nafs).
The passage cites Fath al-Qadir on Abu Hanifa's statement that 'debt is an action'.
3
Analyze the thamarah al-khilaf: the surety's oath
The passage presents a scenario: a surety swears 'I have no debt.' Determine the outcome under each definition. According to the sound opinion (ashah), he does not break his oath; according to the weak opinion, he does.
This is a clear practical difference that shows the importance of the definition.
4
Determine the mufta bihi position and its reasoning
Identify which view is relied upon for fatwa. The passage indicates that the author of al-Bahr al-Ra'iq and the matn (Kanz al-Daqa'iq) follow the demand view. Also note the criticism from al-Nahr of the opposite interpretation.
The mufta bihi is that kafalah is inclusion in demand, and this is consistent with the majority of Hanafi scholars.

كتاب الكفالة

الخلاف نشأ

وفي فتح القدير : ولا يخفى أن ما نقل من قول أبي حنيفة أن الدين فعل يقتضي أن يكون في ذمة الكفيل أيضاً كما هو في ذمة الأصيل إذ فعل الأداء واجب عليه اهـ. وقد يقال إنما وجب عليه لإسقاط المطالبة عنه وأبو حنيفة إنما جعله فعلاً لسقوطه عن الميت إذ لا يتأتى الفعل منه فلم تصح الكفالة عن ميت ،مفلس، وليس مراده أن حقيقته الفعل لأنه وصف قائم بالذمة، وإنما مراده أن المقصود منه الفعل كما لا يخفى. وقد صرحوا في مواضع بأنه وصف ولذا قالوا : الديون تقضى بأمثالها لأن ما في الذمة لا يمكن تسليمه. وفي الإيضاح أخذاً من الغاية أن تعريفها بالضم في الدين لا ينتظم الكفالة بالنفس والكفالة بالعين والكفالة بالفعل اهـ . قلت : نعم لا يشمل لكن المعرف لها بذلك إنما أراد تعريف الكفالة بالمال فإن أصل من أن الكفيل هل يثبت في ذمته المال أو لا . ثم رأيت صاحب البدائع أشار إلى ذلك في بيان حكمها ولم يذكر الشارحون لهذا الاختلاف ثمرة فإن الاتفاق على أن الدين لا يستوفي إلا من أحدهما وأن الكفيل مطالب وأن هبة الدين له صحيحة ويرجع به على الأصيل ولو اشترى الطالب بالدين شيئاً من الكفيل صح مع أن الشراء بالدين من غير من عليه الدين ويمكن أن يقال : إنها تظهر فيما إذا حلف الكفيل أن لا دين عليه فعلى الأصح لا يصح . يحنث، وعلى الضعيف يحنث وجهد المقل دموعه وسيأتي عند قوله وبطل تعليق البراءة من الكفالة بالشرط ما يقتضي أن يكون ثمرة . وفي الخانية : رجل ادعى على غيره أنه ضمن له فلان الغائب كذا كذا درهماً قال الشيخ الإمام يحلفه بالله ما له عليك هذا المال من الوجه الذي يدعي. وعن أبي يوسف إن عرض المدعى عليه للقاضي فإنه يحلفه بالله ما له عليك هذا من الوجه الذي يدعي وإن لم يعرض حلفه بالله ما ضمن والتعريض أن يقول المدعى عليه أن الرجل قد يضمن مالاً ثم يؤدي أو يبرئه الطالب أو يؤديه المضمون عنه فيبرأ الضامن اهـ. وينبغي أن يكون قول الشيخ الإمام مفرعاً على أنها للضم في الدين، وما عن أبي يوسف مفرع على الأصح كما لا يخفى. ومما يضعف أنها الضم في الدين أن المديون لو دفع الدين ثم كفل به إنسان قالوا لا يصح مع قولهم ببقاء الدين بعد الدفع وأن الساقط المطالبة بالألفاظ

قوله : (وينبغي أن يكون قول الشيخ الإمام الخ) قال في النهر : هذا وهم منشؤه توهم أن قوله ماله عليك هذا المال يفيد ما ادعاه وليس كذلك إذ معناه ماله عليك المطالبة به، وكيف يصح ما ادعاه

Book of Kafalah. The dispute arose. In Fath al-Qadir: It is not hidden that what is transmitted from Abu Hanifa's statement that the debt is an action implies that it exists in the dhimmah of the surety as well, just as it is in the dhimmah of the principal, for performing the action is obligatory upon him. It may be said that it became obligatory upon him to remove the demand from him. Abu Hanifa only made it an action because it is dropped from the deceased, since action cannot come from him, so kafalah from a deceased insolvent is not valid. He did not mean that its reality is action because it is a description standing in the dhimmah; rather he meant that the intended purpose is action, as is not hidden. They have stated in places that it is a description, and thus they said: Debts are repaid with their likes because what is in the dhimmah cannot be delivered. In al-Idah, taking from the ultimate purpose, that defining it as inclusion in the debt does not cover kafalah for the person, kafalah for an item, and kafalah for an action. I say: Yes, it does not include, but the one defining it that way only intended the definition of kafalah for wealth, for the root is whether wealth is established in the surety's dhimmah or not. Then I saw the author of al-Bada'i indicated that in explaining its ruling, and the commentators did not mention a consequence for this difference, for it is agreed that the debt is only collected from one of them, that the surety is demanded, that gifting the debt to him is valid and he recovers it from the principal, and if the creditor buys something from the surety with the debt it is valid, even though buying with a debt from someone other than the debtor [is otherwise]. It can be said that it appears when the surety swears that he has no debt: according to the sound opinion he does not break his oath, and according to the weak opinion he breaks his oath. And the effort of the impoverished [is in tears?]. It will come when he says 'and annulment of the suspension of acquittal from kafalah by condition' what indicates that there is a consequence. In al-Khaniyah: A man claimed against another that he guaranteed for him so-and-so the absent person such-and-such dirhams. The Shaykh Imam said: He administers an oath to him by Allah saying 'You have no right to this money from the aspect you claim.' And from Abu Yusuf: If the defendant presents to the judge, he administers an oath to him by Allah 'You have no right to this from the aspect you claim,' but if he does not present, he administers an oath 'You did not guarantee.' And ta'rid (indirect expression) is that the defendant says 'A man may guarantee wealth, then pay or the creditor acquits him or the principal pays, so the guarantor becomes free.' It should be that the Shaykh Imam's statement is based on it being inclusion in the debt, and what is from Abu Yusuf is based on the sound opinion, as is not hidden. And what weakens it being inclusion in the debt is that if the debtor paid the debt and then someone guaranteed it, they said it is not valid, despite their statement that the debt remains after payment, and that what falls is the demand [for it] with words? And likewise the opposite, because each one is a debtor and demanded, but he did not attribute it to a book, so it should be checked from reliable books. In the margin of al-Ramli: I say: Its reasoning is clear, and it will come in the matn that if the principal is acquitted or his payment is deferred, the surety is acquitted and his payment is deferred. And the author followed the opinion that it is inclusion in demand, and it will come from al-Ghazzi that what is in the reliable books is that the money becomes due upon the death of the surety and is taken from his estate. It is reversed. The statement: 'It should be that the statement of the Shaykh Imam...' Al-Nahr said: This is a misconception arising from the illusion that his statement 'you have no right to this money' supports what he claimed, but it is not so, because its meaning is 'you have no right to demand it.' So how can what he claimed be correct? The following: And Abu Yusuf in his later statement did not make acceptance a pillar, so he made kafalah complete by the surety alone in wealth and person.
Source pages p. 343

Commentary
This passage from the commentary discusses the fundamental legal nature of kafalah (suretyship). The author of al-Bahr al-Ra'iq is commenting on a matn (likely Kanz al-Daqa'iq) and delves into the dispute about whether kafalah is 'ضم في الدين' (inclusion in the debt itself) or 'ضم في المطالبة' (inclusion in the demand/right to claim). The passage cites Fath al-Qadir stating that Abu Hanifa considered the debt as an action (فعل) — meaning the obligation to pay, which is an act — so it exists in the dhimma of both principal and surety. However, this is nuanced: Abu Hanifa's view that kafalah is not valid from a deceased insolvent shows that he does not treat it as an actual action but as a description (وصف) in the dhimma that gives rise to the obligation to act. The distinction matters because if kafalah were inclusion in the debt itself, then it would not cover kafalah for the person (kafalah bi'l-nafs) or for an item (kafalah bi'l-ayn). The author notes that the definition by inclusion in the debt is meant only for kafalah for wealth. Then the passage discusses whether this difference has any practical consequence (thamar): it is agreed that the debt can only be collected from one of the two (principal or surety), but there is a specific scenario regarding an oath by the surety that he owes no debt. According to the sound opinion (al-ashah) that kafalah is inclusion in demand, the surety does not break his oath if he swears 'I have no debt' because the actual debt is not in his dhimma. According to the weak opinion (al-da'if), he would break his oath. The passage then discusses a fatwa from al-Khaniyah and the difference between the Shaykh Imam (likely Abu Hanifa? Or later scholar) and Abu Yusuf regarding the wording of the oath. It then quotes al-Nahr criticizing another scholar's misunderstanding. The correct view consistent with the relied-upon position is that kafalah is inclusion in the demand for payment, not in the debt itself. This is the view followed by the author of the matn (Kanz). The passage ends with a note that Abu Yusuf in his later opinion did not make acceptance a pillar of kafalah, which relates to the next section.

Key Terms
كفالة (kafalah): A contract of suretyship where one person (the kafil) undertakes to fulfill an obligation on behalf of another (the asil).
ضم في الدين (damm fi al-dayn): The view that kafalah means the surety's dhimma is joined to the debt itself, making him a co-debtor.
ضم في المطالبة (damm fi al-mutalabah): The view that the surety is joined in the right of the creditor to demand payment, not in the debt itself.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon Hanafi position, what is the legal nature of kafalah?
A) It is inclusion in the debt (ضم في الدين).
B) It is inclusion in the demand (ضم في المطالبة).
C) It is a transfer of ownership.
D) It is a sale of debt.

Key Takeaway
Kafalah in Hanafi fiqh is inclusion in demand, not in the debt; the surety is not a debtor but is subject to claim.

Pillars and Conditions of Kafalah
Explains the pillar (rukn) and conditions (shurut) of kafalah, including an intra-Hanafi dispute on acceptance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the pillars and conditions of kafalah according to the Hanafi school, and distinguish between the views of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf regarding acceptance as a pillar.

Core Idea
In kafalah, the majority require both offer and acceptance; Abu Yusuf's later view dispenses with acceptance but leaves the contract incomplete until the creditor's permission.

Kafalah (suretyship) is a contract that requires certain integral parts and conditions for validity and enforceability. The Hanafi school has a nuanced internal dispute about whether acceptance (qabul) is a pillar of the contract. Additionally, the surety must meet specific conditions: sanity and adulthood for validity, and freedom for execution. Understanding these elements is essential to correctly apply the law of guarantees.

Guided Path
1
Identify the pillar and the dispute
Read the text's statement on the rukn: 'They said it is offer and acceptance.' Note that Abu Yusuf in his later opinion did not consider acceptance a pillar. Determine the two positions and their practical consequence.
The standard view (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) requires both ijab and qabul; Abu Yusuf's later view completes the contract with only the surety's offer.
2
Understand the thamarah (consequence) of the dispute
The text mentions the case where the creditor dies before acceptance. Apply each view: if acceptance is required, death prevents formation and surety is not liable; if not, the contract may be executed or suspended.
This shows how an usul difference affects real-world outcomes.
3
Distinguish between shart al-in'iqaad and shart al-nafaadh
The text divides conditions into those for validity (like sanity and adulthood) and those for execution (like freedom). Understand that missing a validity condition makes the contract batil, while missing an execution condition makes it mawquf.
This classification is a hallmark of Hanafi fiqh.
4
Apply the exception for minors
A minor's kafalah is generally invalid, but an exception exists when a guardian borrows for the orphan's maintenance and the minor guarantees that debt. Examine why this exception is allowed and why it does not extend to guaranteeing the person.
The key is that the debt is already binding on the minor, so the guarantee merely strengthens it.

الثالث في بيان ركنها قالوا هو الإيجاب والقبول بالألفاظ الآتية. ولم يجعل أبو يوسف في قوله الأخير القبول ركنها فجعلها تتم بالكفيل وحده في المال والنفس. واختلف على قوله فقيل يتوقف على إجازة الطالب وقيل تنفذ وللطالب الرد. وثمرة الخلاف فيما إذا مات المكفول له قبل القبول، فمن قال بالتوقف قال لا يؤاخذ الكفيل. الرابع في شرائطها وهي أربعة أنواع في الكفيل والأصيل والطالب والمكفول به . ثم منها ما هو شرط الانعقاد ومنها ما هو شرط النفاذ، أما شرائط الكفيل فالعقل والبلوغ وهما شرطان للانعقاد فلا ينعقد كفالة مجنون وصبي إلا إذا استدان الولي ديناً في نفقة اليتيم وأمره بأن يضمن المال عنه فإنه صحيح، ولو أمره بكفالة نفسه عنه لم يجز لأن ضمان الدين قد لزمه من غير شرط فالشرط لا يزيده إلا تأكيداً لم يكن متبرعاً. فأما ضمان النفس وهو تسليم نفس الأب أو الوصي فلم يكن عليه فكان متبرعاً به فلم يجز، والحرية شرط نفاذها

قال في النهر: ثم رأيته في المحيط المسألة إلى المبسوط ولفظه في كفالة الصبي : وإذا استدان له أبوه أو وصيه وأمر أن يكفل عنه في الدين وبنفسه جازت الكفالة بالدين دون النفس لأن الأب أو الوصي متى استدان على الصبي للنفقة

عز
The third section: On explaining its pillar. They said it is offer and acceptance with the following wordings. And Abu Yusuf in his later statement did not make acceptance a pillar, so he made kafalah complete by the surety alone in wealth and person. And there is disagreement on his statement: it is said it is suspended upon the creditor's permission, and it is said it is executed and the creditor has the right to reject. The consequence of the difference is when the creditor dies before acceptance: the one who says it is suspended says the surety is not held liable. The fourth section: On its conditions, and they are four types: regarding the surety, the principal, the creditor, and the guaranteed item. Then among them are conditions for validity (shart al-in'iqaad) and conditions for execution (shart al-nafaadh). As for the conditions of the surety: sanity and puberty, and these are conditions for validity, so the suretyship of an insane person and a minor is not valid, except when the guardian borrows a debt for the orphan's maintenance and orders him to guarantee the wealth, then it is valid. If he orders him to guarantee his own person, it is not permitted, because the guarantee of debt has become incumbent upon him without condition, so the condition only strengthens it, and he is not a volunteer. As for guaranteeing the person, which is handing over the father or guardian's person, it was not incumbent upon him, so he would be a volunteer, so it is not permitted. And freedom is a condition for execution. Al-Nahr said: Then I saw in al-Muhit the issue transmitted to al-Mabsut, and its wording regarding the minor's suretyship: If his father or guardian borrows for him and orders him to guarantee for him in the debt and his person, the suretyship for the debt is valid but not for the person, because whenever the father or guardian borrows on the orphan for maintenance...
Source pages p. 344

Commentary
This passage systematically explains first the pillars (rukn) of kafalah, then its conditions (shurut). Regarding the pillar, the standard view is that kafalah requires both offer (ijab) and acceptance (qabul) using specific wordings. However, Abu Yusuf in his later opinion (qawlih al-akhir) did not consider acceptance a pillar; he held that kafalah is completed by the surety alone, whether for wealth or for person. This leads to two opinions on his view: one that the contract is suspended on the creditor's permission, and another that it is executed but the creditor has the right to reject. The practical consequence is illustrated if the creditor dies before acceptance: if acceptance is a condition, then death before acceptance means the surety is not liable; but if not, he may be liable. Then the conditions: there are four categories (surety, principal, creditor, guaranteed item), and within them some are conditions for the contract's existence (shart al-in'iqaad) and some for its effectiveness (shart al-nafaadh). For the surety, the conditions for validity (in'iqaad) are sanity and adulthood. Thus, a madman's or child's kafalah is invalid. An exception: if a guardian borrows money for the orphan's necessary maintenance and then instructs the orphan child to act as surety for that debt, this suretyship is valid despite the child's minority, because the debt is already binding and the child's guarantee only strengthens it. However, if the guardian instructs the child to guarantee the guardian's own person (i.e., to hand over himself), it is invalid because the child would be a volunteer without obligation. Similarly, guarantee of the person (kafalah bi'l-nafs) by a father or guardian is not valid because it is a voluntary act without pre-existing obligation. Freedom is a condition for execution (nafaadh): a slave's kafalah is suspended upon the master's consent. The marginal note from al-Nahr clarifies the ruling from al-Muhit regarding a minor's suretyship: the suretyship for a debt is valid but for the person is not, based on the borrowing by the guardian for the orphan's maintenance.

Key Terms
ركن (rukn): An integral part of the contract without which it is not valid.
شرط الانعقاد (shart al-in'iqaad): A condition for the validity and existence of the contract. If missing, the contract is invalid (batil).
شرط النفاذ (shart al-nafaadh): A condition for the enforceability of the contract; if missing, the contract is suspended (mawquf) but not invalid.
كفيل (kafil): The surety/guarantor who undertakes the obligation.
أصيل (asil): The principal debtor on whose behalf the surety guarantees.
طالب (talib): The creditor (the one who holds the right to demand).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the practical consequence (thamarah) of the dispute about acceptance (qabul) being a pillar of kafalah?
A) Whether the surety can revoke the guarantee.
B) Whether the creditor can demand payment immediately.
C) Whether the surety is liable if the creditor dies before accepting.
D) Whether the principal can dismiss the surety.

Key Takeaway
Kafalah requires both offer and acceptance (majority view); the surety must be sane and adult for validity, and free for execution, with a narrow exception for minors when guaranteeing an already binding maintenance debt.

Kafalah — Surety
intermediate

The Pillar of Kafalah: Abu Yusuf's Ikhtilaf on Acceptance
Intra-Hanafi dispute on rukn of kafalah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the intra-Hanafi dispute on the rukn of kafalah and apply the thamarah al-khilaf to determine the guarantor's liability when the guaranteed-for dies before acceptance.

Core Idea
The rukn of kafalah is offer and acceptance per the majority, but Abu Yusuf's later view drops acceptance, altering liability if the claimant dies.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq clarifies the pillar (rukn) of the kafalah contract. The majority Hanafi view requires both offer and acceptance, but Imam Abu Yusuf later held that acceptance is not a pillar. Understanding this difference is essential for determining when a guarantor becomes liable, especially if the beneficiary dies before accepting. The commentary by al-Ramli expands on the nuances.

Guided Path
1
State the majority Hanafi position on the rukn of kafalah
Identify what Abu Hanifa, Muhammad, and early Abu Yusuf consider essential for the contract's validity. Note the requirement of offer (ijab) and acceptance (qabul) using specific wordings.
The majority view is that acceptance is a rukn; without it, the kafalah is not complete.
2
Identify Abu Yusuf's later opinion and its two interpretations
Contrast the majority with Abu Yusuf's later view that acceptance is not a rukn. Then note the two interpretations: some say the contract is suspended on the claimant's approval, others say it is immediately effective but the claimant can reject.
These interpretations lead to different practical outcomes.
3
Trace the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence)
Apply the dispute to the scenario where the guaranteed-for (makful lahu) dies before acceptance. Explain why, under the suspension view, the guarantor is not liable, while under the immediate effect view, the guarantor may be liable unless the claimant rejects.
This shows the legal impact of the ikhtilaf.
4
Examine al-Ramli's expansion on the conditions
Read al-Ramli's commentary that clarifies the majority view: acceptance can occur in the session or later, and discusses scenarios involving minors and merchants. Understand that according to Abu Yusuf's view, acceptance is not required even after the death of the parties.
This expansion helps solidify the boundaries of the ikhtilaf.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الكفالة الثالث في بيان ركنها قالوا هو الإيجاب والقبول بالألفاظ الآتية. ولم يجعل أبو يوسف في قوله الأخير القبول ركناً فجعلها تتم بالكفيل وحده في المال والنفس. واختلف على قوله فقيل يتوقف على إجازة الطالب وقيل تنفذ وللطالب الرد. وثمرة الخلاف فيما إذا مات المكفول له قبل القبول، فمن قال بالتوقف قال لا يؤاخذ الكفيل. قوله : (الثالث في بيان ركنها قالوا هو الإيجاب والقبول) قال الرملي : أقول : أي عندهما وهو قول أبي يوسف أولاً حتى إنها لا تتم بالكفيل وحده ما لم يوجد قبول المكفول له أو قبول أجنبي عنه في مجلس العقد أو خطاب المكفول له أو خطاب أجنبي عنه بأن قال الطالب أكفل بنفس فلان لي فقال كفلت أو قال رجل أجنبي لغيره اكفل بنفس فلان أو قال . فلان عن لفلان فيقول ذلك الغير كفلت تصح الكفالة، وتقف على ما وراء المجلس على إجازة المكفول له وللكفيل أن يخرج نفسه عن الكفالة قبل أن يجيز الغائب ،كفالته، أما إذا لم يوجد شيء من ذلك فقال الكفيل كفلت بنفس فلان لفلان أو بما لفلان على فلان من الدين فإنها لا تقف على ما وراء المجلس حتى لو بلغ الطالب فقبل لم تصح ؛ تتارخانية. وفي جامع الفصولين الكفالة للصبي لم تجز. قيل له : هو حجر على المضار لا المنافع بدليل قبول الهدية والصدقة وفي هذا منفعة فتجوز. قال: الهبة والصدقة تصح بالفعل وفعله معتبر وأما هنا فلا بد من قول وقوله لم يعتبر؛ كذا ذكره في الكفالة. وذكر في الأحكام لو كان الصبي تاجراً صحت الكفالة ولو خاطب عنه أجنبي وقبل عنه توقفت على إجازة وكيله، فإن لم يخاطب أجنبي ولا وليه وإنما خاطب الصبي عند أبي حنيفة ومحمد رحمهما الله تعالى لا تصح، وعند أبي يوسف تصح .اهـ. والولي الأب أو الجد عند عدمه أو الوصي من أحدهما أو القاضي لولا أب ولا جد ولا وصي منهما قوله : ( وثمرة الخلاف الخ قال الرملي : يفهم منه أنه لا يشترط على قوله في المجلس بل يصح قبل موته ويفهم منه أيضاً صحته على قوله ولو بعد موت الكفيل والمكفول عنه تأمل.
The Book of Kafalah. The third section on explaining its pillar: they said it is offer and acceptance with the following wordings. Abu Yusuf in his later statement did not make acceptance a pillar, so he made it complete by the guarantor alone in property and person. There is disagreement on his statement: some say it is suspended on the approval of the claimant, and some say it is effective and the claimant has the right to reject. The practical consequence of the disagreement is when the guaranteed-for dies before acceptance: those who say it is suspended say the guarantor is not held liable. His statement: (The third section on explaining its pillar: they said it is offer and acceptance). Al-Ramli said: I say: meaning according to them (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), and it is the first statement of Abu Yusuf, such that it is not completed by the guarantor alone unless there is acceptance by the guaranteed-for or by a third party on his behalf in the session of contract, or address to the guaranteed-for or to a third party, like if the claimant says 'Guarantee for me the person of so-and-so' and he says 'I have guaranteed', or if a third person says to another 'Guarantee the person of so-and-so' or 'so-and-so for so-and-so' and that other says 'I have guaranteed', the guarantee is valid, and it is suspended on the approval of the guaranteed-for after the session, and the guarantor may withdraw before the absent one approves his guarantee. But if none of this occurs and the guarantor says 'I have guaranteed the person of so-and-so for so-and-so' or 'what so-and-so owes to so-and-so', then it is not suspended after the session, and even if the claimant comes to know and accepts, it is not valid. (TatarKhaniya). And in Jami' al-Fusulain, the guarantee for a minor is not valid. It was said to him: it is a restriction on harm not benefits, as evidenced by accepting gifts and charity, and in this there is benefit so it is permissible. He said: gift and charity are valid by action and his action is considered, but here speech is necessary and his speech is not considered. (Quoted in al-Kafala). And it is mentioned in al-Ahkam that if the minor is a merchant, the guarantee is valid; if a third party addresses on his behalf and accepts on his behalf, it is suspended on the approval of his agent; if no third party nor his guardian addresses, but the minor himself addresses, then according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad (may Allah have mercy on them) it is not valid, according to Abu Yusuf it is valid. The guardian is the father or grandfather in his absence, or the executor from one of them, or the judge if there is no father, grandfather, or executor from them. His statement: (and the practical consequence of the disagreement etc.). Al-Ramli said: It is understood from this that according to his (Abu Yusuf's) statement, it is not conditional that it be in the session, but it is valid before his death, and it is also understood that according to his statement it is valid even after the death of the guarantor and the guaranteed-for. Consider.
Source pages p. 344

Commentary
This passage discusses the pillar (rukn) of kafalah (suretyship/guarantee). The Hanafi scholars state that the rukn is offer (ijab) and acceptance (qabul) using specific wordings. However, Imam Abu Yusuf in his later opinion held that acceptance is not a pillar; thus the contract is concluded by the guarantor alone in both financial and personal guarantees. There are two interpretations of Abu Yusuf's view: (1) the contract is suspended until the claimant approves, and (2) it is immediately effective but the claimant can reject. The practical consequence (thamar) appears if the guaranteed-for (makful lahu) dies before acceptance: under the suspension view, the guarantor is not liable. The commentary by al-Ramli clarifies the first opinion of Abu Yusuf: without acceptance, the guarantee is not complete. He details scenarios where acceptance occurs in the session or later, and when it is not required. The discussion also touches upon the validity of a minor's guarantee: a minor's guarantee is invalid except when his guardian (wali) takes a loan for his maintenance and instructs him to guarantee that debt. The minor's guarantee for his own person is invalid because it is a gratuitous act. Additionally, if the minor is a merchant, his guarantee is valid according to some views. The text also notes that a slave's guarantee requires the master's permission and is not binding until the slave is freed, unlike a minor's guarantee which is void ab initio. The passage then returns to the thamar: from al-Ramli's comment we infer that according to Abu Yusuf's view, acceptance can occur after the session and even after the death of the parties.

Key Terms
الكفالة (al-kafalah): A contract of guarantee or suretyship where a person (kafil) assumes responsibility for another's debt or person.
الركن (al-rukn): The essential element of a contract without which the contract is invalid; for kafalah it is offer and acceptance or the guarantor's undertaking alone according to the ikhtilaf.
الإيجاب والقبول (al-ijab wa al-qabul): Offer and acceptance; the verbal expressions that conclude the contract.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, what is the thamarah al-khilaf when the guaranteed-for dies before acceptance?
A) The guarantor is always liable regardless of the view.
B) Under the suspension view, the guarantor is not liable; under the immediate-effect view, the guarantor may be liable.
C) The contract becomes void automatically.
D) The guarantor's heirs become liable.

Key Takeaway
The rukn of kafalah is offer and acceptance per the majority, but Abu Yusuf's later opinion that acceptance is unnecessary creates a practical difference in liability when the claimant dies before accepting.

Kafalah — Surety
intermediate
