Conditions of Kafalah: Guarantor, Principal, Claimant, and Guaranteed Item
Detailed enumeration of conditions for kafalah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions of validity and enforceability for each party in a kafalah contract and identify which conditions are essential for the contract's formation.

Core Idea
Kafalah conditions split into shart al-in'iqad and shart al-nafadh — missing the first voids the contract; missing the second suspends enforceability.

Kafalah (surety) is a contract with precise requirements. This passage from al-Bada'i' systematically lists the conditions for the guarantor, principal debtor, creditor, and the guaranteed item. Understanding which conditions affect validity (in'iqad) versus enforceability (nafadh) is critical for determining whether a guarantee is binding.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four parties
The text divides conditions into four categories: kafil (guarantor), asil (principal debtor), matlub lahu (creditor/claimant), and makful bihi (guaranteed item). For each, note the specific requirements.
This is a taxonomy exercise — memorise the four categories.
2
Distinguish shart al-in'iqad vs shart al-nafadh
For each condition, determine whether it is a condition for the contract to come into existence (shart al-in'iqad) or for it to be enforceable (shart al-nafadh). Example: sanity of the guarantor is a shart al-in'iqad; freedom is a shart al-nafadh.
Missing a shart al-in'iqad makes the contract void; missing a shart al-nafadh makes it valid but suspended.
3
List conditions for the guarantor
The guarantor must be sane and adult (shart al-in'iqad). Freedom is a shart al-nafadh. Note exceptions: a minor can validly guarantee if the guardian takes a loan for the orphan's maintenance and instructs the minor. A slave's guarantee is not enforced but becomes binding after emancipation.
Memorise the exception for the orphan's guardian.
4
List conditions for the principal and claimant
The principal must be capable of delivering the guaranteed item and must be known. The claimant must be known and present at the contract session (shart al-in'iqad); sanity is required for acceptance, but freedom is not.
Note that the principal need not be free, adult, or sane.
5
List conditions for the guaranteed item
The item must be a liability on the principal (debt, specific item, person, or action), deliverable by the guarantor (excluding hadd and qisas), and if a debt, it must be binding (lazim). Knowledge of the exact amount is not required.
The condition of being binding is specific to kafalah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابع في شرائطها وهي أربعة أنواع في الكفيل والأصيل والطالب والمكفول به . ثم منها ما هو شرط الانعقاد ومنها ما هو شرط النفاذ، أما شرائط الكفيل فالعقل والبلوغ وهما شرطان للانعقاد فلا ينعقد كفالة مجنون وصبي إلا إذا استدان الولي ديناً في نفقة اليتيم وأمره بأن يضمن المال عنه فإنه صحيح، ولو أمره بكفالة نفسه عنه لم يجز لأن ضمان الدين قد لزمه من غير شرط فالشرط لا يزيده إلا تأكيداً لم يكن متبرعاً. فأما ضمان النفس وهو تسليم نفس الأب أو الوصي فلم يكن عليه فكان متبرعاً به فلم يجز، والحرية شرط نفاذها فلم ينفذ كفالة العبد ولو مأذوناً له في التجارة ويؤاخذ بها بعد العتق بخلاف الصبي لا يؤاخذ بها بعد البلوغ لعدم انعقادها، فإن أذن المولى لعبده فيها فإن كان مديوناً لم يجز وإلا جازت وبيع فيها إلا إن فداه . ولم تجز كفالة المكاتب عن أجنبي ولو أذن مولاه ويطالب بها بعد كفالة المكاتب والمأذون عن مولاهما، ولا يشترط أن يكون الكفيل صحيحاً كفالة المريض لكن من الثلث لأنها تبرع وأما شرائط الأصيل فالأول أن يكون قادراً على تسليم المكفول به إما بنفسه أو بنائبه فلم تصح الكفالة عن ميت مفلس الثاني أن يكون معلوماً فلو كفل بما على واحد لم تصح ولا يشترط أن يكون حراً بالغ عاقلاً. وأما شرائط المكفول له فالأول أن يكون معلوماً . الثاني وجوده في مجلس العقد وهو شرط الانعقاد وقد تقدم في بيان الركن وتفرع على اشتراط قبوله أنه لا بد من عقله لا حريته . وأما شرائط المكفول به فالأول أن يكون مضموناً على الأصيل ديناً أو عيناً أو نفساً أو فعلاً ولكن يشترط في العين أن تكون مضمونة لنفسها الثاني أن يكون مقدور التسليم من الكفيل فلا تجوز بالحدود والقصاص. الثالث أن يكون الدين لازماً وهو خاص بالكفالة فلا تجوز الكفالة ببدل الكتابة. ولا يشترط أن يكون معلوم القدر ؛ الكل من البدائع مختصراً.
The fourth section on its conditions: they are four types regarding the guarantor, the principal, the claimant, and the guaranteed item. Then among them are conditions for validity (in'iqad) and conditions for enforceability (nafadh). As for the conditions of the guarantor: sanity and puberty; these are conditions for validity, so the guarantee of an insane person or a minor is not valid unless the guardian takes a loan for the orphan's maintenance and orders him to guarantee the money, then it is valid. And if he orders him to guarantee his own person, it is not valid because the guarantee of the debt has become binding on him without a condition, so the condition only strengthens it; he was not making a gratuitous transfer. As for the guarantee of the person (i.e., surrendering the person of the father or guardian), it was not binding on him, so he would be making a gratuitous transfer, so it is not valid. Freedom is a condition for enforceability, so the guarantee of a slave is not enforced even if he is permitted to trade, but he is held liable after emancipation, unlike a minor who is not held liable after puberty because it was never valid. If the master permits his slave to give a guarantee, then if the slave is in debt it is not valid; otherwise it is valid and he may be sold for it unless he is redeemed. The guarantee of a mukatab (slave under contract of manumission) for a third party is not valid even with his master's permission, though he may be pursued for it after his manumission. The guarantee of a mukatab and a ma'dhun (slave permitted to trade) on behalf of their masters is valid. It is not required that the guarantor be healthy; the guarantee of a sick person is valid but from the one-third (of his estate) because it is a gratuitous transfer. As for the conditions of the principal: first, that he be capable of delivering the guaranteed item, either himself or through his agent; thus a guarantee on behalf of a deceased insolvent is not valid. Second, that he be known; if one guarantees for 'someone who owes a debt', it is not valid. It is not required that he be free, adult, or sane. As for the conditions of the guaranteed-for: first, that he be known. Second, his presence in the session of contract; this is a condition for validity, and it has preceded in the explanation of the pillar, and it branches on the requirement of his acceptance that his sanity is necessary, not his freedom. As for the conditions of the guaranteed item: first, that it be a liability upon the principal, whether debt, specific item, person, or action; but with the specific item, it must be one that is liable in itself. Second, that the guarantor be capable of delivering it; thus it is not valid for hadd penalties and qisas. Third, that the debt be binding (lazim); this is specific to kafalah, so the guarantee of the substitute for kitabah (mukatab's contract) is not valid. It is not required that its amount be known. All taken from al-Bada'i' in summary.
Source pages p. 344

Commentary
This passage systematically lists the conditions (shurut) for a valid and enforceable kafalah contract. The conditions are divided into four categories: those relating to the guarantor (kafil), the principal debtor (asil), the claimant/creditor (matlub lahu), and the guaranteed item (makful bihi). The text distinguishes between conditions for validity (in'iqad) and conditions for enforceability (nafadh). For the guarantor, sanity and puberty are necessary for the contract to be valid. An insane person or a minor cannot validly give a guarantee, except in the specific case where the orphan's guardian has taken a loan for maintenance and instructs the minor to guarantee that debt; this is valid because it is not a gratuitous act but a fulfillment of an already binding obligation. However, if the guardian orders the minor to guarantee his own person, it is invalid because that would be a pure donation. Freedom is a condition for enforceability: a slave's guarantee is not enforced immediately, though the slave becomes liable after emancipation. If the slave is authorized by his master, a guarantee given while not in debt is valid, and the slave may be sold to pay it unless the master ransoms him. A mukatab's guarantee for a stranger is invalid even with permission, but a mukatab or ma'dhun can validly guarantee on behalf of their master. A sick person's guarantee is valid but limited to one-third of his estate (as it is a donation). For the principal debtor, he must be capable of delivering the guaranteed item and must be identifiable. It is not necessary that he be free, sane, or adult. For the guaranteed-for (claimant), he must be known and present at the contract session (a condition for validity). His sanity is required because acceptance is needed, but freedom is not. For the guaranteed item, it must be a liability (debt, specific item, person, or action). For a specific item, it must be one that is itself liable (e.g., a lost item). The item must be deliverable by the guarantor (so hadd and qisas are excluded because they cannot be performed by a substitute). The debt must be binding (lazim), so the guarantee of the kitabah price is invalid. Knowledge of the exact amount is not required.

Key Terms
شرط الانعقاد (shart al-in'iqaad): A condition without which the contract does not come into existence; e.g., sanity and puberty of the guarantor.
شرط النفاذ (shart al-nafadh): A condition for the contract to be enforceable and binding; e.g., freedom of the guarantor.
الأصيل (al-aseel): The principal debtor on whose behalf the guarantee is given.
المكفول به (al-makful bihi): The subject matter of the guarantee; it can be a debt, specific item, person, or action.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the ruling on a guarantee given by a minor when the guardian instructs him to guarantee a debt taken for the orphan's maintenance?
A) It is invalid because the minor lacks capacity
B) It is valid because the debt was already binding and the instruction makes it not a gratuitous transfer
C) It is valid only if the minor is mature
D) It is invalid because it is a gratuitous transfer

Key Takeaway
The conditions for kafalah are divided into conditions for validity (in'iqad) and conditions for enforceability (nafadh) — missing the former voids the contract, while missing the latter suspends it.

Kafalah — Surety
beginner
6 min
Cause and Ruling of Kafalah
Cause and ruling of kafalah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two causes (sabab) that bring kafalah into existence and identify the first ruling (hukm) according to al-Bada'i'.

Core Idea
Kafalah is caused by creditor pressure and guarantor's intervention; its ruling establishes the guarantor's liability.

In this lesson, we examine the underlying reason (sabab) for the existence of a kafalah contract and the legal effect (hukm) that follows. The Hanafi jurists explain that kafalah does not arise from a mere agreement but from a specific context: the creditor's demand and the guarantor's intention to relieve the debtor. Understanding this cause clarifies the purpose of the contract and its first ruling—the establishment of liability (thubut).

Guided Path
1
Identify the sabab (cause) of kafalah
The text states that the cause of existence is 'the creditor putting pressure on the debtor, with the third party intending to repel it from him.' Recognize that the sabab involves two parties: the creditor's action (pressure) and the guarantor's intention (to relieve).
The cause is not a condition but a motivating factor.
2
Distinguish between cause of existence and cause of legislation
The passage gives a second cause: 'the reason for its legislation is to remove this need and harm.' Understand that the sabab wujūd (cause of existence) explains why a specific contract occurs, while sabab sharʿī (cause of legislation) explains why the Law permits it.
The hikmah (wisdom) is to prevent harm.
3
Identify the first hukm (ruling) of kafalah
According to al-Bada'i', kafalah has two rulings; the first is 'thubut' (establishment) of the guarantee. This means the guarantor becomes liable, and the creditor gains the right to demand payment from him.
The second ruling is not given in this text.
4
Reflect on the hikmah (legislative wisdom)
The text states that kafalah is legislated to remove need and harm. Consider how this contract benefits both creditor (security) and debtor (relief from pressure) and the guarantor (reward or self-interest).
This hikmah is a common theme in Hanafi mu'āmalāt.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الخامس في سببها قالوا : سبب وجودها تضييق الطالب على المطلوب مع قصد الخارج دفعه عنه إما تقرباً إلى الله تعالى أو إزالة للأذى عن نفسه إذا كان المطلوب ممن يهمه ما أهمه وسبب شرعيتها رفع هذه الحاجة والضرر الذي ذكرناه السادس في حكمها ففي البدائع لها حكمان: أحدهما ثبوت
The fifth section on its cause: they said the cause of its existence is the creditor putting pressure on the debtor, with the third party intending to repel it from him, either to draw closer to Allah or to remove harm from himself if the debtor is someone whose concerns matter to him. And the reason for its legislation is to remove this need and harm that we mentioned. The sixth section on its ruling: in al-Bada'i' it has two rulings: one is the establishment [of the guarantee]...
Source pages p. 345

Commentary
This passage explains the sabab (cause) and hukm (ruling) of kafalah. The sabab for the existence of kafalah is twofold: from the creditor's side, he pressures the debtor; from the guarantor's side, he intends to relieve the debtor, either as a pious act or to remove harm from himself if he is personally affected. The wisdom (hikmah) behind its legislation is to address this need and prevent harm. This is a rationale for why Islamic law permits such third-party involvement. The ruling (hukm) according to al-Bada'i' includes two aspects, but the text only gives the first: thubut (establishment) of the guarantee, which likely refers to the liability of the guarantor and the creditor's right to claim. The second ruling is not provided due to the cut-off text. This section is concise and summarizes the underlying purpose without delving into detailed fiqh rules.

Key Terms
سبب الكفالة (sabab al-kafalah): The reason that leads to the existence of a kafalah contract: the creditor's demand and the third party's intention to relieve the debtor.
حكم الكفالة (hukm al-kafalah): The legal effects of the kafalah contract; primarily the liability of the guarantor and the rights of the creditor.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, what is one reason the shari'ah legislated kafalah?
A) To profit from the guarantor
B) To remove the need and harm arising from the creditor's pressure
C) To make debts mandatory
D) To provide a way for slaves to become free

Key Takeaway
Kafalah exists when creditor pressure meets a guarantor's intent to relieve, and its first ruling is the establishment of the guarantor's liability; the underlying wisdom is harm removal.

Kafalah — Surety

Kafalah from a Slave, Mukatab, Mazun: Capacity of the Guarantor and Conditions
Ruling on capacity and conditions in kafalah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the rules of kafalah for slaves, minors, mukatabs, mazuns, and sick persons, and identify the conditions of the principal, creditor, and guaranteed object for a valid kafalah.

Core Idea
A slave's kafalah is suspended; a minor's is invalid; a mukatab and mazun can guarantee for their master; conditions include known parties and a binding debt.

Kafalah is a guarantee contract that requires the guarantor to have capacity. This lesson explores the Hanafi rulings for guarantors with limited capacity: slaves, minors, mukatabs, mazuns, and sick persons. It also examines the essential conditions of the principal debtor, the creditor, and the guaranteed object.

Guided Path
1
Identify the guarantor type
Determine whether the guarantor is a slave, minor, mukatab, mazun, or sick person. Each has a different ruling based on their capacity.
2
Apply the rule for that type
For slave: kafalah is not executed until manumission. For minor: invalid. For mukatab: cannot guarantee for a stranger but can for his master. For mazun: can guarantee for his master. For sick: valid but from one-third.
Pay attention to exceptions like when the slave is in debt.
3
Analyze conditions of the principal (asil)
The principal must be capable of delivering the guaranteed object and must be known. Kafalah for a dead insolvent or unknown person is invalid.
4
Analyze conditions of the creditor and object
The creditor must be known and present at the contract. The guaranteed object must be a liability, binding, and deliverable.
Hadd penalties and qisas are not deliverable.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
عتقه، فتصح وتصح فلم ينفذ كفالة العبد ولو مأذوناً له في التجارة ويؤاخذ بها بعد العتق بخلاف الصبي لا يؤاخذ بها بعد البلوغ لعدم انعقادها، فإن أذن المولى لعبده فيها فإن كان مديوناً لم يجز وإلا جازت وبيع فيها إلا إن فداه . ولم تجز كفالة المكاتب عن أجنبي ولو أذن مولاه ويطالب بها بعد كفالة المكاتب والمأذون عن مولاهما، ولا يشترط أن يكون الكفيل صحيحاً كفالة المريض لكن من الثلث لأنها تبرع وأما شرائط الأصيل فالأول أن يكون قادراً على تسليم المكفول به إما بنفسه أو بنائبه فلم تصح الكفالة عن ميت مفلس الثاني أن يكون معلوماً فلو كفل بما على واحد لم تصح ولا يشترط أن يكون حراً بالغ عاقلاً. وأما شرائط المكفول له فالأول أن يكون معلوماً . الثاني وجوده في مجلس العقد وهو شرط الانعقاد وقد تقدم في بيان الركن وتفرع على اشتراط قبوله أنه لا بد من عقله لا حريته . وأما شرائط المكفول به فالأول أن يكون مضموناً على الأصيل ديناً أو عيناً أو نفساً أو فعلاً ولكن يشترط في العين أن تكون مضمونة لنفسها الثاني أن يكون مقدور التسليم من الكفيل فلا تجوز بالحدود والقصاص. الثالث أن يكون الدين لازماً وهو خاص بالكفالة فلا تجوز الكفالة ببدل الكتابة. ولا يشترط أن يكون معلوم القدر ؛ الكل من البدائع مختصراً. الخامس في سببها قالوا : سبب وجودها تضييق الطالب على المطلوب مع قصد الخارج دفعه عنه إما تقرباً إلى الله تعالى أو إزالة للأذى عن نفسه إذا كان المطلوب ممن يهمه ما أهمه وسبب شرعيتها رفع هذه الحاجة والضرر الذي ذكرناه السادس في حكمها ففي البدائع لها حكمان: أحدهما ثبوت
كذلك
:
كان لهما الرجوع بذلك في مال الصبي فكان أداء الدين على الصبي إلا أن الوصي ينوب عنه في الأداء فإذا أمر بالضمان فقد أذن له بالأداء وهو يملك الأداء بإذنه فيجب عليه الأداء فلم يكن هذا الضمان تبرعاً اهـ. وهو صريح في أن الصبي يطالب بهذا المال بموجب الكفالة ولولاها لكان الطلب إنما هو على الولي، وبهذا التقرير بطل قول عصري هذا الاستثناء مستدرك بل لا تصح كفالة الصبي مطلقاً فتدبره اهـ . قلت : ومثل ما نقله عن المحيط مذكور في الولوالجية. قوله: (وتصح كفالة المكاتب والمأذون عن مولاها قال في النهر وينبغي أن يقيد ذلك بما إذا كانت بأمره، ثم رأيته في عقد الفرائد معزياً إلى المبسوط حيث قال : وكفالة العبد التاجر عن سيده بمال أو بنفسه بغير إذنه باطل
His manumission: so it is valid. The kafalah of a slave is not executed even if he is permitted to trade, and he is pursued after manumission. Unlike a minor, who is not pursued after puberty because the contract was not concluded. If the master permits his slave for kafalah, then if the slave was in debt it is not permissible, otherwise it is permissible, and he is sold for it unless the master ransoms him. The kafalah of a mukatab for a stranger is not permissible even if his master permits, and he is pursued after kitabah. The kafalah of a mukatab and a mazun for their masters is valid. It is not a condition that the guarantor be healthy; the kafalah of a sick person is valid but from one-third because it is a donation. As for the conditions of the principal (asil): first, he must be capable of delivering the guaranteed object, either himself or through his agent, so kafalah for a dead insolvent is not valid. Second, he must be known, so if he guarantees for an unknown person it is not valid. It is not a condition that he be free, adult, or sane. As for the conditions of the creditor (makful lahu): first, he must be known. Second, his presence at the contracting session is a condition of validity. It has preceded in the explanation of the pillar that it follows from the condition of his acceptance that he must be sane, not free. As for the conditions of the guaranteed object (makful bih): first, it must be a liability on the principal, whether a debt, a specific item, a person, or an act. But for a specific item, it must be liable in itself. Second, it must be capable of being delivered by the guarantor, so it is not valid for hadd penalties and qisas. Third, the debt must be binding, and this is specific to kafalah, so kafalah for the alternative of kitabah is not permissible. It is not a condition that the amount be known. All from al-Bada'i summarised. Fifth: its cause. They said: the cause of its existence is the pressure of the claimant on the sought person with the intention of the third party repelling it from him, either seeking closeness to Allah or removing harm from himself if the sought person is someone who worries about the matter. And the cause of its legislation is to remove this need and harm which we mentioned. Sixth: its ruling. In al-Bada'i it has two rulings: one is the establishment of recourse. Similarly: they both had recourse to the property of the minor, so the payment of the debt was on the minor, except that the guardian acts on his behalf in payment, so if he orders him to guarantee, he has permitted him to pay, and he can pay with his permission, so payment is obligatory upon him, so this guarantee was not a donation. It is explicit that the minor is pursued for this wealth by virtue of the kafalah, and without it the demand would be upon the guardian. By this clarification, the statement of a contemporary that this exception is redundant is invalidated; rather, the kafalah of a minor is absolutely invalid. Reflect on it. I say: similar to what he transmitted from al-Muhit is mentioned in al-Walwalijiyyah. His statement: 'The kafalah of a mukatab and a mazun for their master is valid' – al-Nahr said: it should be restricted to when it is by his order. Then I saw it in 'Aqd al-Fara'id citing from al-Mabsut where he said: The kafalah of a trading slave for his master with wealth or himself without his permission is invalid.
Source pages p. 345

Commentary
This passage is a ruling on the capacity of the guarantor (kafeel) in kafalah. The author discusses the validity of kafalah by a slave, a mukatab, a mazun, a sick person, and a minor. For a slave, even if permitted to trade, his kafalah is not executed until after manumission, when he is pursued. A minor's kafalah is invalid and he is not pursued after puberty. If a master permits his slave to give kafalah, it is only valid if the slave is not in debt; otherwise it is invalid and the slave may be sold. A mukatab cannot give kafalah for a stranger, even with permission, but can for his master. A sick person's kafalah is valid but restricted to one-third of his estate. The passage also includes a long commentary on a minor's case, explaining that a guardian can order payment and the minor can be pursued for the debt. The intended meaning is that the kafeel must have legal capacity; slaves and minors have limited capacity.

Key Terms
كفالة (kafalah): A contract of guarantee where the guarantor (kafeel) assumes liability for a debt, person, or act of the principal (asil).
مكاتب (mukatab): A slave who has contracted with his master to purchase his freedom by paying installments.
مأذون (ma'dhun): A slave permitted by his master to engage in trade.
أصيل (asil): The principal debtor on whose behalf the guarantee is given.
مكفول له (makful lahu): The creditor who is the beneficiary of the guarantee.
مكفول به (makful bih): The guaranteed object, which can be a debt, specific item, person, or act.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, what is the ruling of a slave's kafalah if he is not in debt and his master permits?
A) It is valid immediately.
B) It is valid but not executed until manumission.
C) It is invalid altogether.
D) It is valid only if the slave is not a minor.

Key Takeaway
The capacity of the guarantor determines the validity of kafalah: slaves' kafalah is suspended, minors' is invalid, and mukatabs and mazuns can guarantee only for their masters.

Condition of the Principal (Asil) Being Known
Commentary on the condition that the principal (asil) must be known to the guarantor

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the condition of the principal (asil) being known in kafalah and apply the ruling that knowledge by face suffices even without knowing the name.

Core Idea
In kafalah, the guarantor must know the principal – but knowledge of the face suffices; a name is not required.

This lesson examines a key condition in the contract of guarantee (kafalah): the principal (asil) must be known to the guarantor. The Hanafi school discusses what constitutes sufficient knowledge. The passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq clarifies that identifying the principal by face is enough, even if the name is unknown. This ruling is derived from a witness testimony scenario that shows naming is not a condition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the condition
Locate the second condition for kafalah: that the principal (asil) be known (ma'luman). The passage discusses this condition.
2
Understand 'known' here
Determine what constitutes knowledge. The passage indicates that knowing the face suffices; exact name or lineage is not required.
The condition is actual identification, not formal naming.
3
Analyze the testimony example
Consider the case where a witness testifies about a guarantee for a man known by face but not by name. The passage states this is permissible (ja'iz) and uses it as evidence.
This example directly supports the ruling.
4
Derive the ruling
Conclude that the kafalah is valid if the guarantor can identify the principal by any means that removes ignorance (jahalah), such as face recognition.
The condition is fulfilled by actual knowledge, not paperwork.
5
Reflect on the principle
Note the underlying principle: the condition is being known (ma'luman), not being named. The ruling is based on removing uncertainty about the principal's identity.
This reflects the Hanafi emphasis on substantive knowledge over formalism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (الثاني أن يكون معلوماً قال الرملي : وأما المكفول عنه فسيأتي قريباً في عن التتارخانية أنهما لو شهدا أنه كفل لهذا الرجل بنفس رجل نعرفه بوجهه ولكن لا نعرفه باسمه فهو جائز، وإن قال اكفل بنفس رجل لا نعرفه لا بوجهه ولا باسمه فالشهادة جائزة، وأن هذه المسألة دليل على أنه لا تشترط تسمية المكفول عنه وذكر نسبة تأمل.
His statement: 'Second, that he be known.' Al-Ramli said: As for the guaranteed person (makful 'anhu), it will come shortly in al-Tatar Khaniyyah that if they [two witnesses] bear witness that he guaranteed for this man by the person of a man we know by his face but we do not know his name, it is permissible. And if he says, 'I guarantee for a man we do not know by face or name', then the testimony is permissible. And this case is evidence that it is not a condition to mention the name of the guaranteed person and mention of lineage is not required. Reflect.
Source pages p. 345

Commentary
This lesson is a commentary on the condition of the principal (asil) being known. The commentator al-Ramli cites al-Tatar Khaniyyah that the guarantor must know the person he is guaranteeing, but exact knowledge of the name is not necessary; it is sufficient if he can identify him by face. This ruling is based on the principle that the condition is being known, not being named. The example given is of testimony in court; if a man guarantees for a person known by face but not by name, it is valid. Therefore, the condition is fulfilled with any means of identification that removes ignorance.

Key Terms
مكفول عنه (makful 'anhu): The principal (asil) on whose behalf the guarantee is given; also called 'الأصيل'.
كفالة (kafalah): Guarantee or surety contract.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) No, because the name must be mentioned
B) Yes, it is permissible
C) It is invalid unless the guarantor knows the name
D) It depends on the amount of the debt

Key Takeaway
Knowledge of the principal by face suffices for a valid kafalah; naming is not required.

Six Aspects of Kafalah: Cause, Ruling, Nature, Merits, Types, Evidence
Systematic exposition of six aspects of kafalah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the six aspects of kafalah (cause, ruling, nature, merits, types, evidence) according to the Hanafi school and explain each with reference to the text.

Core Idea
Kafalah is a binding guarantee: its cause is relieving pressure, its evidence is Quran and hadith, and it brings both reward and risk.

This lesson examines kafalah (surety/guarantee) from six perspectives: its cause, ruling, nature, merits, types, and evidence. The Hanafi school provides a structured analysis, distinguishing between guarantor's liability for debt, person, or item. Understanding these aspects helps you grasp the legal framework of a common financial and personal guarantee.

Guided Path
1
Identify the cause (sabab) of kafalah
The cause is the pressure of the claimant on the debtor combined with the guarantor's intention to relieve that pressure, either for proximity to Allah or to remove harm from himself.
The sabab is conceptual, not contractual.
2
Examine the ruling (hukm) on liability
Understand how the guarantor is pursued: for a debt, the full amount if sole, half if two; for a person, he must produce him; for an item, deliver the specific item or its substitute; for an act, all together. Stipulating an option of more than three days is invalid in certain contexts.
The ruling varies by type of obligation.
3
Analyze the nature (sifa) and assess merits and demerits
Note that kafalah is initially a non-binding contract (ja'iz) but becomes binding (lazim) upon acceptance. Its merits: relieves hardship of claimant and debtor, considered a high act blessed by Allah. Demerits: potential liability and regret, as stated in the Torah.
The nature affects revocability.
4
Review types (anwa') and evidence (dalil)
Two types: kafalah for a person (kafalah bil-nafs) and kafalah for wealth (kafalah bil-mal). Evidence includes Quran 12:72 and the hadith 'al-za'im gharim' narrated by Abu Dawud and al-Tirmidhi.
The evidence establishes the legal basis.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الخامس في سببها قالوا : سبب وجودها تضييق الطالب على المطلوب مع قصد الخارج دفعه عنه إما تقرباً إلى الله تعالى أو إزالة للأذى عن نفسه إذا كان المطلوب ممن يهمه ما أهمه وسبب شرعيتها رفع هذه الحاجة والضرر الذي ذكرناه السادس في حكمها ففي البدائع لها حكمان: أحدهما ثبوت مطالبة الكفيل بما على الأصيل فإن كان عليه دين طولب بكله الكفيل أن كان واحداً، وإن كانا اثنين طولب كل واحد بنصفه وفي الكفالة بالنفس يطالب بإحضاره إن أمكن كما سيأتي . والكفيل بالعين يطالب بتسليمها حال قيامها وببدلها حال هلاكها وبالتسليم يطالب لها وبالفعل جميعاً، وقدمنا أنه اشتراط الخيار فيها أكثر من ثلاثة فيما يصح تعليقه وما لا يصح قبيل الصرف السابع في صفتها فهي عقد جائز به ،لازم وسيأتي أن له الرجوع عنها في . مسألة بايع فلاناً فما بايعته فهو علي الثامن في محاسنها ومساويها، فمحاسنها جليلة وهي تفريج كرب الطالب الخائف على ماله والمطلوب الخائف على نفسه فقد كفاهما مؤنة ما أهمهما وهو نعمة كبيرة عليهما، ولذا كانت من الأفعال العالية حتى امتن الله بها حيث قال وكفلها زکریا [آل عمران : ٣٧] في قراءة التشديد المتضمن للامتنان على مريم إذ جعل لها من يقوم بمصالحها ويقوم بها. ومساويها كما في المجتبى قال : الامتناع على التكفل أقرب إلى الاحتياط لأنه مكتوب في التوراة والزعامة أولها ملامة وأوسطها ندامة وآخرها غرامة» اهـ. التاسع في أنواعها سيأتي أنها نوعان : كفالة بالنفس وكفالة بالمال العاشر في دليلها قوله تعالى ولمن جاء به حمل بعير وأنابة زعيم [يوسف: ۷۲] وقوله صلى الله عليه وسلم «الزعيم غارم»(۱) رواه أبو داود والترمذي. وفي الدر المنثور : الزعيم الكفيل وغارم من الغرم وهو أداء شيء لازم اهـ.
Fifth: its cause. They said: The cause of its existence is the pressure of the claimant on the sought person with the intention of the third party repelling it from him, either seeking closeness to Allah or removing harm from himself if the sought person is someone who worries about the matter. And the cause of its legislation is to remove this need and harm which we mentioned. Sixth: its ruling. In al-Bada'i it has two rulings: one is the establishment of pursuit of the guarantor for what is on the principal. If the principal has a debt, the guarantor is pursued for all of it if he is one, and if there are two, each is pursued for half. In kafalah for a person, the guarantor is pursued to bring him if possible, as will come. The guarantor for a specific item is pursued to deliver it while it exists, and its substitute when it perishes, and by delivery he is pursued for it, and for an act all together. We have mentioned that stipulating an option of more than three days is invalid in what can be suspended and what cannot, similar to currency exchange. Seventh: its nature. It is a non-binding contract that becomes binding, and it will come that he can revoke it in the case of 'Sell to so-and-so, and if you do not sell, then it is on me.' Eighth: its merits and demerits. Its merits are great: it removes the distress of the claimant who fears for his wealth and the sought person who fears for himself, thus it spares both the trouble of what concerns them, and it is a great blessing upon them. Therefore it is among the high acts, even Allah has favored with it where He said: 'And Kafalah Zakariyya' [Al Imran: 37] in the reading of tashdid, which implies favor upon Maryam as He made for her someone to take care of her affairs and stand by her. Its demerits as in al-Mujtaba: Refraining from becoming a guarantor is closer to caution because it is written in the Torah: 'Guarantee: its beginning is blame, its middle is regret, and its end is liability.' Ninth: its types. It will come that they are two: kafalah for a person and kafalah for wealth. Tenth: its evidence. The Almighty's saying: 'And for whoever brings it, a camel's load, and I am its guarantor' [Yusuf: 72] and the Prophet's saying: 'The guarantor (za'im) is liable' (reported by Abu Dawud and al-Tirmidhi). In al-Durr al-Manthoor: 'za'im is the kafil, and gharim is from ghurm which is payment of something binding.'
Source pages p. 345

Commentary
This passage provides a comprehensive legal analysis of kafalah covering six aspects. The cause (sabab) is the pressure on the debtor and the desire of the guarantor to relieve him. The ruling (hukm) explains the liability of the guarantor: for debts, full amount if sole, half if two; for a person, he must produce him; for items, deliver the item or its substitute; for acts, all are required. The nature (sifa) is that it is initially a non-binding contract (ja'iz) that becomes binding (lazim) upon acceptance. The merits (mahasin) include relieving hardship, exemplified by the Quranic story of Zakariyya sponsoring Maryam. The demerits (masawi) include potential liability and regret. The types (anwa') are two: kafalah bil-nafs (for the person) and kafalah bil-mal (for wealth). The evidence (dalil) includes Quran 12:72 and the hadith 'al-za'im gharim' (the guarantor is liable).

Key Terms
زعيم (za'im): A synonym for guarantor (kafil); one who undertakes liability.
كفالة (kafalah): Surety or guarantee; a contract where one person (kafil) undertakes liability for another's obligation.
غارم (gharim): One who is liable to pay; a debtor or guarantor who must fulfill an obligation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, what is the evidence for the permissibility of kafalah?
A) The hadith 'al-za'im gharim' and the Quranic verse 'And I am its guarantor'
B) Consensus of the Companions
C) Analogy to sales
D) The hadith 'Whoever guarantees a man, he is responsible'

Key Takeaway
Kafalah is a binding guarantee permitted by Quran and hadith, with specific liability rules for debts, persons, and items.

Condition of the Guaranteed Object: Must Be a Binding Liability
Commentary on conditions for the guaranteed object and invalidity of kafalah for badal al-kitabah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the condition that the guaranteed object must be a binding liability on the principal and identify when kafalah is invalid due to the object being a weak debt.

Core Idea
Kafalah is only valid for binding debts; weak debts like badal al-kitabah cannot be guaranteed.

This lesson examines a key condition for the object of a guarantee (makful bih) in Hanafi law. The object must be a binding liability on the principal. The commentator uses this to explain why kafalah for the installment payments of a mukatab slave (badal al-kitabah) is invalid, as it is a weak debt. Understanding this condition prevents invalid guarantees.

Guided Path
1
Identify the condition for the object
The text states the first condition: 'that it be a liability on the principal.' This means the debt must be binding and enforceable against the original debtor.
The condition is derived from the principle that a guarantee transfers liability, so the original must be liable.
2
Analyze the proof via analogy
The text uses qiyas (analogy) with maintenance for a wife before court order: just as that is not a binding debt, badal al-kitabah is a weak debt (dayn da'if) because the slave can cancel the contract.
The 'illa (effective cause) is that the obligation is not fully binding.
3
Apply the condition to the fatwa case
The fatwa is clear: kafalah for badal al-kitabah is invalid because it fails the condition of being a binding liability. This also applies to other non-binding obligations like pre-judgment maintenance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (فالأول أن يكون مضموناً على الأصيل) قال الرملي : يعلم بذلك جواب واقعة الفتوى وهي الكفالة بالمسلم فيه في وهو عدم صحتها لأن المكفول به غير مضمون على الأصيل، وسيأتي أن الكفالة بالمال شرطها أن يكون الدين صحيحاً وسيأتي في شرح قوله وصح لو ثمناً أنه لو كفل بالثمن ثم ظهر فساد البيع يرجع الكفيل بما دفعه وكيف صح به وهو لا يطالب به الأصيل فأنى يطالب به الكفيل؟ تأمل. قوله : (فلا تجوز ببدل (الكتابة قال في النهر : وينبغي أن يكون ذلك الكفالة بنفقة الزوجة قبل القضاء بها أو المضي لما قدمناه . من أنها لا تصير ديناً إلا بها وبدل الكتابة دين إلا أنه ضعيف، ولا الكفالة به فما ليس ديناً أولى وقد أفتيت به
His statement: 'First, that it be a liability on the principal.' Al-Ramli said: By that, the answer to the fatwa case is known, which is the kafalah for a Muslim transaction? in that it is invalid because the guaranteed object is not a liability on the principal. It will come that the kafalah for wealth has the condition that the debt be valid, and it will come in the explanation of his statement 'and it is valid even if it is a price' that if he guarantees the price and then the defective sale appears, the guarantor returns what he paid, and how can it be valid if he is not pursued by the principal? Then how can the guarantor be pursued? Reflect. His statement: 'It is not permissible for the alternative of kitabah.' Al-Nahr said: That should be similar to the kafalah for the maintenance of a wife before a judgment or passage of time as we have presented, because it does not become a debt except by that, and the alternative of kitabah is a debt but it is weak, so kafalah for it is not permissible, and that which is not a debt is more worthy. And I have given fatwa accordingly.
Source pages p. 346

Commentary
This lesson covers the first condition of the guaranteed object: it must be a liability (mamun) on the principal. The commentator al-Ramli uses this to answer a fatwa about kafalah for a Muslim transaction (probably salam) which is invalid because the object is not a liability on the principal. He also discusses that if the kafalah is for a price (thaman) in a sale that turns out to be void, the guarantor can recover what he paid, but the validity of the original kafalah is questionable. The second part discusses the condition that the debt must be binding (lazim). Kafalah for the alternative of kitabah (badal al-kitabah) is not permissible because it is a weak debt, and similarly kafalah for maintenance before judgment is not valid.

Key Terms
بدل الكتابة (badal al-kitabah): The financial compensation paid by a mukatab slave for his freedom in installments.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, is kafalah for badal al-kitabah permissible?
A) Yes, because it is a debt
B) No, because it is a weak debt
C) Yes, with the permission of the slave
D) No, because it is a donation

Key Takeaway
Kafalah is only valid for debts that are binding and enforceable; weak debts like badal al-kitabah cannot be guaranteed.

The Four Names in Kafalah: Parties and Their Arabic Terms
Definition of the four parties in kafalah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and state the four parties in a kafalah contract and their correct Arabic terms, including the special case for kafalah bi-l-nafs.

Core Idea
The four parties in kafalah have precise Arabic terms: makful 'anhu, makful lahu, kafil, and makful bih.

This lesson clarifies the essential terminology used in contracts of guarantee (kafalah). Without this precision, legal documents become ambiguous and enforceable rights may be unclear. The passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq establishes the correct nomenclature and a critical distinction when the guarantee involves a person rather than a debt.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Four Terms
List the four Arabic names mentioned in the passage: al-makful 'anhu, al-makful lahu, al-kafil, and al-makful bih.
2
Match Each Term to Its Role
Assign each term to its role: makful 'anhu = debtor (principal), makful lahu = creditor (beneficiary), kafil = guarantor (obligor), makful bih = the object of guarantee (debt or person).
For debts, makful bih is the debt itself.
3
Note the Special Case for Persons
When the guarantee is for a person's appearance (kafalah bi-l-nafs), the person is called makful bih, not makful 'anhu. This is a critical distinction.
The term makful 'anhu is reserved solely for the debtor.
4
Apply the Terminology to Avoid Error
In any kafalah contract, correctly label each party using these terms. A common mistake is using 'makful 'anhu' for the guaranteed person; the passage corrects this.
Precision prevents confusion in legal documents.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويحتاج إلى معرفة أسامي أربعة : المكفول عنه وهو المديون، والمكفول له وهو الدائن، والكفيل وهو الملتزم، والمكفول به وهو الدين ويقال للمكفول بنفسه مكفول به ولا يقال مكفول عنه ؛ كذا في التتارخانية.
And one needs to know four names: the one guaranteed for (makful 'anhu) who is the debtor, the one guaranteed to (makful lahu) who is the creditor, the guarantor (kafil) who is the obligor (multazim), and the guaranteed object (makful bih) which is the debt. And the one guaranteed for his person is called 'makful bih' (guaranteed object) and not 'makful 'anhu'. This is from al-Tatar Khaniyyah.
Source pages p. 346

Commentary
This is a brief terminological lesson defining the four essential parties in a kafalah contract: (1) المكفول عنه (makful 'anhu) = the debtor/principal (asil); (2) المكفول له (makful lahu) = the creditor/beneficiary; (3) الكفيل (kafil) = the guarantor/obligor; (4) المكفول به (makful bih) = the debt/obligation. Importantly, when the guarantee is for a person (kafalah bi-l-nafs), the person is referred to as 'makful bih' (the object of guarantee), not 'makful 'anhu'. This precision is crucial in legal documents and court proceedings.

Key Terms
مكفول عنه (makful 'anhu): The debtor or principal on whose behalf the guarantee is made.
مكفول له (makful lahu): The creditor or beneficiary to whom the guarantee is made.
كفيل (kafil): The guarantor who undertakes the obligation.
مكفول به (makful bih): The object of guarantee; for debts it is the debt, for persons it is the person himself.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the term for the person whose appearance is guaranteed in a kafalah bi-l-nafs?
A) Makful 'anhu
B) Makful lahu
C) Makful bih
D) Kafil

Key Takeaway
Precision in naming the parties in kafalah is crucial: makful 'anhu is the debtor, and makful bih is the object, which in kafalah bi-l-nafs becomes the person himself.

Multiple Guarantors for the Same Person: Validity and Evidence
Ruling with proof: establishing validity of multiple sureties

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on multiple kafalah bi-l-nafs and explain the evidence from the hadith 'al-za'im gharim'.

Core Idea
Multiple guarantors for the same person are valid just like multiple debts, due to the general hadith.

This lesson tackles the question of whether one person can be guaranteed by multiple sureties, or whether one surety can guarantee multiple persons. The Hanafi school affirms validity on the basis of the general command of the Prophet ﷺ. We will analyse the proof and address a common objection.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
What exactly is being ruled upon? The act is kafalah bi-l-nafs (guarantee of the person) being done multiple times—either multiple guarantors for one person or one guarantor for multiple persons. The ruling is that it is valid (sahih).
The text explicitly says 'and it is valid for the self even if repeated'.
2
Locate the dalil
What proof does the author cite? The hadith 'al-za'im gharim' (the guarantor is liable). This is a general statement from the Prophet ﷺ.
The hadith is narrated by Abu Dawud and Tirmidhi, and is considered a hadith ahad with zanni thubut and dalala.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
How does this hadith prove the ruling? The word 'za'im' is general and includes both personal and financial guarantees. The term 'gharim' implies liability, which is understood as incurring harm—applicable to personal guarantees as well.
The Hanafi principle: al-amm yufidu al-wujub or at least al-amm yuthbitu al-hukm. Here it establishes validity.
4
Address the objection
An objector might say that there is no financial liability (ghurm) in personal guarantees. The response: 'ghurm' means incurring harm in a broad sense, not limited to financial loss. The guarantor incurs the responsibility to produce the person, which is a form of harm.
The text also mentions a relevant Quranic verse fragment (though possibly misplaced) to support the broader meaning of punishment/harm.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وتصح بالنفس وإن تعددت أي الكفالة بأن أخذ منه كفيلاً ثم كفيلاً ثم آخر وجاز رجوع الضمير إلى النفس بأن كفل واحد نفوساً كما يجوز بالديون الكثيرة لإطلاق قوله عليه الصلاة والسلام الزعيم غارم من غير فصل بين الكفالة بالمال والكفالة بالنفس. ولا يقال لا غرم في كفالة النفس لأنا نقول : الغرم لزوم ضرر عليه ومنه قوله تعالى أن عذابها من فساد البيع يرجع الكفيل بما دفعه وكيف صح به وهو لا يطالب به الأصيل فأنى يطالب به الكفيل؟ تأمل.
His statement: 'And it is valid for the self even if repeated' i.e., the kafalah by taking from him a guarantor, then a second, then another, and it is permissible; the pronoun refers to 'self' meaning that one person can guarantee multiple individuals, just as it is permissible for multiple debts, due to the general application of his saying: 'The guarantor is liable' without distinction between kafalah for wealth and kafalah for the person. And it is not said that there is no liability in kafalah for the person, for we say: liability is the occurrence of harm upon him, and from it is His saying: '... its punishment' from the corruption of the transaction? The guarantor returns what he paid, and how can it be valid if he is not pursued by the principal? Then how can the guarantor be pursued? Reflect.
Source pages p. 346

Commentary
This lesson comments on the validity of multiple kafalahs for the same person or one person guaranteeing several individuals. The key point is that kafalah bi-l-nafs is treated like kafalah bi-l-mal in terms of permissibility; there is no requirement to limit to one guarantor or one guaranteed person. The hadith 'al-za'im gharim' is general and includes both types. The text also refutes an objection that there is no 'ghurm' (liability) in personal suretyship by arguing that 'ghurm' means incurring harm, which applies here. The fragment about 'an 'adhabaha' and 'fasad al-bay' seems out of place; it may be a scribal error or misplaced marginal note. The intended ruling is that multiple sureties are valid.

Key Terms
كفالة بالنفس (kafalah bi-l-nafs): A guarantee to produce the person of the principal (asil) to the creditor.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Is kafalah bi-l-nafs valid if one person guarantees multiple individuals simultaneously?
A) No, only one person can be guaranteed
B) Yes, it is valid based on the general hadith
C) It is valid only if the guarantor knows all names
D) It is invalid because of uncertainty

Key Takeaway
The generality of the hadith 'al-za'im gharim' establishes that kafalah bi-l-nafs with multiple guarantors or multiple guaranteed persons is valid.

Synonyms for the Guarantor in Hanafi Legal Usage
Enumeration of synonyms with usage notes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the eight Arabic synonyms for a guarantor (kafil) and state the specific domain of each according to al-Mawardi.

Core Idea
Eight synonyms for the guarantor; each has a specific domain of application in Hanafi law.

In Arabic legal terminology, the term 'kafil' (guarantor) has multiple synonyms, each with a nuanced usage. Understanding these synonyms is essential for accurately interpreting classical texts and differentiating between types of guarantees. This lesson presents the synonyms enumerated by al-Ramli and the customary distinctions noted by al-Mawardi.

Guided Path
1
Identify the base term
The passage states that the guarantor (الكفيل) is the obligor (الملتزم). This is the root term from which all synonyms derive.
2
List the eight synonyms
Al-Ramli provides eight synonyms: ضامناً، ضميناً، جميلاً، زعيماً، كافلاً، كفيلاً، صبيراً، قبيلاً. Memorize this list.
Some terms appear similar; note the spelling differences.
3
Learn al-Mawardi's customary distinctions
Al-Mawardi distinguishes three terms: ضمين (damin) for financial matters, حميل (hamil) for blood-money (diyat), and زعيم (za'im) for large sums. The other synonyms may have general or alternative uses.
This custom is not absolute but provides legal precision.
4
Apply the terminology
When reading fiqh texts, identify which synonym is used to infer the type of guarantee involved (financial, diyat, or large sum).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (والكفيل وهو الملتزم) قال الرملي : ويسمى الملتزم لذلك ضامناً وضميناً وجميلاً وزعيماً وكافلاً وكفيلاً وصبيراً وقبيلاً. قال الماوردي: غير أن العرف جار بأن الضمين مستعمل في الأموال والحميل في الديات والزعيم في الأموال العظام، (۱) رواه أبو داود في كتاب البيوع باب ۸۸ الترمذي في كتاب البيوع باب ٣٩. ابن ماجه في كتاب الصدقات باب ۹. أحمد في مسنده (٥/ ٢٦٧، ٢٩٣).
His statement: 'And the guarantor (kafil) is the obligor (multazim).' Al-Ramli said: The obligor is also called damin, damin (again?), jamil, za'im, kafil, kafidh? Actually ضامناً وضميناً وجميلاً وزعيماً وكافلاً وكفيلاً وصبيراً وقبيلاً. Al-Mawardi said: But the custom is that 'damin' is used for financial matters, 'hamil' is used for blood-wits, and 'za'im' is used for large sums. (1) Narrated by Abu Dawud in the Book of Sales, chapter 88, al-Tirmidhi in the Book of Sales, chapter 39, Ibn Majah in the Book of Charity, chapter 9, Ahmad in his Musnad (5/267, 293).
Source pages p. 346

Commentary
This passage defines the legal term 'kaafil' as the obligor (multazim) and provides a list of synonyms: ضامن, ضمين, جميل, زعيم, كافل, كفيل, صبير, قبيل. Each term may have a specific technical usage; for example, according to al-Mawardi, 'damin' is used for financial guarantees, 'hamil' (from the list?) for blood-money (diyat), and 'za'im' for large amounts. The hadith references at the end indicate the chain of narration for the hadith 'al-za'im gharim'.

Key Terms
ضمين (damin): A synonym for guarantor, specifically used in financial contexts.
حميل (hamil): Synonym for guarantor used specifically for blood-money (diyat).
زعيم (za'im): Synonym for guarantor used for large sums of money.
كافل (kafil): The primary term for guarantor, the base word.
كفيل (kafil (variant)): Alternate form of kafil, same meaning.
جميل (jamil): Synonym for guarantor, less common.
صبير (sabir): Synonym for guarantor, rare.
قبيل (qabil): Synonym for guarantor, meaning 'receiver' or 'undertaker'.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to al-Mawardi, which term is used for a guarantor in blood-money (diyat) cases?
A) Damin
B) Za'im
C) Hamil
D) Kafil

Key Takeaway
The eight synonyms for kafil are not arbitrarily interchangeable; each has a customary domain that a Hanafi jurist must recognize.

Kafala Using Expressions Referring to the Body
Ruling on validity of kafala using body part expressions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state which body part expressions are valid for kafala and explain why, applying the Hanafi principle of 'urf and qiyas with talaq.

Core Idea
An expression is valid for kafala if it signifies the whole person, even if a part is mentioned.

In this passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq, the author explains that kafala (suretyship) can be validly concluded using expressions that refer to a part of the body, provided those parts are commonly understood to represent the whole person. This is established by analogy with divorce law and by custom. Understanding which expressions are valid is crucial for practitioners of Hanafi law.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is kafala (suretyship) using expressions that mention a body part. The ruling is that it is valid (sahih) if the part signifies the whole.
2
Locate the dalil
The author cites analogy (qiyas) with divorce (talaq), where such expressions are also valid. Additionally, custom ('urf) is used.
The text says 'we have already mentioned this in divorce.'
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
In talaq, phrases like 'your neck' are valid because they are metaphors for the whole person. By qiyas, the same applies to kafala because both contracts involve a person's responsibility.
The common 'illa is that the expression indicates the whole person.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Imam Muhammad did not rule on 'ayn (eye/self). Al-Balkhi held that kafala and talaq are valid with 'ayn if intent for the whole is clear.
The author notes that 'ayn has become common for the self in later times.
5
Evaluate the tarjih
The mufta bihi position is that any expression commonly understood to refer to the whole person makes kafala valid, including 'ayn in our time.
This is based on reliance on later custom and verification in Fath al-Qadir.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
عن البدن وبجزء شائع وبضمنته وبعلى وإلى وأنا زعيم وقبيل به لا بأنا ضامن لمعرفته وإن شرط كما في ورقبته وعنقه وكل عضو منه يعبر به عن جميع البدن أو بثلثه أو ربعه، وقد قدمناه في الطلاق. وقد ذكروا صحة الكفالة بالروح ولم يذكروه في الطلاق وينبغي الوقوع به، وذكروا في الطلاق الفرج ولم يذكروه هنا وينبغي صحة الكفالة به إذا كانت امرأة؛ كذا في التتارخانية. ولم يذكر محمد رحمه الله تعالى ما إذا كفل بعينه قال البلخي : لا يصح الطلاق إلا أن ينوي به البدن والذي يجب أن تصح الكفالة به كالطلاق إذا تعين مما يعبر به عن الكل يقال عين القوم وهو عين في الناس ولعله لم يكن معروفاً في زمانهم، أما في زماننا فلا شك في ذلك بخلاف ما لو قال بيده أو رجله ويتأتى في دمه ما تقدم في الطلاق؛ كذا في فتح القدير قيدنا بكونه جزء الكفيل عنه لأن الكفيل لو أضاف الجزء إليه بأن قال الكفيل لك نصفي أو ثلثي فإنه لا يجوز ; ذكره في الكرخي في باب الرهن كذا في السراج الوهاج
Concerning the body, and a common part, and with dhimmatihi, and with 'alay, and ilay, and ana za'im, and qabil bihi, but not with 'ana damin li-ma'rifatihi' even if conditional. As in his neck, his nape, and every limb that is used to express the whole body, or a third or a fourth of it, and we have already mentioned this in divorce. They have mentioned the validity of kafala with soul, but they did not mention it in divorce, and it should be valid. They mentioned in divorce the private part, and they did not mention it here, and kafala with it should be valid if it is a woman. Thus in al-Tatar Khaniyya. Muhammad, may Allah have mercy on him, did not mention the case of kafala with his 'ain. Al-Balkhi said: Divorce is not valid unless he intends the body, and that kafala should be valid by it, like divorce if it is specified as an expression meaning the whole. One says 'ayn al-qawm, and he is 'ayn among the people. Perhaps it was not known in their time. As for our time, there is no doubt about it. Unlike if he said 'by his hand or his leg'. And in the case of his blood, what preceded in divorce applies. Thus in Fath al-Qadir. We qualified it as being a part of the guarantor himself, because if the guarantor adds the part to himself by saying 'I guarantee for you my half or my third', it is not permissible. Mentioned in al-Karkhi in the chapter of rahn. Thus in al-Siraj al-Wahhaj.
Source pages p. 348

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof regarding the validity of kafala (suretyship) when the guarantor uses expressions referring to the body or a part thereof. The author, based on earlier discussions in the book of talaq (divorce), explains that kafala is valid if the expression refers to the entire person, even if a part is mentioned, provided that part is commonly used to denote the whole. For example, saying 'I guarantee his neck' or 'his nape' is valid because these terms are metonymically used for the whole person. Similarly, expressions like 'his soul' or 'a third of him' are valid. The passage notes that Imam Muhammad did not explicitly mention the case of 'ayn (eye/self), but later scholars like al-Balkhi argued that it should be valid if it clearly refers to the whole person, especially in later times when 'ayn came to be used for the self. However, if the expression refers to a specific limb not used metonymically (like 'his hand' or 'his leg'), the kafala is not valid. The passage also distinguishes between saying 'I guarantee a part of him' (like half) and saying 'I guarantee for you my half' (i.e., of the guarantor himself); the latter is invalid because it involves uncertainty. The authority is from the Hanafi school, relying on analogy with divorce and on the principle of 'urf (custom).

Key Terms
كفالة (kafala): A contract of suretyship where one person (the guarantor) becomes responsible for the presence or debt of another.
بدن (badan): The body; used metonymically for the whole person in kafala.
رقبة (ra'aba): Neck; a term commonly used to refer to the entire person in contractual contexts.
عين ('ayn): Eye or self; debated whether it can refer to the whole person.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following expressions is valid for kafala in the Hanafi school?
A) 'I guarantee his hand.'
B) 'I guarantee his neck.'
C) 'I guarantee half of him.' (without specifying which half)
D) 'I guarantee my half.' (i.e., of myself)

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi kafala, any expression that customarily signifies the whole person – even a body part like neck or soul – makes the suretyship valid, by analogy with talaq.

Valid Expressions for Kafala: Dhimantu, 'Ala, Ila, Za'im, Qabil
Ruling with proof on valid phrases for kafala

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and apply the five valid expressions for kafala (dhimantu, 'ala, ila, za'im, qabil) and explain their proofs.

Core Idea
Kafala is valid with any word that clearly indicates guarantee, including dhimantu, 'ala, ila, za'im, and qabil.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq expounds the valid verbal formulas for a kafala (suretyship) contract. The Hanafi school, based on Quran, hadith, and linguistic usage, accepts a range of expressions beyond just 'kafala' or 'daman'. We will examine each phrase, its evidentiary basis, and the condition of specification.

Guided Path
1
List the five expressions
Identify each phrase: dhimantu (ضمنت), 'ala (علي), ila (إلي), za'im (زعيم), qabil (قبيل). Note their literal meanings: I guarantee, upon me, to me, guarantor, guarantor.
2
Identify the proof for each
For dhimantu: explicit statement. For 'ala: linguistic obligation. For ila: hadith 'Whoever leaves a burden...to me'. For za'im: Quran 12:72. For qabil: lexical usage and classical texts.
The primary proof is the Quran for za'im.
3
Note the common condition
The person or property being guaranteed must be specified, unless context makes it clear. For example, 'dhimantu' without specifying is not valid; 'ala' with a condition like 'until we meet' is valid because the condition clarifies.
4
Analyze the dispute over za'im
Some scholars (e.g., al-Razi) argue that Quran 12:72 refers to ju'ala (commission) rather than kafala. The Hanafi response is that the word 'za'im' linguistically means kafil, and the context does not negate its use for guarantee.
This is a legitimate ikhtilaf with practical consequences.
5
Conclude the mufta bihi
All five expressions are valid for kafala in the Hanafi school. Conditional phrases like 'ala until we meet' are valid if the condition is clear.
The relied-upon view from al-Bahr al-Raiq.

Al-Fakhr al-Razi mentioned after establishing that it is evidence of kafala, but this is kafala for returning stolen property, which is kafala for something not yet obligatory, but such kafala was valid among them. Al-Qadi mentioned that in this verse there is evidence for the permissibility of ju'ala and guarantee of the reward before completion. In al-Tatar Khaniyya: 'I am (a) qabil for you with the person of so-and-so' is kafila, just as if he said 'ala that I bring him to you.

Commentary
This passage is a series of rulings with proofs on the valid phrases for kafala. It begins with 'dhimmatihi' (often read as 'dhimmatihi' or 'dimmatihi' meaning responsibility). The author clarifies that saying 'dhimantu laka fulanan' (I guarantee you so-and-so) is valid because it explicitly states the guarantee. However, if one says 'ana damin hatta najtami'a' (I am a guarantor until we meet), it is not valid because the object of guarantee is not specified as a person or property. Then the phrase 'ala (upon me) is discussed: 'ala indicates obligation, so saying 'ala' is a valid commitment. Similarly, 'ila (to me) is valid based on the hadith 'Whoever leaves a burden (or orphan), then to me.' The phrase 'ana za'im' (I am a guarantor) is valid because the Quran uses 'za'im' to mean kafil. The passage discusses the verse of Yusuf (12:72) and interprets it as an example of kafala. It mentions that some scholars consider the verse as evidence for ju'ala (commission) rather than kafala. Finally, the phrase 'qabil' is also valid, as it is synonymous with kafil. The authority comes from the Quran, hadith, and classical works of the school.

Key Terms
ضمنت (dhimantu): I guarantee; one of the explicit phrases for kafala.
زعيم (za'im): A guarantor; used in Quran as synonymous with kafil.
قبيل (qabil): A guarantor; derived from qabala (to accept responsibility).

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) 'I am a guarantor until we meet.' (said as 'ana damin')
B) 'Upon me until we meet.' (said as 'ala')
C) 'I am a za'im for you.'
D) 'I guarantee you' without specifying the person.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school validates a wide range of verbal expressions for kafala as long as the guarantee intention is clear and the subject is specified.

Validity of 'Damin li-Ma'rifatihi' and Similar Kafala Expressions
Dispute on kafala wording

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on 'ana damin li-ma'rifatihi', distinguish between valid and invalid kafala expressions, and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute.

Core Idea
Guaranteeing knowledge is not kafala; only commitment to the person or debt creates liability.

In this lesson, we examine a classic intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the validity of the phrase 'ana damin li-ma'rifatihi' (I am a guarantor for his knowledge) in creating a kafala (guarantee). The source passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq clarifies the relied-upon position that such an expression is invalid, contrasts it with other expressions like 'li-ta'rifihi' and 'li-wajhihi', and discusses the rare opinion of Abu Yusuf based on custom.

Guided Path
1
Identify the primary phrase under dispute
The phrase 'ana damin li-ma'rifatihi' is examined; what does it commit the guarantor to? Only knowledge, not the debtor's presence or debt.
Ma'rifa is intransitive.
2
Understand the basis for invalidity
The guarantor has not assumed any actual responsibility (dhimmah) for the person or debt; knowledge is intransitive. This is akin to saying 'I will point him out' which does not guarantee.
The majority view: Abu Hanifa and Muhammad.
3
Contrast with ta'rif
'Ana damin li-ta'rifihi' implies an obligation to make the debtor known, which is transitive and thus may create a valid kafala according to some mashayikh.
Ta'rif is a transitive masdar.
4
Recognize valid expressions: wajh, dhimma, etc.
Saying 'ana damin li-wajhihi' is valid because 'face' represents the whole person. Also expressions like 'dhimantuhu' or 'huwa alayya' carry clear responsibility.
These are unambiguous.
5
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf and tarjih
Abu Yusuf held the phrase valid due to custom (urf), but the fatwa is on the invalidity following Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, as confirmed by later authorities.
Tarjih authority: Fath al-Qadir, al-Khulasa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (لا) بأنا ضامن لمعرفته) أي لا تصح بهذا القول. وقال أبو يوسف : يصير . للعرف لأنهم يريدون به الكفالة. وجه ما في الكتاب أنه التزم معرفته دون المطالبة فصار كالتزامه دلالة عليه أو قال أوقفك عليه . وقال الفقيه أبو الليث : هذا القول عن أبي يوسف غير مشهور والظاهر ما عنهما. وفي خزانة الواقعات : وبه يفتى أي بظاهر الرواية ; كذا في فتح القدير. وفي الخلاصة: وعليه الفتوى . قيد بالمعرفة لأنه لو قال أنا ضامن لتعريفه ففيه اختلاف المشايخ، والوجه اللزوم لأنه مصدر متعد إلى اثنين فقد التزم أن يعرفه الغريم بخلاف معرفته فإنه لا يقتضي إلا معرفة الكفيل للمطلوب ; كذا في فتح القدير. ولو قال أنا ضامن لوجهه فإنه يؤخذ به لأن الوجه يعبر به عن الجملة فكأنه قال أنا ضامن له ; كذا في السراج الوهاج. وأشار إلى أنه لو قال أنا أعرفه لا يكون كفيلاً كما في السراج . وفي الخانية : ولو قال أنا كفيل لمعرفة فلان لا يكون كفيلاً ولو قال معرفة فلان علي قالوا يلزمه أن يدل عليه اهـ وفي التتارخانية : ألفاظ الكفالة كل ما ينبئ عن العهدة في العرف والعادة ثم قال : لو كفل بنفس رجل وسلمه إليه وبرئ، ثم إن الطالب لزم ضامناً بالنفس لا الكفالة بالمال ولم أر أحداً من مشايخنا ذكرها في ألفاظ الكفالة بالمال لكن قال الشيخ أبو نصر : الأقطع عند قول القدوري في الكفالة بالنفس، وكذلك إن قال ضمنته أو هو علي أو إلي أو أنا زعيم به أو قبيل به، فإذا ثبت أن هذه الألفاظ يصح الضمان بها فلا فرق بين ضمان النفس وضمان المال اهـ. وينبغي أن يقال هذه الألفاظ إن أطلقت تحمل على الكفالة بالنفس وإذا كان هناك قرينة على الكفالة بالمال فتتمحض حينئذ للكفالة به .اهـ. قلت : ومفاده أن البيان ليس شرطاً في صحتها وأنها عند عدمه تحمل على الكفالة بالنفس وهو خلاف ما في الخانية ولا يمكن حمله على ما في السراج لوجود البيان بالإضافة فيه، وفرق بين أنا ضامن وبين هو على خلافاً لما في المنح تأمل : قوله : (كانت
His saying: (but not with 'I am a guarantor for his knowledge') meaning it is not valid with this statement. Abu Yusuf said: it becomes, due to custom, because they intend kafala by it. The reasoning in the book is that he committed to his knowledge, not to the claim, so it is like committing to indicate him, or saying 'I will make you stand him.' Al-Faqih Abu al-Layth said: this statement from Abu Yusuf is not well-known, and the apparent view is from both (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad). In Khizanat al-Waqi'at: and by it is given fatwa, i.e., by the apparent narration. Thus in Fath al-Qadir. In al-Khulasa: and upon it is the fatwa. He qualified with 'knowledge' because if he said 'I am a guarantor for his definition', there is disagreement among the mashayikh, and the correct view is obligation, because 'definition' is a transitive infinitive requiring two objects, so he committed to make the creditor know the debtor, unlike 'his knowledge' which only implies the guarantor's knowledge of the sought. Thus in Fath al-Qadir. If he says 'I am a guarantor for his face', he is taken by it because 'face' expresses the whole, so it is as if he said 'I am a guarantor for him.' Thus in al-Siraj al-Wahhaj. He indicated that if he says 'I know him', he is not a kafil, as in al-Siraj. In al-Khaniyya: if he says 'I am a kafil for the knowledge of so-and-so', he is not a kafil. If he says 'knowledge of so-and-so is upon me', they said he is obliged to guide to him. In al-Tatar Khaniyya: the expressions of kafala are everything that indicates responsibility in custom. Then he said: if he guarantees the person of a man and hands him over and is released, then the claimant becomes a guarantor for the person, not for property. I have not seen any of our mashayikh mention them in the expressions of kafala for property. But Sheikh Abu Nasr said: the decisive view according to Quduri is that in kafala for the person, likewise if he said 'dhimantuhu' or 'huwa 'alayya' or 'ila' or 'ana za'im bihi' or 'qabil bihi', then if it is established that these expressions are valid for guarantee, there is no difference between guarantee of person and property. It should be said: these expressions, if absolute, are carried on kafala for the person, and if there is a context for kafala for property, then they are specifically for it. I say: its implication is that bayan is not a condition for validity, and in its absence it is carried on kafala for the person, which contradicts what is in al-Khaniyya and cannot be reconciled with al-Siraj because of the existence of bayan by addition in it. There is a difference between 'ana damin' and 'huwa 'alayya' contrary to what is in al-Munyah. Ponder. His saying: (was)
Source pages p. 348

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute about the validity of the phrase 'ana damin li-ma'rifatihi' (I am a guarantor for his knowledge) for kafala. The relied-upon position (zahir al-riwaya) is that it is invalid because the guarantor only commits to knowing the person, not to producing him or being responsible for his debt. Abu Yusuf argued for validity based on custom ('urf), but his view is not well-known and the fatwa is on the opposite. The author then distinguishes between 'ma'rifah' (knowledge) and 'ta'rif' (definition): 'ana damin li-ta'rifihi' (for his definition) might be valid because it implies an obligation to make the creditor know the debtor. Saying 'ana damin li-wajhihi' (for his face) is valid because 'face' signifies the whole person. Saying 'ana a'rifuhu' (I know him) is not kafala. Saying 'ma'rifatu fulan 'alayya' (knowledge of so-and-so is upon me) obliges him to guide. The passage also discusses that expressions like 'dhimantu', 'ala', 'ila', 'za'im', 'qabil' apply to kafala for both person and property, though some scholars restrict to person. The final comment notes a difference between 'ana damin' and 'huwa 'alayya'.

Key Terms
معرفة (ma'rifa): Knowledge; in this context, it refers to the guarantor's knowledge of the person, not an obligation to produce him.
تعريف (ta'rif): Definition or making known; implies an obligation to inform the creditor of the debtor's identity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon Hanafi position (mufta bihi), which of the following creates a valid kafala?
A) 'Ana damin li-ma'rifatihi'
B) 'Ana damin li-wajhihi'
C) 'Ana a'rifuhu'
D) 'Ma'rifatu fulan alayya'

Key Takeaway
The phrase 'ana damin li-ma'rifatihi' does not create a valid kafala in the Hanafi school because it only guarantees knowledge, not the person or debt.

Interpretation of the 'Zaim' Verse and the Term 'Qabil'
Commentary on the 'Zaim' verse and definition of 'Qabil'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi interpretation of the 'zaim' verse as a messenger's announcement rather than a suretyship, and define the term 'qabil' as a valid term for kafalah.

Core Idea
The 'zaim' in Surah Yusuf was a messenger announcing a reward, not a surety; but 'qabil' is a proper term for a surety.

This lesson delves into the linguistic and legal analysis of the terms 'zaim' and 'qabil' as they relate to kafalah. We examine the famous verse from Surah Yusuf (12:72), where the 'zaim' is often mistakenly thought to be a guarantor. The Hanafi school clarifies that the verse illustrates a ju'alah (reward for service), not a kafalah. Then we define 'qabil' with lexical support, showing it is a valid term for a surety, and the phrase 'ana qabilun laka bi-nafsi fulan' constitutes a valid kafalah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the terms
Focus on two key terms from the passage: 'zaim' and 'qabil'. Note that each has distinct legal meanings in different contexts.
2
Analyze the verse interpretation
The passage argues that 'zaim' in Surah Yusuf refers to a messenger announcing a reward, not a surety. Trace the reasoning: the speaker is a messenger, so rulings of kafalah are not binding. This is supported by Fakhr al-Razi and Al-Qadi.
Consider why the verse is not direct evidence for kafalah.
3
Define 'qabil'
From lexical and Fiqh sources, the passage defines 'qabil' as the surety (kafil). Understand its usage in the phrase 'ana qabilun laka bi-nafsi fulan', which constitutes a valid kafalah.
Note that 'qabil' is also a collective noun for a group, but the context here is suretyship.

Source Text

ولمن جاء به حمل بعير وأنا به زعيم إن ذلك كفالة وليس منها في شيء لأن القائل مستأجر لمن جاء به وهو الذي يلزمه ضمان الأجرة التي عقد عليها لمن جاء به وليس ضماناً عن أحد، وجوابه يحمل على أنه كان رسولاً من جهة الملك والرسول سفير فلا تجب الأحكام عليه كأن يقول إن الملك قال لمن جاء به حمل بعير ثم يقول من جهته وأنا بذلك الحمل على الملك كفيل. وذكر الفخر الرازي بعدما قرر أنها دليل الكفالة إلا أن هذه كفالة لرد مال السرقة وهو كفالة ما لم يجب لأنه لا يحل للسارق أن يأخذ شيئاً على رد السرقة ولعل مثل هذه الكفالة كانت تصح عندهم اهـ. وذكر القاضي أن في هذه الآية دليلاً على جواز الجعالة وضمان الجعل قبل تمام العمل اهـ. وفي الدر المنثور للأسيوطي عن مجاهد في قوله وأنابه زعيم قال : الزعيم هو المؤذن الذي قال أيتها العير اهـ . قوله : (وقبيل) (به أي بفلان لأن القبيل هو الكفيل ولذا سمي ا الصك قبالة لأنه يحفظ الحق فمعناه القابل للضمان. وفي الصحاح : القبيل الكفيل والعريف وقد قبل به يقبل به قبالة ونحن في قبالته أي في عرافته، والقبيل الجماعة تكون من الثلاثة فصاعداً من قوم شتي مثل الروم والزنج والعرب والجمع قبل اهـ. وفي التتارخانية : أنا قبيل لك بنفس فلان كان كفيلاً كما لو قال على أن آتيك به سواء
And for whoever brings it, a camel-load, and I am a guarantor for it—that is kafalah, but it is not from it at all, because the speaker is hiring whoever brings it, and he is the one obliged to guarantee the wage he contracted for whoever brings it, and it is not a guarantee from someone. The answer is that it is interpreted that he was a messenger from the king, and the messenger is an envoy, so rulings are not binding on him, as if he said: The king said: 'For whoever brings it, a camel-load,' then he says on his behalf: 'And I am a surety for that load upon the king.' And Fakhr al-Razi mentioned, after establishing that it is evidence for kafalah, except that this is kafalah for returning stolen property, and it is a kafalah for what is not yet obligatory, because it is not permissible for the thief to take anything for returning stolen property, and perhaps such kafalah was valid among them. And Al-Qadi mentioned that in this verse there is evidence for the permissibility of ju'alah and guaranteeing the reward before completion of the work. And in Al-Durr al-Manthur by al-Suyuti, from Mujahid regarding His saying 'and I am a guarantor for it,' he said: Al-zaim is the announcer who said 'O caravan'. His saying: (and qabil) by him, i.e., by so-and-so, because al-qabil is the surety, and for this reason the document is called qabalah because it preserves the right, so its meaning is the acceptor of guarantee. And in Al-Sihah: Al-qabil is the surety and the overseer, and he has accepted, yiqbal bihi qabalah, and we are in his qabalah meaning his oversight. And al-qabil is a group of three or more from different peoples like Romans, Zanj, and Arabs, plural qabail. And in al-Tatar Khaniyyah: 'I am a qabil for you for the person of so-and-so' is a surety just as if he said 'I guarantee to bring him to you' equally.

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the verse of Yusuf (AS) and the term 'qabil'. The author (Ibn Nujaym) explains that the phrase 'I am a zaim (guarantor) for it' from the verse is not actually kafalah (suretyship) but rather a promise of reward (ju'alah) for returning the lost item, because the speaker is a messenger. The discussion cites Fakhr al-Razi and others. Then the author defines 'qabil' as the surety (kafil) and cites lexical sources. The key point is that saying 'ana qabilun laka bi-nafsi fulan' is a valid form of kafalah.

Key Terms
زعيم (za‘īm): In this context, interpreted as a messenger/announcer, not a surety
قبيل (qabīl): The surety (kafil), one who accepts responsibility

Key Takeaway
The term 'qabil' is a valid term for surety in Hanafi kafalah, while the 'zaim' in Surah Yusuf refers to a messenger, not a guarantor.

Kafalah — Surety
