لأهل
His going and returning. If the time elapsed and he did not bring him, he imprisons him. If he is absent and his location is not known, he is not demanded [to produce him]. He said: (If he is absent and his location is not known, he is not demanded to produce him) because he is incapable. And it is necessary to establish that he is absent with unknown location, either by the admission of the claimant (and the commentator limited himself to this) or by evidence that the surety presents, as in al-Qunyah from Ali al-Sughdi: When the principal is absent, the creditor may demand the surety [to produce him] until he brings him. The trick to repel this is for the surety to claim against him: 'Your opponent is absent in an unknown absence, so show me his location.' If he provides evidence for that, the litigation is repelled from him. End. And regarding the claimant's demanding [the surety] when he is unable to produce the principal, there is a difference: al-Sarakhsi said that he demands him, and Shaykh al-Islam said that he does not demand him; as in al-Tatarkhaniyah. If they differ and there is no evidence, and the surety says 'I do not know his location' and the claimant says 'You know it', then if he has a known travel for trade at every time, the claimant's word is taken and the surety is ordered to go to that place. Otherwise, the surety's word is taken because he relies on the original state, which is ignorance. And his statement 'he is not demanded' is conditioned on the claimant not proving that he is in a specific location. If he proves it, the surety is ordered to go to him and bring him because he knows his location. If he knows that he has apostatized and joined the abode of war, the surety is given a time for his going and returning, and it is not voided by joining the abode of war because although it is a death by legal fiction, it is concerning his wealth; otherwise he is required to repent and return. Thus it was stated unrestrictedly in al-Nihayah. And al-Dhakhirah restricted it to when the surety is able to return him, i.e., if there is an agreement between us and them that they will return the apostate to us; otherwise, he is not held liable. End. This is an indispensable restriction. Then in every place we say that he is ordered to go to him, the claimant may secure a surety from the surety until the end. And in al-Khaniyah: The surety for the person, if he prevents the principal from traveling: if the surety is immediate, he may prevent him until he produces him at the time of the surety's liability; if the surety is deferred, he may not prevent him from leaving before the term comes. End. Its apparent meaning is that the surety may constrain the principal if it is immediate, even if the claimant does not constrain him. He said: (If he delivers him in a place where the principal [creditor] can sue him, like a city, he is released) because he has fulfilled what he undertook, since he only undertook to deliver him once, and the claimant's purpose has been achieved, so the suretyship remains no more, just as if he was surety for property and paid it. He stated it absolutely, so it includes whether there is a time for delivery and he delivered before it or not, because the term is the surety's right, so he may waive it, like a deferred debt if the debtor pays before the term. Delivery is by vacating [the principal] between him and the opponent, i.e., by removing obstacles, so he says: 'This is your opponent, take him if you wish.' If he delivers him after his demand, he is released absolutely; otherwise he is not released until he says: 'I deliver him to you by virtue of the suretyship.' And in al-Qunyah: The principal creditor was sitting with some people in a school, and the surety came with the principal and said: 'This is the principal,' but he did not sit but passed and went out to another door. This amount is considered delivery. End. He restricted it by his saying 'such that he can sue' to exclude when he is not absent from him. He said: (And otherwise the word is the surety's etc.) This contradicts his earlier statement: 'And it is necessary to establish that he is absent with unknown location,' because it implies that the surety's statement 'I do not know his location' is not sufficient. Consider. He said: (This amount is considered delivery) he said. [Page 353] Such that the principal creditor can sue him, like a city, he is released. And if he stipulated his delivery in the judge's court, he should deliver him there, because the condition is beneficial. If he delivers him in his court, he is released. And his saying 'he should deliver him there' indicates the requirement of that condition. So if he delivers him in the market, he is not released. This is the statement of Zufar, and it is the fatwa in our time due to people's negligence in establishing the truth. The place of difference is in a town where they are not accustomed to removing the debtor from the hand of his opponent, as in al-Tatarkhaniyah. This is one of the issues in which fatwa is given according to Zufar, may Allah have mercy on him. Among them are: the sitting of a sick person in his prayer like the sitting of the praying person in the tashahhud; hearing the evidence from the wife of an absent person so that the judge may determine maintenance for her; that the agent for litigation does not take possession; making the collector liable if he informs against someone to the sultan and causes him to pay something; that seeing the house from the courtyard is not sufficient, rather seeing its interior is necessary; that seeing the apparent part of a folded garment is not sufficient, rather it must be unfolded. These are seven, but not meant for enumeration. And in al-Qunyah: He became surety for the person in the city and delivered him in the rural districts; it is valid if there is a judge there. And al-'Ala' al-Tajiri and al-Badr stated: Apparently it is not valid. He said about it: Their answer is good because most judges of the rural districts of Khwarazm are oppressors, so he cannot sue him with justice except in their rural districts. End. And if he delivers him in another city other than the city in which he was surety, he is released according to Abu Hanifah if there is a ruler or judge and the suretyship was not restricted to a specific city; otherwise there is no release by agreement, as in al-Tatarkhaniyah, because it is possible to bring him to the judge's court. According to the two companions, he is not released for the possibility that his witnesses are in the specified place? The correct expression would be: for the possibility that his witnesses are in the city in which he was surety; otherwise, if specified, there is no release by agreement as he mentioned. He said: (It is said that it is a difference of time and era). Al-Zayla'i said: Abu Hanifah said that in his time when the dominant were the people of...

Commentary
This passage is a comprehensive commentary (sharh) on the matn of Kanz al-Daqaiq regarding the rulings of kafalah bil nafs (suretyship for the person). The author, Zayn al-Din ibn Nujaym, examines several interconnected issues. First, when the principal (al-makful 'anhu) is absent and his location is unknown, the surety is not required to produce him because he is incapable. However, this absence must be established either by the claimant's admission or by evidence from the surety. There is a difference of opinion among later scholars: al-Sarakhsi holds that the claimant may still demand the surety to produce the principal even when the surety is unable, while Shaykh al-Islam holds that he may not. When the surety claims ignorance of the location and the claimant claims knowledge, the surety's word is accepted unless the principal has a known travel route for trade, in which case the surety must go there. If the principal has apostatized and joined enemy territory, the surety is given time to retrieve him, but this is conditioned on the ability to do so (e.g., a treaty for return). The surety also has the right to prevent the principal from traveling if the suretyship is immediate (hala), but not if deferred (mu'ajjala). The second major topic is the release of the surety upon delivery of the principal. The surety is released if he delivers the principal in a place where the claimant can sue him, such as a city with a judge. Delivering in a wilderness or rural area does not suffice. If the surety delivers after the claimant demands, he is released absolutely; otherwise, he must explicitly state that he delivers by virtue of the suretyship. A crucial detail is the condition of delivery in the judge's court (majlis al-qadi). If such a condition is stipulated, delivery elsewhere does not release, and this is the opinion of Imam Zufar, which is followed in later fatwa due to people's negligence. The author lists seven issues where Zufar's opinion is adopted. Finally, the passage discusses delivery in another city: Abu Hanifah rules that it releases if there is a ruler or judge, while the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) disagree, fearing that the claimant's witnesses may not be present. This difference is attributed to changes in time and custom, as noted by al-Zayla'i. The commentary reflects the Hanafi method of detailed analysis of conditions and practical adaptation.

Key Terms
مجلس القاضي (majlis al-qadi): The judge's court where legal proceedings take place.
رساتيق (rasatiq): Rural districts or villages outside a city.
مصر (misr): A city or large town with a judge and established judiciary.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why did later Hanafi fatwa adopt Imam Zufar's opinion on delivery in the judge's court?
A) Because it was the original position of Abu Hanifah.
B) Because people had become negligent in establishing the truth.
C) Because it is easier for the surety.
D) Because the majority of scholars agreed on it.

Key Takeaway
The surety's release upon delivery is conditional on effective delivery in a proper location, and stipulated conditions (like the judge's court) must be strictly adhered to, as per later fatwa following Zufar.

Kafalah — Surety
intermediate
10 min
Discharge of the Surety in Kafala bi'n-Nafs: Delivery, Stipulation, and the Zufar Dispute
Commentary on the ruling of kafala bi'n-nafs regarding delivery and stipulation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which a surety is discharged upon delivering the principal, explain the effect of stipulating the judge's court, and compare the positions of Abu Hanifa and Zufar, identifying the mufta bihi.

Core Idea
A surety is discharged only if delivery enables the claimant to sue; stipulating the court makes it exclusive in Zufar's view.

This passage from 'Al-Bahr al-Ra’iq' commentary on Kanz al-Daqa’iq addresses a key question in kafala bi'n-nafs: when does delivery of the principal actually discharge the surety? The text explores the effect of the place of delivery, especially when a specific place like the judge's court is stipulated. It also presents an intra-Hanafi disagreement between Abu Hanifa and his student Zufar, and explains why later scholars adopted Zufar's view in times of corruption.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its default ruling
The act is the surety (kafil) delivering the principal (makful bihi) to the guarantee holder (makful lahu). The default ruling is that the surety is discharged if the delivery occurs in a place where the guarantee holder can sue the principal.
Focus on 'yumkinhu an yukhasimahu' – ability to sue is key.
2
Analyse the effect of stipulating the place of delivery
If the surety stipulates delivery in the judge's court, then delivering elsewhere (e.g., market) does not discharge him according to Zufar. The condition is beneficial and must be honoured.
Note the phrase 'sallamahu thumma' – delivers him there.
3
Compare the positions of Abu Hanifa and Zufar
Abu Hanifa holds that delivery anywhere in the city is sufficient as long as the guarantee holder can sue. Zufar requires strict adherence to the stipulated place, and his view is now the mufta bihi due to people's negligence.
Use the madhhab_dispute_map to structure the comparison.
4
Identify the thamarah al-khilaf (practical difference)
The practical consequence: if a surety delivers in the market despite stipulation, he is discharged according to Abu Hanifa but not according to Zufar. The fatwa follows Zufar.
This is a clear example of tarjih based on changing social conditions.
5
Note the seven issues where Zufar's view is taken
The text lists seven issues (including this one) where fatwa is given with Zufar's opinion. This illustrates how later Hanafi fatwa can adopt minority views when maslaha demands it.
The list is not exhaustive; it shows the principle of selection.

بحيث يقدر المكفول له أن يخاصمه كمصر برئ ولو شرط تسليمه في مجلس القاضي سلمه سلمه في برية أو في سواد فإنه لا يبرأ لعدم قدرته على مخاصمته في ذلك المكان، سواء شرط تسليمه في مجلس القاضي أو لا. وفي الخانية : وهو نظير ما إذا سلم المديون الدين للطالب حين خرج اللصوص فإنه لا يبرأ وفي القنية : سلم الكفيل بالنفس المكفول عنه إلى الطالب ليلاً في مكان لا يمكنه العصمة وفر منه فإن كان التسليم بطلبه يخرج عن العهدة اهـ. قوله : (ولو شرط تسليمه في مجلس القاضي سلمه ثم لأن الشرط مفيد فإن سلمه في مجلسه برئ. وأفاد بقوله سلمه ثم إلى اشتراط ذلك فإن سلمه في السوق لم يبرأ وهو قول زفر وبه يفتى في زماننا لتهاون الناس في إقامة الحق ومحل الاختلاف في بلدة لم يعتادوا نزع الغريم من يد خصمه كذا في التتارخانية وهذه إحدى المسائل التي يفتى فيها بقول زفر رحمه الله تعالى، ومنها قعود المريض في صلاته كقعود المصلي في التشهد، ومنها سماع البينة من امرأة الغائب ليقرر القاضي لها نفقة ومنها أن الوكيل بالخصومة لا يلي القبض، ومنها تضمين الساعي إذا سعى به إلى السلطان وغرمه شيئاً، ومنها أن رؤية البيت من الصحن لا يكفي بل لا بد من رؤية داخله ومنها أن رؤية ظاهر الثوب مطوياً لا يكفي بل لا بد من نشره فهي سبع، وليس المراد الحصر.
Such that the guarantee holder is able to sue him (the principal) like in Egypt, he is discharged. And if he stipulates delivery in the judge's court, he delivers him. If he delivers him in a wilderness or rural area, he is not discharged due to his inability to sue him in that place, whether he stipulated delivery in the judge's court or not. In al-Khaniyya: It is analogous to when the debtor pays the debt to the claimant at the time when thieves come out, he is not discharged. In al-Qunyah: The surety for the person delivered the guaranteed person to the claimant at night in a place where protection is not possible, and he fled from him; if the delivery was at his request, he exits from liability. His statement: (And if he stipulates delivery in the judge's court, he delivers him there) because the condition is beneficial; if he delivers him in his court, he is discharged. And his statement 'delivers him there' indicates the stipulation of that; if he delivers him in the market, he is not discharged, and this is the view of Zufar, and fatwa is given with it in our time due to people's negligence in establishing the truth. The place of disagreement is in a town where they are not accustomed to taking the debtor from the hand of his adversary, as in al-Tatarkhaniyya. This is one of the issues in which fatwa is given with the view of Zufar. And among them: the sitting of a sick person in his prayer like the sitting of the one praying in tashahhud; and among them: hearing the testimony of the absent person's wife so that the judge may fix maintenance for her; and among them: that the agent for litigation does not take possession; and among them: making the collector liable if he goes to the sultan and pays something; and among them: that seeing the house from the courtyard is not sufficient, rather it is necessary to see its interior; and among them: that seeing the apparent part of the folded garment is not sufficient, rather it is necessary to unfold it. So these are seven, but enumeration is not intended.
Source pages p. 353

Commentary
This passage from the Sharh of Kanz al-Daqaiq discusses the ruling of kafala bi'n-nafs (suretyship for the person) regarding the discharge of the surety (kafil) upon delivering the principal (makful) to the guarantee holder (makful lahu). The author begins by stating that if the surety delivers the principal in a place where the guarantee holder can easily sue him (like in a city with a judge), the surety is discharged. However, if he delivers in a remote place such as a wilderness or rural area where suing is impossible, the surety is not discharged, regardless of whether a condition was stipulated. This is similar to a debtor paying a debt when thieves are present—the payment does not discharge him because it is not secure. The commentary then explains the phrase 'wa law sharata taslimahu fi majlis al-qadi sallamahu' (if he stipulates delivery in the judge's court, he delivers him there). The condition is beneficial; if the surety delivers the principal in the judge's court, he is discharged. The phrase 'sallamahu thumma' indicates that if he stipulates the court, then delivering in the market does not discharge. This is the view of Imam Zufar (a Hanafi jurist), and the later scholars give fatwa with his view in times of corruption and negligence. The disagreement applies only in towns where it is not customary to forcibly take the debtor from his adversary. This is one of seven issues where fatwa is given according to Zufar's view; the other six briefly listed are: (1) the sick person sitting in prayer like tashahhud, (2) hearing the testimony of the absent wife for maintenance, (3) the litigation agent not taking possession, (4) liability of a collector who goes to the sultan and pays something, (5) seeing the house from outside being insufficient, (6) seeing the folded garment being insufficient. The passage notes that the enumeration is not exhaustive.

Key Terms
الكفالة (al-kafala): A contract of suretyship where one person (kafil) guarantees the appearance of another (makful) or payment of a debt.
الكفيل (al-kafil): The surety who guarantees the person or debt of another.
المكفول به (al-makful bihi): The principal (person) whose appearance or debt is guaranteed.
المكفول له (al-makful lahu): The guarantee holder (claimant) who benefits from the surety.
القول بزفر (al-qawl bi-Zufar): The legal opinion of Imam Zufar ibn al-Hudhayl, a Hanafi jurist, considered in certain issues.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, what is the ruling if a surety stipulates delivery in the judge's court but delivers the principal in the market?
A) The surety is discharged because the market is within the same city.
B) The surety is not discharged; this is the view of Zufar and the fatwa in our time.
C) The surety is discharged only if he delivers the principal directly to the guarantee holder.
D) The surety is discharged because the condition is not binding.

Key Takeaway
In kafala bi'n-nafs, the surety's discharge depends on the place of delivery; if a specific place is stipulated, strict adherence is required according to the mufta bihi (Zufar's view), especially when people are negligent.

Kafalah — Surety

Voidance of Kafala bil-Nafs by Death of Principal or Surety
Explaining the ruling that kafala bil nafs becomes void upon death of principal or surety

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that kafala bil nafs becomes void upon the death of the principal or surety, and explain the talil (legal reasoning) for this voidance.

Core Idea
A surety for a person is discharged by death because the obligation to produce the person cannot survive death.

In this lesson, we examine a ruling from al-Bahr al-Raiq on what terminates a suretyship of the person (kafala bil nafs). The text clarifies that upon the death of either the principal debtor (matlub) or the surety (kafil), the contract becomes void and the surety is discharged. This is based on the impossibility of performance after death. The author also distinguishes the case of suretyship for property (kafala bil mal), which survives death, and addresses a less authoritative intra-Hanafi opinion from al-Karkhi, who held that the obligation transfers to the heir.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling on death of principal or surety
Read the opening phrase: 'And it becomes void upon death of the principal and the surety, not the creditor.' This is the core ruling.
2
Examine the reasoning (talil) for voidance
The text gives the reason: 'due to his inability to produce him after his death.' The surety cannot physically produce a deceased person, and the heirs do not inherit this obligation because inheritance applies to rights, not duties.
3
Distinguish from kafala bil mal
The passage contrasts: if the surety for property dies, the contract does not void; the debt is paid from the estate, and heirs may reclaim from the principal. This highlights the personal nature of kafala bil nafs.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Al-Karkhi held that the obligation continues and the heir must produce the principal. However, the relied-upon position (mufta bihi) follows al-Hidayah and rejects this view.
5
Apply to special cases and exceptions
Consider death of the creditor (does not void), discharge of principal (only specific wording discharges the surety), and suretyship of a slave (liability depends on the nature of the claim).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( وتبطل بموت المطلوب والكفيل الطالب) لعجزه عن إحضاره له بعد موته، وكذا بعد موت الكفيل، ووارثهما لا يقوم مقامهما لأن الخلفية فيما له لا فيما عليه، وما له لا يصلح لإيفاء هذا الحق وهو إحضار إلا بالرشوة فيكون على هذا التقدير مصره أسهل لإثبات حقوقه اهـ قوله : (وفي البزازية ضمن نفس رجل وحبس المطلوب في السجن لا يبرأ) أي ويطالب الكفيل لما في كافي الحاكم حيث قال : وإذا المكفول به بدين أو غيره أخذت الكفيل به لأنه يقدر على أن يفكه مما حبس به بأداء حق الذي حبسه اهـ . اهـ. وأشار باقتصاره في بطلانها على موت المطلوب والكفيل إلى أنها لا تبطل بإبراء الأصيل لما في البزازية : ولو كفل بنفس ثم أقر الطالب أنه لا حق له قبل المكفول به له أن يأخذ الكفيل بتسليمه ولا يبرأ، ولو قال الطالب لا حق لي قبل المكفول به لا من جهته ولا من جهة غيره لا بوكالة ولا بوصاية ولا بولاية برئ من الكفالة .اهـ. فقولهم براءة الأصيل توجب براءة الكفيل إنما هو في الكفالة بالمال اللهم إلا أن يقال أن صورة براءة الأصيل فيما إذا كانت الكفالة بالنفس أن يقول ما ذكره فحينئذ الكلام على عمومه وفي السراج الوهاج : إذا مات المكفول له لم تبطل ويسلمه الكفيل إلى ورثته فإن سلمه إلى بعضهم برئ منهم خاصة وللباقين مطالبته بإحضاره، فإن كانوا صغاراً فلوصيهم مطالبته بأحضاره فإن سلمه إلى أحد الوصيين بريء في حقه وللآخر مطالبته ؛ كذا في الينابيع .اهـ. ومن الغريب ما في منظومة ابن وهبان وعزاه في الشرح إلى النتف أنها تبطل بموت الطالب والمعروف في المذهب خلافه. وفي فروق الكرابيسي : الكفالة على الكفالة جائزة وبموت الأصيل يبطلان وبموت الكفيل الأول يبرأ الثاني. والحوالة بعد الحوالة تبطل الحوالة الأولى لأن الكفالة للتوثق والثانية تزيده والحوالة نقل وهما لا يجتمعان .اهـ.
His statement: (And it becomes void upon death of the principal and the surety, not the creditor) due to his inability to produce him after his death, and likewise after the death of the surety, and their heirs do not stand in their place because inheritance is for what is owed to them, not what is owed by them, and what is owed to them is not suitable for fulfilling this right, which is producing the principal. The author followed the author of al-Hidayah in its voidance by the death of the surety. In al-Karkhi in the chapter of compromise regarding rights which are not property, it does not become void by the death of the surety, and his heir is demanded to produce him; as in al-Siraj al-Wahhaj. It is restricted to suretyship of the person because the surety for property, if he dies, does not become void because its ruling after his death is possible: he pays from his property, then the heirs have recourse against the principal if it was at his request and the debt was due; if it was deferred, they have no recourse until the term arrives. Otherwise, it is like his payment himself. As for the death of the creditor, it does not void it because his executor and heirs succeed him. He used the general term 'principal' so it includes the slave. But in al-Khulasa: if one becomes surety for the person of a slave and the slave dies, the surety is discharged if the claimed thing is property upon the slave; if the claimed thing is the person of the slave, he is not discharged and must pay his value. (Then a long commentary on the condition of the times)  He indicated by restricting its voidance to death of the principal and surety that it does not become void by the discharge of the original debtor; as in al-Bazzaziyya: if one becomes surety for a person, then the creditor admits that he has no right against the principal, he may still take the surety for delivery and he is not discharged. But if the creditor says 'I have no right against the principal, neither from his side nor from another's, not by agency, not by executorship, not by guardianship,' then the surety is discharged. So their statement that the discharge of the original debtor causes the discharge of the surety is only in suretyship for property. Unless it is said that the form of discharge of the original debtor in suretyship of the person is that he says what was mentioned; then the statement is general. In al-Siraj al-Wahhaj: if the creditor dies, it does not become void, and the surety delivers the principal to his heirs. If he delivers to some of them, he is discharged with respect to them specifically, and the rest may demand him to produce him. If they are minors, their guardian may demand him to produce him. If he delivers to one of two guardians, he is discharged regarding him, and the other may demand him. As in al-Yanabi'. Extraordinary is what is in the versification of Ibn Wahban, attributed in the commentary to al-Nataf, that it becomes void by the death of the creditor; but the known view in the school is contrary. In al-Furuq of al-Karabisi: suretyship upon suretyship is permissible; by the death of the original debtor both become void; by the death of the first surety the second is discharged. Al-hawala after al-hawala voids the first hawala because suretyship is for strengthening and the second increases it, while hawala is a transfer and they cannot coexist. End.
Source pages p. 354

Commentary
This passage from the commentary of al-Bahr al-Raiq explains the ruling that a suretyship of the person (kafala bil nafs) is voided upon the death of either the principal (matlub) or the surety (kafil), but not upon the death of the creditor (talib). The reason given is that after death, the surety cannot physically produce the principal, and the heirs do not inherit this obligation because inheritance concerns what is owed to the deceased, not what is owed by him. However, a minority view from al-Karkhi holds that the obligation transfers to the heir. The author then distinguishes suretyship of property, which survives the surety's death and is paid from his estate. The passage also addresses special cases: if the principal is a slave, the surety's liability depends on whether the claim is for the slave's value or his person. Further, it clarifies that the surety is not discharged merely by the creditor's acquittal of the principal, unless the acquittal explicitly negates any right from any source. The death of the creditor does not void the suretyship; the surety must deliver the principal to the heirs. Lastly, the text mentions that successive suretyships (kafala ala kafala) are valid.

Key Terms
مطلوب (matlub): The person whose appearance is guaranteed in kafala bil nafs.
كفيل (kafil): The person who guarantees to produce the principal (matlub) to the creditor.
طالب (talib): The creditor who demands the principal.
كفالة بالنفس (kafala bil nafs): A contract where the surety guarantees the physical presence of the principal.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In Hanafi fiqh, what is the effect of the death of the principal (matlub) on a kafala bil nafs?
A) The kafala becomes void and the surety is discharged.
B) The kafala continues and the surety's heir must produce the principal.
C) The surety must pay a penalty equal to the value of the principal.
D) The kafala becomes void only if the creditor also dies.

Key Takeaway
The death of either the principal or the surety voids a suretyship of the person (kafala bil nafs) because the obligation to produce the person cannot survive death.

Kafalah — Surety

Release of Surety by Delivery of Principal
Explaining when the surety is released by delivering the principal to the creditor

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the rule that the surety is discharged by delivery of the principal and identify the conditions and exceptions to this rule.

Core Idea
Delivery of the principal suffices to release the surety, even without explicit mention.

This lesson examines the Hanafi ruling on the discharge of a surety (kafil) in kafala bil nafs. The key point is that physically delivering the principal (matlub) to the creditor (talib) automatically releases the surety, even if the surety did not verbally declare his discharge. The commentator supports this with analogies from other areas of fiqh and then discusses special situations such as delivery to an agent, a judge, or a guardian.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main rule
The surety is discharged by delivering the principal to the creditor. This is the core ruling presented in the matn.
The ruling applies even if the surety does not say 'I am discharged'.
2
Understand the proof by qiyas
The commentator analogizes to a debtor paying a debt, a usurper returning stolen property, and a seller delivering sold goods. In each case, delivery itself causes discharge without explicit declaration.
The common illa (effective cause) is that delivery of what is owed fulfills the obligation.
3
Note the condition of demand
If the creditor demanded delivery, the surety is discharged upon delivery. If the creditor did not demand, the surety must indicate that the delivery is for the kafala.
This prevents the delivery from being mistaken as a gift or other transaction.
4
Examine the creditor's refusal
If the creditor refuses to accept delivery, he is compelled to accept in the sense that he is regarded as having taken possession, and the surety is discharged.
This is analogous to the debtor or usurper: the recipient must accept what is rightfully due.
5
Explore special recipients
Delivery to an agent, a judge, or a guardian may discharge the surety under certain conditions, as detailed in the passage.
The key is delivery to the rightful party or authorized representative.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( وبرئ بدفعه إليه وإن لم يقل إذا دفعته إليك فأنا بريء) لأن موجب الدفع إليه البراءة فتثبت وإن لم ينص عليها كالمديون إذا سلم الدين والغاصب إذا سلم المغصوب والبائع إذا سلم المبيع. وأطلقه فشمل ما إذا قال سلمته إليه بجهة الكفالة أو لا إن طلبه منه، وأما إذا لم يطلبه منه فلا بد يقول لك كما قدمناه. وإذا أقر الطالب بقبض المكفول برئ الكفيل ولا يحتاج فيه إلى النص لأن الظاهر أنه لا يقر إلا باستيفاء حقه، ولو سلم الكفيل المكفول إلى الطالب فأبى أن يقبله أجبر على قبوله بمعنى أنه ينزل قابضاً كالغاصب إذا رد العين والمديون إذا دفع الدين بخلاف ما إذا سلمه فضولي فإنه لا يجبر كما إذا قضى الدين فضولي أي غير مأمور بذلك، والضمير في قوله «إليه» راجع إلى الطالب. وأطلقه فشمل ما إذا كان غير صاحب الحق كما إذا كان الآخذ للكفيل وكيل الدائن فيبرأ بتسليمه إلى الموكل مطلقاً وإلى الوكيل إن أضافه إلى نفسه وإن أضافه إلى موكله لم يبرأ بتسليمه إلى الوكيل لأنه رسول ؛ كذا في التتارخانية. وكما إذا أخذ القاضي من المدعى عليه كفيلاً بالنفس بطلب المدعي أو بغير طلبه وسلمه الكفيل إلى القاضي برئ، وإن سلمه إلى المدعي لا يبرأ هذا إذا لم يضفه القاضي فإن أضافه وقال القاضي إن المدعي يطلب منك كفيلاً بالنفس فأعطه كفيلاً بنفسك فسلم الكفيل للقاضي لا يبرأ ، وإن سمله إلى المدعي يبرأ ؛ في الخانية والبزازية. ورسول القاضي وأمينة كالقاضي، ولو كفل بنفسه إلى الوصي فسلمه إلى الورثة أو الغريم لا يبرأ كذا في البزازية وفي القنية : كفل بنفس رجل على أن كذا
His statement: (And he is discharged by delivering him to him, even if he did not say 'if I deliver him to you then I am discharged') because the consequence of delivery to him is discharge, so it is established even if not explicitly stated, like the debtor when he hands over the debt, the usurper when he hands over the usurped object, and the seller when he hands over the sold item. He used the general term so it includes whether he said 'I delivered him to you as part of the suretyship' or not, as long as he demanded it from him. But if he did not demand it from him, then he must say 'for you' as we mentioned earlier. If the creditor acknowledges receipt of the principal, the surety is discharged and need not explicitly state it, because the apparent meaning is that he acknowledges only after fulfilling his right. If the surety delivers the principal to the creditor and the creditor refuses to accept, he is compelled to accept in the sense that he is considered to have taken possession, like the usurper when he returns the object and the debtor when he pays the debt. Unlike if a stranger delivers it, he is not compelled, just as if a stranger pays the debt without being authorized. The pronoun 'him' in 'to him' refers to the creditor. He used the general term so it includes when the person taking the surety is an agent of the creditor; he is discharged by delivering to the principal (the client) absolutely, and to the agent if he added [the delivery] to himself; but if he added it to his client, he is not discharged by delivering to the agent because the agent is a messenger. As in al-Tatarkhaniyya. Likewise, if the judge takes a surety of the person from the defendant at the request of the plaintiff or without his request, and the surety delivers to the judge, he is discharged; if he delivers to the plaintiff, he is not discharged, this is when the judge did not specify. If the judge specified and said 'the plaintiff requests a surety of the person from you, so give him a surety of yourself,' then if the surety delivers to the judge, he is not discharged; if he delivers to the plaintiff, he is discharged. In al-Khaniyya and al-Bazzaziyya. The judge's messenger and his trustee are like the judge. If one becomes surety for the person to a guardian, then delivers him to the heirs or the creditor, he is not discharged. As in al-Bazzaziyya. In al-Quniyya: one became surety for the person of a man on condition that...
Source pages p. 355

Commentary
This passage discusses the release of the surety (kafil) in kafala bil nafs by delivering the principal (matlub) to the creditor (talib). The commentator explains that the delivery itself causes discharge, even if the surety does not explicitly say 'I am discharged upon delivery.' This is analogous to other cases: a debtor handing over money, a usurper returning stolen property, or a seller delivering goods. The delivery must be at the creditor's demand; if the creditor has not demanded, the surety must indicate that the delivery is for the kafala. If the creditor acknowledges receipt, that suffices for discharge. If the creditor refuses to accept delivery, he is compelled to accept (i.e., regarded as having received). The passage then details special cases: delivery to an agent of the creditor, delivery to a judge, and delivery to a guardian. The general rule is that delivery must be to the rightful recipient.

Key Terms
دفع (daf'): Physical delivery of the principal to the creditor.
براءة (bara'a): Legal release of the surety from his obligation.
قبض (qabd): Taking possession by the creditor.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If the surety delivers the principal to the creditor, but the creditor refuses to accept, what happens?
A) The surety is not discharged until the creditor accepts.
B) The creditor is compelled to accept, and the surety is discharged.
C) The surety must deliver to a judge instead.
D) The surety remains liable until the creditor's death.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, the surety is released by simply delivering the principal to the creditor, without the need for any verbal declaration by either party.

Ruling on Successive Suretyship (Kafala ala Kafala)
Brief note on the permissibility of successive suretyship

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on successive suretyship and identify its basic condition.

Core Idea
A surety may stand as surety for another surety — the Hanafi school validates sequential suretyship.

This lesson addresses a specific sub-issue within kafalah: can one person become a surety for another surety's obligation? The Hanafi ruling is clear: it is permissible (ja'iz). The matn states this explicitly and refers to the detailed explanation in al-Khaniyya. Understanding this ruling helps in complex guarantee arrangements.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ruling
State the ruling of the matn: 'Kafala ala kafala is ja'iz (permissible/valid).'
The term ja'iz here means legally valid and permissible.
2
Trace the Source
Note that the commentator refers to al-Khaniyya for the full explanation. This indicates the ruling is well-established in the Hanafi school.
Al-Khaniyya is a Hanafi fatwa collection.
3
Understand the Practical Scenario
Envision a situation: Zaid guarantees Ali's debt. Then Bakr becomes a surety for Zaid's guarantee obligation. This is kafala ala kafala.
The first guarantee must be binding for the second to be valid.
4
Apply the Condition
The validity depends on the first suretyship being established and enforceable. If the first is void, the second is void.
This condition is implicit from general kafalah rules.

Kafala bi'l-Nafs: Invalidity by Death Only, Not Acquittal
Commentary on the matn's exceptional invalidity conditions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two conditions that invalidate kafala bi'l-nafs, explain why ibrā' al-aṣīl does not invalidate it, and identify the rare intra-Hanafi dispute about death of the claimant.

Core Idea
Kafala bi'l-nafs ends only with death of the demanded or guarantor; acquittal of the principal has no effect.

The author of Kanz al-Daqā'iq precisely limited the invalidity of kafala bi'l-nafs to two cases: death of the maṭlūb (the person demanded) or the kafīl (the guarantor). This brevity signals a crucial ruling: the guarantee is not nullified by the creditor's acquittal (ibrā') of the principal debtor. In this lesson, we unpack this distinction, examine the evidence from al-Bazzāziyya, and confront a rare contrary opinion within the Hanafi school.

Guided Path
1
Identify the invalidity conditions from the matn
Read the condensed statement from Kanz: the guarantee is invalidated only by death of the demanded person or the guarantor. What does this imply about other potential invalidities?
The matn's silence on other causes is deliberate.
2
Understand the distinction between kafala bi'l-nafs and bi'l-māl
The commentator explains that the general rule – 'acquittal of the principal discharges the guarantor' – applies only to kafala bi'l-māl (suretyship for property). Why would the rule differ for person suretyship?
Kafala bi'l-nafs aims to secure the body, not a debt.
3
Analyze the Bazzāziyya evidence
Study the two scenarios from al-Bazzāziyya: (1) the claimant acknowledges no right against the principal, yet the guarantor is still bound; (2) the claimant explicitly states no right from any direction – only then is the guarantor discharged. What is the effective statement for acquittal?
The discharge of the guarantor requires a specific, comprehensive waiver.
4
Recognize the rare intra-Hanafi dispute
Ibn Wahbān, quoting al-Nutaf, holds that death of the claimant (ṭālib) also invalidates the kafala. Compare this with the dominant position and consider why it is rejected.
The known madhhab position is that only death of the principal or guarantor invalidates.
5
Summarize the operative ruling
Compile the muftā bihi: kafala bi'l-nafs is not nullified by ibrā' al-aṣīl or by death of the claimant. The only invalidations are death of the maṭlūb or the kafīl.
This is the position for fatwa and practice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اهـ. وأشار باقتصاره في بطلانها على موت المطلوب والكفيل إلى أنها لا تبطل بإبراء الأصيل لما في البزازية : ولو كفل بنفس ثم أقر الطالب أنه لا حق له قبل المكفول به له أن يأخذ الكفيل بتسليمه ولا يبرأ، ولو قال الطالب لا حق لي قبل المكفول به لا من جهته ولا من جهة غيره لا بوكالة ولا بوصاية ولا بولاية برئ من الكفالة .اهـ. فقولهم براءة الأصيل توجب براءة الكفيل إنما هو في الكفالة بالمال اللهم إلا أن يقال أن صورة براءة الأصيل فيما إذا كانت الكفالة بالنفس أن يقول ما ذكره فحينئذ الكلام على عمومه وفي السراج الوهاج : إذا مات المكفول له لم تبطل ويسلمه الكفيل إلى ورثته فإن سلمه إلى بعضهم برئ منهم خاصة وللباقين مطالبته بإحضاره، فإن كانوا صغاراً فلوصيهم مطالبته بأحضاره فإن سلمه إلى أحد الوصيين بريء في حقه وللآخر مطالبته ؛ كذا في الينابيع .اهـ. ومن الغريب ما في منظومة ابن وهبان وعزاه في الشرح إلى النتف أنها تبطل بموت الطالب والمعروف في المذهب خلافه. وفي فروق الكرابيسي : الكفالة على الكفالة جائزة وبموت الأصيل يبطلان وبموت الكفيل الأول يبرأ الثاني. والحوالة بعد الحوالة تبطل الحوالة الأولى لأن الكفالة للتوثق والثانية تزيده والحوالة نقل وهما لا يجتمعان .اهـ.
He concluded. And he indicated by limiting its nullification to the death of the demanded and the guarantor that it is not nullified by acquittal of the principal. Because in al-Bazzāziyya: If one guaranteed in person, then the claimant acknowledged that he has no right against the guaranteed person, he may still take the guarantor to hand him over and he is not discharged. And if the claimant said: I have no right against the guaranteed person, neither from his side nor from another's, not by agency, nor by testamentary agency, nor by guardianship, then he is discharged from the guarantee. So their statement that acquittal of the principal entails acquittal of the guarantor is only in guarantee of property. Unless it is said that the form of acquittal of the principal in the case of guarantee in person is that he says what was mentioned, then the statement is general. And in al-Sirāj al-Wahhāj: If the guaranteed-for dies, it does not become void, and the guarantor hands him over to his heirs. If he hands him over to some of them, he is discharged specifically from them, and the rest may demand his presence. If they are minors, their guardian may demand his presence. If he hands him over to one of two guardians, he is discharged with respect to him, and the other may demand. Thus in al-Yanābī. And from the strange things in the poem of Ibn Wahbān, attributed in the commentary to al-Nutaf, that it becomes void by the death of the claimant, but the known position in the madhhab is contrary to it. And in Furūq al-Karābīsī: Guarantee on a guarantee is permissible. By the death of the principal, both become void. By the death of the first guarantor, the second is discharged. And ḥawāla after ḥawāla nullifies the first ḥawāla, because guarantee is for reassurance and the second increases it, while ḥawāla is transfer and they do not combine.
Source pages p. 355

Commentary
This lesson discusses the conditions under which kafala bi'l-nafs (suretyship for the person) becomes invalid. The author of Kanz al-Daqā'iq limited the nullification to the death of the demanded person (maṭlūb) or the guarantor (kafīl). The commentator explains that this indicates that kafala is not invalidated by the claimant's (ṭālib) acquittal of the principal debtor (aṣīl). Evidence from al-Bazzāziyya shows that if the ṭālib acknowledges that he has no right against the maṭlūb, the kafīl is still obliged to hand him over; only if the ṭālib specifically states that he has no right from any direction does the kafīl become discharged. The general rule that discharge of the principal discharges the guarantor applies only to kafala bi'l-māl (suretyship for property). However, some reconcile this by considering that the form of acquittal in kafala bi'l-nafs is the statement mentioned. Regarding the death of the mafkūl lahu (the person for whom the guarantee was given), it does not invalidate the kafala; the kafīl must hand the maṭlūb over to the heirs. If there are multiple heirs and he hands to some, he is only discharged from their share. If the heirs are minors, the guardian can demand. The rare opinion of Ibn Wahbān that it is invalidated by the death of the ṭālib is rejected in the madhhab. Finally, from Furuq al-Karābīsī, kafala on kafala is permissible; if the original principal dies, both are void; if the first guarantor dies, the second is discharged. Similarly, ḥawāla after ḥawāla invalidates the first because the purposes conflict.

Key Terms
الكفالة بالنفس (al-kafāla bi'l-nafs): Suretyship for the person of the debtor, where the guarantor must produce the debtor's body.
الكفالة بالمال (al-kafāla bi'l-māl): Suretyship for property, where the guarantor is liable for a debt.
الإبراء (al-ibrā'): Acquittal or discharge of a debtor by the creditor.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon Hanafi position, which of the following does NOT invalidate kafala bi'l-nafs?
A) Death of the demanded person (maṭlūb)
B) Death of the guarantor (kafīl)
C) Death of the claimant (ṭālib)
D) Death of the principal debtor (aṣīl)

Key Takeaway
Kafala bi'l-nafs is only invalidated by the death of the demanded person or the guarantor; neither acquittal of the principal nor death of the claimant voids it.

Discharge by Delivery: The Core Rule and Its Proof by Analogy
Expansion on the matn's core ruling with analogical proofs

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that delivering the guaranteed person discharges the guarantor and apply the qiyas (analogy) with debt payment to justify this ruling.

Core Idea
Delivering the guaranteed person to the claimant is like paying a debt; it automatically discharges the guarantor.

This lesson unpacks the core principle of kafālah: the guarantor (kafīl) is discharged simply by handing over the person whose appearance he guaranteed (maṭlūb) to the claimant (ṭālib). No special wording is needed. The commentator strengthens this by drawing powerful analogies with a debtor paying a debt, a usurper returning stolen goods, and a seller delivering sold items. Understanding why delivery alone suffices—and when it does not—is essential for correctly applying the law of guarantees.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is delivering the maṭlūb to the ṭālib. The ruling is that the kafīl becomes bari' (discharged). Recognize this as the central hukm of the passage.
Discharge occurs even if the kafīl does not verbally state that he is delivering in his capacity as guarantor.
2
Locate the dalil: qiyas on debt payment
The text explicitly compares the kafīl's delivery to a debtor paying a debt, a usurper returning property, and a seller delivering goods. Identify these as asl (original cases) and the kafīl's delivery as the far' (new case).
This is a qiyas jali (clear analogy) because the illah is performance of a legally required act.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
In each asl, the obligation is fulfilled by the act itself (payment, return, delivery). The same cause (sabab) operates for the kafīl: the act of delivering the person fulfills his obligation, so discharge follows automatically.
The text says 'because the cause of delivery to him is discharge' — the sabab is the delivery itself.
4
Note the conditions and exceptions
The ruling is conditional on the ṭālib having demanded delivery. If no demand, the kafīl must specify that he is delivering as a guarantor. Also, a stranger (fūḍūlī) cannot compel acceptance, unlike the authorized kafīl.
The refusal of the ṭālib does not prevent discharge; he is compelled to accept.
5
Refute the misconception of repeated delivery
The passage explicitly denies that the kafīl must deliver multiple times until the ṭālib's underlying claim is satisfied. One delivery is enough, because the contract aims at reassurance, not repeated performance.
This is a key negative point: the kafāla contract requires a single delivery, not repeated ones.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( وبرئ بدفعه إليه وإن لم يقل إذا دفعته إليك فأنا بريء) لأن موجب الدفع إليه البراءة فتثبت وإن لم ينص عليها كالمديون إذا سلم الدين والغاصب إذا سلم المغصوب والبائع إذا سلم المبيع. وأطلقه فشمل ما إذا قال سلمته إليه بجهة الكفالة أو لا إن طلبه منه، وأما إذا لم يطلبه منه فلا بد يقول لك كما قدمناه. وإذا أقر الطالب بقبض المكفول برئ الكفيل ولا يحتاج فيه إلى النص لأن الظاهر أنه لا يقر إلا باستيفاء حقه، ولو سلم الكفيل المكفول إلى الطالب فأبى أن يقبله أجبر على قبوله بمعنى أنه ينزل قابضاً كالغاصب إذا رد العين والمديون إذا دفع الدين بخلاف ما إذا سلمه فضولي فإنه لا يجبر كما إذا قضى الدين فضولي أي غير مأمور بذلك، والضمير في قوله «إليه» راجع إلى الطالب. وأطلقه فشمل ما إذا كان غير صاحب الحق كما إذا كان الآخذ للكفيل وكيل الدائن فيبرأ بتسليمه إلى الموكل مطلقاً وإلى الوكيل إن أضافه إلى نفسه وإن أضافه إلى موكله لم يبرأ بتسليمه إلى الوكيل لأنه رسول ؛ كذا في التتارخانية. وكما إذا أخذ القاضي من المدعى عليه كفيلاً بالنفس بطلب المدعي أو بغير طلبه وسلمه الكفيل إلى القاضي برئ، وإن سلمه إلى المدعي لا يبرأ هذا إذا لم يضفه القاضي فإن أضافه وقال القاضي إن المدعي يطلب منك كفيلاً بالنفس فأعطه كفيلاً بنفسك فسلم الكفيل للقاضي لا يبرأ ، وإن سمله إلى المدعي يبرأ ؛ في الخانية والبزازية. ورسول القاضي وأمينة كالقاضي، ولو كفل بنفسه إلى الوصي فسلمه إلى الورثة أو الغريم لا يبرأ كذا في البزازية وفي القنية : كفل بنفس رجل على أن كذا يسلمه إلى المكفول له متى طالبه به ثم سلمه إليه قبل أن يطالبه ولم يقبله يبرأ لأن حكم الكفالة وجوب التسليم وهو ثابت في الحال. وقوله على أن يسلمه إليه متى طالبه به يذكر للتأكيد لا للتعليق فقد سلمه إليه حال كونه كفيلاً فيبرأ .اهـ. وإنما ذكروا هذه المسألة أعني مسألة مع ظهورها كما قاله الفقيه أبو الليث لدفع توهم أنه يلزم الكفيل تسليمه مرة بعد مرة إلى أن يستوفي حقه لأن الكفالة ما أريدت إلا للتوثق لاستيفاء الحق، فما لم يستوفه يجب عليه تسليمه إلى أن يستوفيه فأزال هذا الوهم ببيان أن عقد الكفالة يوجب التسليم مرة لا بقيد التكرار ؛ كذا في فتح القدير .
He said: (And he is discharged by delivering him to him, even if he does not say: when I have delivered him to you I am discharged) because the cause of delivery to him is discharge, so it is established even if not stipulated, like a debtor when he pays the debt, and a usurper when he returns the usurped item, and a seller when he delivers the sold item. And he gave it absolutely, so it includes whether he said: I delivered him to him by way of guarantee or not, if he demanded it from him. But if he did not demand it from him, he must say it to you as we have mentioned. And if the claimant acknowledges receipt of the guaranteed person, the guarantor is discharged and no explicit statement is needed, because the apparent meaning is that he only acknowledges when his right is fulfilled. And if the guarantor delivers the guaranteed person to the claimant and he refuses to accept him, he is compelled to accept him, meaning that he is considered as having taken possession, like a usurper when he returns the property and a debtor when he pays the debt, unlike if a stranger delivers him, then he is not compelled, as if a stranger pays the debt, i.e., one not ordered to do so. And the pronoun in his saying 'to him' refers to the claimant. And he gave it absolutely, so it includes when he is not the owner of the right, as when the taker of the guarantor is the agent of the creditor; then he is discharged by delivering him to the principal absolutely, and to the agent if he referred it to himself, but if he referred it to his principal, he is not discharged by delivering him to the agent because he is a messenger. Likewise, if the judge takes from the defendant a guarantor for the person, at the request of the plaintiff or without his request, and the guarantor delivers him to the judge, he is discharged. If he delivers him to the plaintiff, he is not discharged, this is when the judge does not refer it. If he refers it and the judge says: The plaintiff asks for a guarantor from you for the person, so give him a guarantor for yourself, and the guarantor delivers him to the judge, he is not discharged; if he delivers him to the plaintiff, he is discharged. And the judge's messenger and his trusted deputy are like the judge. And if one guarantees in person to the guardian, and then delivers him to the heirs or the creditor, he is not discharged. And in al-Qunyah: A man guaranteed in person on condition that he would deliver him to the guaranteed-for whenever he demands him, then he delivered him before he demanded him and he did not accept him, he is discharged because the ruling of guarantee is the obligation of delivery, and it is established at present. And his saying 'on condition that he delivers him to him whenever he demands him' is mentioned for emphasis, not for condition; so he delivered him while he was a guarantor, so he is discharged. They mentioned this issue, even though it is obvious, as Abū al-Layth said, to refute the misconception that the guarantor must deliver him repeatedly until his right is fulfilled. Because guarantee is intended only for reassurance for the fulfillment of the right, so as long as it is not fulfilled, he must deliver him until it is fulfilled. He removed this misconception by clarifying that the contract of guarantee requires delivery once, not with the condition of repetition. Thus in Fatḥ al-Qadīr.
Source pages p. 355

Commentary
This passage explains that the kafīl (guarantor) is discharged by simply delivering the maṭlūb (the person whose appearance is guaranteed) to the ṭālib (the claimant). The cause of discharge is the delivery itself; no explicit wording of discharge is needed. The commentator draws analogies with a debtor paying a debt, a usurper returning stolen property, and a seller delivering the sold item. The rule applies whether the kafīl explicitly states that he is delivering in his capacity as surety or not, provided the ṭālib demanded delivery. If the ṭālib did not demand, the kafīl must mention the purpose. If the ṭālib acknowledges receipt, discharge occurs even without explicit statement. If the ṭālib refuses to accept, he is compelled to do so, and the kafīl is considered to have performed his duty. A stranger (fūḍūlī) cannot compel acceptance. The ruling is extended to delivery to a representative: if the ṭālib's agent took the surety, delivering to the principal (the ṭālib) always discharges; delivering to the agent only discharges if the agent referred the bailment to himself. Delivery to a judge discharges, unless the judge specified that the surety is for the claimant, in which case only delivery to the claimant discharges. The judge's messenger and deputy have the same status. If the guarantee was made to a guardian (waṣī), delivering to the heirs or other creditors does not discharge. A crucial point is that the obligation to deliver arises immediately and is fulfilled by a single delivery. There is a misconception that the kafīl might have to deliver repeatedly until the claimant's right (e.g., a debt) is fully satisfied; the text clarifies that the guarantee contract only requires one delivery, not repeated ones. The purpose of kafāla is reassurance, so once the person is available for the claimant to proceed with his claim, the kafīl's duty is done.

Key Terms
برئ (bari'a): Discharged from the obligation of the guarantee; no longer liable to produce the person.
فضولي (fūḍūlī): A stranger who acts without authorization; his delivery does not bind the ṭālib to accept.
دفع (daf'): Physical delivery or handing over; the act that fulfills the kafīl's obligation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi ruling, what is the cause of the kafīl's discharge?
A) The kafīl's verbal declaration that he is delivering for the guarantee
B) The ṭālib's written acknowledgment of receipt
C) The actual delivery of the guaranteed person to the ṭālib
D) The payment of the underlying debt by the guaranteed person

Key Takeaway
A guarantor is discharged by a single delivery of the guaranteed person to the claimant, because the act of delivery itself is the legal cause of discharge, analogous to paying a debt.

Special Cases: Delivery to Agents, the Judge, and the Single-Delivery Principle
Expansion on the matn's ruling covering delivery to representatives and single-delivery clarification

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the ruling of discharge by delivery in cases involving agents, judges, and guardians, and explain why only one delivery is required.

Core Idea
Delivery to an authorized representative invokes the same discharge, but delivery to a judge or guardian requires careful attention to the capacity in which they act.

The previous lesson established that delivering the guaranteed person to the claimant discharges the guarantor. But what if the claimant appointed an agent? Or if a judge took the guarantee? This lesson explores those variations, showing how the same principle applies with nuanced conditions. It also reinforces the vital point that the guarantee contract obliges only one delivery, not a repeated obligation until the underlying claim is satisfied.

Guided Path
1
Delivery to the claimant's agent
If the claimant's agent (wakīl) took the guarantee from the kafīl, delivering to the agent discharges the kafīl only if the agent refers the bailment to himself. If the agent says 'I am taking for my principal', delivery to the agent does not discharge; it must be to the principal.
The distinction is whether the agent acts as a principal in the bailment or as a mere messenger (rasūl).
2
Delivery to the judge
If a judge takes a guarantee from a defendant at the plaintiff's request, delivering to the judge discharges. If the judge took it without the plaintiff's request, delivery to the plaintiff does not discharge; must deliver to the judge. If the judge specifies 'the plaintiff asks for a guarantor, so give me a guarantor', then delivery to the judge does not discharge; must deliver to the plaintiff.
The judge's capacity (agent of the court vs. agent of the plaintiff) matters.
3
Delivery to the guardian (waṣī) and others
If a guarantee is made to a guardian, delivering the person to the heirs or to a creditor of the estate does not discharge the kafīl. The delivery must be to the guardian himself.
Similar to the agent case: delivery must be to the specific person entitled to receive.
4
The single-delivery principle
The passage explicitly refutes the idea that the kafīl must deliver repeatedly until the underlying claim is satisfied. The kafālah contract requires only one delivery, because its purpose is reassurance, not collateral.
This is a key negative: the kafīl is not a repeat performer. One successful delivery ends the obligation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( وبرئ بدفعه إليه وإن لم يقل إذا دفعته إليك فأنا بريء) لأن موجب الدفع إليه البراءة فتثبت وإن لم ينص عليها كالمديون إذا سلم الدين والغاصب إذا سلم المغصوب والبائع إذا سلم المبيع. وأطلقه فشمل ما إذا قال سلمته إليه بجهة الكفالة أو لا إن طلبه منه، وأما إذا لم يطلبه منه فلا بد يقول لك كما قدمناه. وإذا أقر الطالب بقبض المكفول برئ الكفيل ولا يحتاج فيه إلى النص لأن الظاهر أنه لا يقر إلا باستيفاء حقه، ولو سلم الكفيل المكفول إلى الطالب فأبى أن يقبله أجبر على قبوله بمعنى أنه ينزل قابضاً كالغاصب إذا رد العين والمديون إذا دفع الدين بخلاف ما إذا سلمه فضولي فإنه لا يجبر كما إذا قضى الدين فضولي أي غير مأمور بذلك، والضمير في قوله «إليه» راجع إلى الطالب. وأطلقه فشمل ما إذا كان غير صاحب الحق كما إذا كان الآخذ للكفيل وكيل الدائن فيبرأ بتسليمه إلى الموكل مطلقاً وإلى الوكيل إن أضافه إلى نفسه وإن أضافه إلى موكله لم يبرأ بتسليمه إلى الوكيل لأنه رسول ؛ كذا في التتارخانية. وكما إذا أخذ القاضي من المدعى عليه كفيلاً بالنفس بطلب المدعي أو بغير طلبه وسلمه الكفيل إلى القاضي برئ، وإن سلمه إلى المدعي لا يبرأ هذا إذا لم يضفه القاضي فإن أضافه وقال القاضي إن المدعي يطلب منك كفيلاً بالنفس فأعطه كفيلاً بنفسك فسلم الكفيل للقاضي لا يبرأ ، وإن سمله إلى المدعي يبرأ ؛ في الخانية والبزازية. ورسول القاضي وأمينة كالقاضي، ولو كفل بنفسه إلى الوصي فسلمه إلى الورثة أو الغريم لا يبرأ كذا في البزازية وفي القنية : كفل بنفس رجل على أن كذا يسلمه إلى المكفول له متى طالبه به ثم سلمه إليه قبل أن يطالبه ولم يقبله يبرأ لأن حكم الكفالة وجوب التسليم وهو ثابت في الحال. وقوله على أن يسلمه إليه متى طالبه به يذكر للتأكيد لا للتعليق فقد سلمه إليه حال كونه كفيلاً فيبرأ .اهـ. وإنما ذكروا هذه المسألة أعني مسألة مع ظهورها كما قاله الفقيه أبو الليث لدفع توهم أنه يلزم الكفيل تسليمه مرة بعد مرة إلى أن يستوفي حقه لأن الكفالة ما أريدت إلا للتوثق لاستيفاء الحق، فما لم يستوفه يجب عليه تسليمه إلى أن يستوفيه فأزال هذا الوهم ببيان أن عقد الكفالة يوجب التسليم مرة لا بقيد التكرار ؛ كذا في فتح القدير .
He said: (And he is discharged by delivering him to him, even if he does not say: when I have delivered him to you I am discharged) because the cause of delivery to him is discharge, so it is established even if not stipulated, like a debtor when he pays the debt, and a usurper when he returns the usurped item, and a seller when he delivers the sold item. And he gave it absolutely, so it includes whether he said: I delivered him to him by way of guarantee or not, if he demanded it from him. But if he did not demand it from him, he must say it to you as we have mentioned. And if the claimant acknowledges receipt of the guaranteed person, the guarantor is discharged and no explicit statement is needed, because the apparent meaning is that he only acknowledges when his right is fulfilled. And if the guarantor delivers the guaranteed person to the claimant and he refuses to accept him, he is compelled to accept him, meaning that he is considered as having taken possession, like a usurper when he returns the property and a debtor when he pays the debt, unlike if a stranger delivers him, then he is not compelled, as if a stranger pays the debt, i.e., one not ordered to do so. And the pronoun in his saying 'to him' refers to the claimant. And he gave it absolutely, so it includes when he is not the owner of the right, as when the taker of the guarantor is the agent of the creditor; then he is discharged by delivering him to the principal absolutely, and to the agent if he referred it to himself, but if he referred it to his principal, he is not discharged by delivering him to the agent because he is a messenger. Likewise, if the judge takes from the defendant a guarantor for the person, at the request of the plaintiff or without his request, and the guarantor delivers him to the judge, he is discharged. If he delivers him to the plaintiff, he is not discharged, this is when the judge does not refer it. If he refers it and the judge says: The plaintiff asks for a guarantor from you for the person, so give him a guarantor for yourself, and the guarantor delivers him to the judge, he is not discharged; if he delivers him to the plaintiff, he is discharged. And the judge's messenger and his trusted deputy are like the judge. And if one guarantees in person to the guardian, and then delivers him to the heirs or the creditor, he is not discharged. And in al-Qunyah: A man guaranteed in person on condition that he would deliver him to the guaranteed-for whenever he demands him, then he delivered him before he demanded him and he did not accept him, he is discharged because the ruling of guarantee is the obligation of delivery, and it is established at present. And his saying 'on condition that he delivers him to him whenever he demands him' is mentioned for emphasis, not for condition; so he delivered him while he was a guarantor, so he is discharged. They mentioned this issue, even though it is obvious, as Abū al-Layth said, to refute the misconception that the guarantor must deliver him repeatedly until his right is fulfilled. Because guarantee is intended only for reassurance for the fulfillment of the right, so as long as it is not fulfilled, he must deliver him until it is fulfilled. He removed this misconception by clarifying that the contract of guarantee requires delivery once, not with the condition of repetition. Thus in Fatḥ al-Qadīr.
Source pages p. 355

Commentary
This passage explains that the kafīl (guarantor) is discharged by simply delivering the maṭlūb (the person whose appearance is guaranteed) to the ṭālib (the claimant). The cause of discharge is the delivery itself; no explicit wording of discharge is needed. The commentator draws analogies with a debtor paying a debt, a usurper returning stolen property, and a seller delivering the sold item. The rule applies whether the kafīl explicitly states that he is delivering in his capacity as surety or not, provided the ṭālib demanded delivery. If the ṭālib did not demand, the kafīl must mention the purpose. If the ṭālib acknowledges receipt, discharge occurs even without explicit statement. If the ṭālib refuses to accept, he is compelled to do so, and the kafīl is considered to have performed his duty. A stranger (fūḍūlī) cannot compel acceptance. The ruling is extended to delivery to a representative: if the ṭālib's agent took the surety, delivering to the principal (the ṭālib) always discharges; delivering to the agent only discharges if the agent referred the bailment to himself. Delivery to a judge discharges, unless the judge specified that the surety is for the claimant, in which case only delivery to the claimant discharges. The judge's messenger and deputy have the same status. If the guarantee was made to a guardian (waṣī), delivering to the heirs or other creditors does not discharge. A crucial point is that the obligation to deliver arises immediately and is fulfilled by a single delivery. There is a misconception that the kafīl might have to deliver repeatedly until the claimant's right (e.g., a debt) is fully satisfied; the text clarifies that the guarantee contract only requires one delivery, not repeated ones. The purpose of kafāla is reassurance, so once the person is available for the claimant to proceed with his claim, the kafīl's duty is done.

Key Terms
وكيل (wakīl): Agent or deputy of the claimant; his capacity determines if delivery to him discharges.
قاضي (qāḍī): Judge; delivery to him may discharge depending on the situation.
وصي (waṣī): Guardian or executor of a will; delivery to him must be to him personally.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If the judge took a guarantee from the kafīl without the plaintiff's request, and the kafīl delivers the person to the plaintiff, what is the ruling?
A) The kafīl is discharged because the plaintiff is the rightful recipient
B) The kafīl is not discharged because delivery must be to the judge
C) The kafīl is discharged if the plaintiff accepts
D) The kafīl is not discharged unless the judge ratifies

Key Takeaway
The single-delivery principle applies across all cases: one correct delivery discharges the kafīl, but the correct recipient varies—agent, judge, or guardian—depending on the capacity in which they took the guarantee.

Discharge by Self-Surrender or Agent Delivery in Kafālah
Commentary on discharge via self-surrender or representative delivery

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which the kafīl is discharged by the maṭlūb's self-surrender or by delivery of his agent, and distinguish between kafāla bi-amrih and bi-ghayri amrih.

Core Idea
Self-surrender discharges the guarantor only if explicitly for the guarantee; without the maṭlūb's request, he may flee without sin.

The previous lesson covered how the kafīl can be discharged by performing the obligation himself or by the maṭlūb's death. This lesson expands on two additional modes: the maṭlūb voluntarily surrendering himself, and the kafīl's agent or messenger delivering him. The details reveal important conditions and a key distinction regarding the maṭlūb's obligation to cooperate.

Guided Path
1
Identify the modes of discharge
From the passage, determine the two additional ways the kafīl can be discharged: by the maṭlūb surrendering himself, or by the delivery of the kafīl's agent/messenger. Understand that these are extensions of the basic principle that fulfilling the purpose (presence of the maṭlūb) ends the guarantee.
2
Extract the condition of explicit intent
Note that the surrender must be explicitly 'from the guarantee' (min kafālatihi). Without this expression, the discharge does not occur. This applies both to self-surrender and to delivery by an agent.
The text mentions that if the debtor pays to the kafīl without mentioning the guarantee, it is considered a general repayment, not a discharge of the kafāla.
3
Distinguish kafāla bi-amrih vs bi-ghayri amrih
Recognize the critical distinction: if the kafāla was at the maṭlūb's request (bi-amrih), he is obliged to surrender and can be compelled. If without his order, he is not obliged and may flee without sin. This distinction affects other rulings like travel restrictions.
The commentator reconciles a seeming contradiction between sources by noting this distinction.
4
Address delivery by a stranger without authorization
If an unrelated person delivers the maṭlūb to the claimant without the kafīl's order, the discharge is suspended (muwaqqaf) on the claimant's acceptance. If the claimant accepts, the kafīl is discharged; if he remains silent, not.
This is analogous to the stranger acting as an unauthorized agent.
5
Extend to sub-guarantors and deputies
The passage also notes that if the kafīl of the kafīl (a sub-guarantor) delivers the maṭlūb, the original kafīl is discharged. Similarly, an explicit mention of 'deputy' (nā'ib) is recommended for clarity.
This shows the principle that the act of a representative is like the act of the principal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وبتسليم المطلوب نفسه كفالته من وبتسليم وكيل الكفيل ورسوله أي يبرأ الكفيل بتسليم هؤلاء لأن المطلوب يطالب بتسليم نفسه فإذا سلم نفسه حصل المقصود فلا معنى لبقائها كالمحيل إذا قضى الدين بنفسه فإنه يصح قبل الطالب أولاً، وفعل نائب الكفيل كفعله . وقيد بقوله من كفالته لأنه لا يبرأ الكفيل حتى يقول المكفول سلمت نفسي إليك الكفالة ولو أخر قوله من الكفالة لكان أولى لأن الوكيل والرسول كالمكفول لا بد من التسليم عنها وإلا لا يبرأ . وقيد بتسليم النفس لأن المديون لو دفع الدين إلى الكفيل قبل أن يوفي عنه ولم يقل إنه عن كفالتك كان قضاء لأنه الغالب وتستحق عليه فانصرف إليه كذا في القنية. وقيد بالوكيل والرسول لأنه لو سلمه أجنبي بغير أمر الكفيل وقال سلمت إليك عن الكفيل وقف على قبوله فإن قبله الطالب برئ الكفيل وإن سكت لا. وفي السراج الوهاج : : ولو سلم المكفول بالنفس نفسه إلى المكفول له بجهة الكفالة فإنه يجبر على القبول حتى يبرأ الكفيل وهذا إذا كانت الكفالة بالأمر، أما إذا كانت بغير الأمر لا يبرأ؛ كذا في الفوائد اهـ. ولم يظهر هذا التفصيل ثم ظهر لي أن المراد أمر المطلوب وأن الكفالة بالنفس على وجهين : إما أن تكون بأمر المطلوب أو بغير أمره لما في التتارخانية : ولو كفل بنفسه بغير أمره فلا مطالبة للكفيل عليه إلا أن يجده فيسلمه فيبرأ .اهـ. فعلى هذا إذا ضمنه بغير أمره لا يأثم بعدم التمكين منه فله الهرب بخلاف ما إذا كان بأمره، وعلى هذا فما قدمناه من منعه من السفر إنما هو فيما إذا كانت بأمره، وزاد في الإصلاح على رسوله إليه. وقال في الإيضاح : وإنما قال إليه لأن رسوله إلى غيره كالأجنبي .اهـ. وفي التتارخانية : يشترط التسليم عن الكفالة ولا يحتاج أن يقول عن كفالة فلان إنما يحتاج تعيينه إذا كان كفله لرجلين، ولو قال بعد قوله ورسوله وكفيله لكان أولى لأن كفيل الكفيل لو سلمه بری كما في التتارخانية، فلو قال وبتسليم نائب الكفيل عنه لكان أحسن
He said: (And by the surrender of the demanded person himself, from his guarantee, and by the delivery of the guarantor's agent and messenger) meaning: The guarantor is discharged by the delivery of these persons, because the demanded person is required to surrender himself, so if he surrenders himself, the purpose is achieved, so there is no point in its remaining, like the transferor (muḥīl) when he pays the debt himself, then it is valid before the claimant first. And the action of the guarantor's deputy is like his own action. And he restricted by his saying 'from his guarantee', because the guarantor is not discharged until the guaranteed person says: I have surrendered myself to you for the guarantee. And if he delayed his saying 'from the guarantee', it would be better, because the agent and messenger are like the guaranteed person, delivery must be from the guarantee, otherwise he is not discharged. And he restricted by the surrender of the person, because if the debtor paid the debt to the guarantor before he paid off on his behalf and did not say it is for your guarantee, it is considered repayment because it is the norm, and it becomes due on him, so he is discharged by it. Thus in al-Qunyah. And he restricted by the agent and messenger, because if a stranger delivers him without the order of the guarantor and says: I have delivered to you on behalf of the guarantor, it is suspended on acceptance. If the claimant accepts, the guarantor is discharged; if he remains silent, not. And in al-Sirāj al-Wahhāj: And if the guaranteed person for the person delivers himself to the guaranteed-for by way of guarantee, then he is compelled to accept until the guarantor is discharged. This is if the guarantee was by order. But if it was without order, he is not discharged. Thus in al-Fawā'id. And this detail was not apparent to me, then it became apparent to me that what is meant is the order of the demanded person, and that guarantee for the person has two types: either it is by order of the demanded person or without his order. Because in al-Tātārkhāniyya: And if one guarantees in person without his order, there is no claim for the guarantor against him except that he finds him and delivers him, then he is discharged. So according to this, if he guarantees him without his order, he does not sin by not enabling himself, so he may flee, unlike if it was by his order. And accordingly, what we have mentioned about his prevention from travel is only when it was by his order. And he added in al-Iṣlāḥ 'on his messenger to him'. And he said in al-Īḍāḥ: He only said 'to him' because his messenger to someone else is like a stranger. And in al-Tātārkhāniyya: Delivery must be from the guarantee, and he does not need to say 'from the guarantee of so-and-so'; he only needs specification if he was a guarantor for two men. And if he said after his saying 'and his messenger' and his guarantor, it would be better, because the guarantor of the guarantor, if he delivers him, he is discharged. As in al-Tātārkhāniyya, if he said 'and by the delivery of the deputy of the guarantor on his behalf', it would be best.
Source pages p. 356

Commentary
This lesson covers additional means by which the kafīl can be discharged: (1) the maṭlūb (the person whose appearance is guaranteed) voluntarily surrenders himself, (2) the kafīl's agent (wakīl) or messenger (rasūl) delivers him. The key point is that the maṭlūb must explicitly surrender himself 'for the guarantee' (min kafālatihi); otherwise the kafīl is not discharged. The agent's act is equivalent to the kafīl's own act. If a stranger delivers without the kafīl's authorization, the discharge is suspended upon the claimant's acceptance. A crucial distinction is made: if the kafāla was at the maṭlūb's request (bi-amrih), the maṭlūb is obliged to surrender; if without his request, the kafīl has no right to demand his surrender, and the maṭlūb may even flee without sin. This distinction also affects other rulings, such as whether the maṭlūb can be prevented from traveling. The text also emphasizes that delivery must be specifically from the kafāla (i.e., in fulfillment of the guarantee). If the kafīl guaranteed for two different persons, he must specify for which one he is delivering. If the kafīl of the kafīl (i.e., a sub-guarantor) delivers, he is discharged, and it is better if the phrase explicitly includes 'the deputy of the guarantor'.

Key Terms
تسليم النفس (taslīm al-nafs): The voluntary surrender of the guaranteed person to the claimant, fulfilling the guarantee obligation.
وكيل الكفيل (wakīl al-kafīl): The agent appointed by the guarantor to deliver the guaranteed person on his behalf.
بأمره (bi-amrih): With the order/request of the maṭlūb; the guarantee was made at his behest.
بغير أمره (bi-ghayri amrih): Without the order/request of the maṭlūb; the guarantee was made voluntarily by the kafīl.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Yes, because he is responsible for producing him
B) No, because the guarantee was without order
C) Yes, because fleeing invalidates the guarantee
D) No, only if the claimant catches him

Key Takeaway
Self-surrender discharges the kafīl only if explicitly for the guarantee; the maṭlūb's obligation to cooperate depends on whether he requested the guarantee.
