Conditional Clause in Person-Guarantee: From Kafīl to Ḍāmin
Introduction to a conditional clause in kafala bi'l-nafs

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a conditional clause in kafala bi'l-nafs and explain when it converts the guarantee into property liability.

Core Idea
A conditional clause can turn person-guarantee into property liability, but only if the original kafala was at the request of the guaranteed person.

Kafala bi'l-nafs and kafala bi'l-māl are distinct in Hanafi fiqh. But what happens when the guarantor adds a condition linking the two? This lesson examines a specific clause: 'If I do not bring him tomorrow, I am liable for the property.' We will see that the Hanafi school holds that this clause is effective only in certain contexts—a nuance that protects both the guarantor and the creditor.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Conditional Clause
Read the clause: 'If I do not bring him tomorrow, then he is liable for what I guaranteed with himself.' Note that it combines a personal guarantee (kafala bi'l-nafs) with a property liability (kafala bi'l-māl).
The clause is clearly conditional: the property liability is triggered only if the person is not produced.
2
Distinguish Kafala bi'l-Nafs and Kafala bi'l-Māl
Recall that kafala bi'l-nafs is a guarantee of the person's presence, while kafala bi'l-māl is a guarantee of a debt. They have different conditions and effects.
A single contract can be structured as either, but the Hanafi school treats them separately.
3
Understand the Legal Effect of the Condition
The clause transforms the nature of the contract: the kafīl becomes a ḍāmin if the condition is fulfilled (i.e., he fails to bring the person). However, the text hints that this transformation depends on whether the original kafala was bi-amrih (at the request of the maṭlūb) or not.
The passage references 'the order of the demanded' as a key factor.
4
Note the Nuance: Request-Based Distinction
When the original kafala bi'l-nafs was undertaken at the request of the person being guaranteed (maṭlūb), the conditional clause is effective. Otherwise, the clause may not convert it into property liability. This protects against unilateral extensions of liability.
This nuance is derived from the reference to 'amr al-maṭlūb' in the text.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (فإن قال إن لم أواف به غداً فهو ضامن لما بكفلت بنفسه). قوله : (ثم ظهر لي أن المراد أمر المطلوب الخ) وعن هذا قال في النهر : والوجه فيه
He said: (If he says: If I do not bring him tomorrow, then he is liable for what I guaranteed with himself). He said: (Then it appeared to me that what is meant is the order of the demanded, etc.) And about this he said in al-Nahr: The aspect in it is...
Source pages p. 356

Commentary
This brief passage begins to address a specific clause: if the guarantor says 'If I do not bring the person tomorrow, I am liable (ḍāmin) for the property I guaranteed by his person' — i.e., a conditional conversion of kafāla bi'l-nafs into kafāla bi'l-māl. The commentator then notes that he came to understand that the key distinction is whether the original kafāla was at the request of the maṭlūb (bi-amrih) or not. He then references al-Nahr for further detail, but the text cuts off. This lesson is incomplete in the window.

Key Terms
ضامن (ḍāmin): Liable for property (in this context, by a conditional clause).
كفالة بالنفس (kafāla bi'l-nafs): Guarantee to produce a person; the guarantor (kafīl) is liable for the person's presence.
كفالة بالمال (kafāla bi'l-māl): Guarantee of a debt or property; the guarantor (ḍāmin) is liable for the debt.
أمر المطلوب (amr al-maṭlūb): The request of the person demanded (the principal).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

C) If I fail to produce him, I will pay a fine
D) If the debtor escapes, I am free

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, a conditional clause in a person-guarantee converts it into a property guarantee only if the original guarantee was at the request of the person being guaranteed.

Release of Guarantor by Delivery of Principal or Agent
Explains how guarantor is released by delivery of principal or agent

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which a guarantor (kafil) is released by delivery of the principal (matlub) or his agent, and apply the Hanafi rule that the contract requires a single delivery, not repeated deliveries.

Core Idea
A guarantor is released by a single delivery of the principal; repeated deliveries are never required.

In Hanafi fiqh, a guarantor (kafil) undertakes to produce the principal (matlub) when demanded. This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq explains exactly how that obligation is discharged—by whom the principal may be delivered, and what conditions must be met for the guarantor to be freed. The key point is that the contract of kafala requires only one delivery, correcting a common misunderstanding that the guarantor must keep delivering until the debt is paid.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main ruling
Read the opening sentence: the guarantor is released by delivery of the principal himself, or by delivery of the guarantor's agent or messenger. Note the phrase 'from his guarantee' (min kafalatihi) which qualifies the release.
2
Understand the rebuttal of a misunderstanding
The author explains why this issue is discussed despite its apparent clarity: some might mistakenly think the guarantor must deliver the principal repeatedly until the debt is satisfied. The contract, however, necessitates a single delivery only. This is supported by citing Fath al-Qadir.
3
Trace the qiyas analogy to al-muhil
The commentary draws an analogy (qiyas) to the case of a referrer (al-muhil) in hawala (transfer of debt). When the referrer pays the debt himself, he is released. Similarly, when the principal delivers himself, the guarantor is released because the purpose is achieved.
This is a jaliy (clear) qiyas.
4
Distinguish the two types of kafala bi al-amr and bighayr al-amr
Later, the passage distinguishes between kafala with the principal's command and without. If the guarantee was by the principal's command, the guarantor can prevent the principal from traveling; if without, the principal may flee and the guarantor does not sin. This affects release conditions.
5
Apply the rule to agent/messenger delivery and stranger delivery
If the guarantor's agent or messenger delivers, it is like the guarantor himself delivering. If a stranger (ajnabi) delivers without the guarantor's order, the release is contingent upon the claimant's acceptance. If the claimant accepts, the guarantor is freed; if silent, not.
Also note that the delivery must be explicitly 'from the guarantee' otherwise it may be considered repayment of debt.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بريء وبتسليم المطلوب نفسه من كفالته وبتسليم وكيل الكفيل ورسوله. يسلمه إلى المكفول له متى طالبه به ثم سلمه إليه قبل أن يطالبه ولم يقبله يبرأ لأن حكم الكفالة وجوب التسليم وهو ثابت في الحال. وقوله على أن يسلمه إليه متى طالبه به يذكر للتأكيد لا للتعليق فقد سلمه إليه حال كونه كفيلاً فيبرأ. وإنما ذكروا هذه المسألة أعني مسألة مع ظهورها كما قاله الفقيه أبو الليث لدفع توهم أنه يلزم الكفيل تسليمه مرة بعد مرة إلى أن يستوفي حقه لأن الكفالة ما أريدت إلا للتوثق لاستيفاء الحق، فما لم يستوفه يجب عليه تسليمه إلى أن يستوفيه فأزال هذا الوهم ببيان أن عقد الكفالة يوجب التسليم مرة لا بقيد التكرار؛ كذا في فتح القدير.
قوله: (وبتسليم المطلوب نفسه كفالته من وبتسليم وكيل الكفيل ورسوله) أي يبرأ الكفيل بتسليم هؤلاء لأن المطلوب يطالب بتسليم نفسه فإذا سلم نفسه حصل المقصود فلا معنى لبقائها كالمحيل إذا قضى الدين بنفسه فإنه يصح قبل الطالب أولاً، وفعل نائب الكفيل كفعله. وقيد بقوله من كفالته لأنه لا يبرأ الكفيل حتى يقول المكفول سلمت نفسي إليك الكفالة ولو أخر قوله من الكفالة لكان أولى لأن الوكيل والرسول كالمكفول لا بد من التسليم عنها وإلا لا يبرأ. وقيد بتسليم النفس لأن المديون لو دفع الدين إلى الكفيل قبل أن يوفي عنه ولم يقل إنه عن كفالتك كان قضاء لأنه الغالب وتستحق عليه فانصرف إليه كذا في القنية. وقيد بالوكيل والرسول لأنه لو سلمه أجنبي بغير أمر الكفيل وقال سلمت إليك عن الكفيل وقف على قبوله فإن قبله الطالب برئ الكفيل وإن سكت لا. وفي السراج الوهاج: ولو سلم المكفول بالنفس نفسه إلى المكفول له بجهة الكفالة فإنه يجبر على القبول حتى يبرأ الكفيل وهذا إذا كانت الكفالة بالأمر، أما إذا كانت بغير الأمر لا يبرأ؛ كذا في الفوائد. ولم يظهر هذا التفصيل ثم ظهر لي أن المراد أمر المطلوب وأن الكفالة بالنفس على وجهين: إما أن تكون بأمر المطلوب أو بغير أمره لما في التتارخانية: ولو كفل بنفسه بغير أمره فلا مطالبة للكفيل عليه إلا أن يجده فيسلمه فيبرأ. فعلى هذا إذا ضمنه بغير أمره لا يأثم بعدم التمكين منه فله الهرب بخلاف ما إذا كان بأمره، وعلى هذا فما قدمناه من منعه من السفر إنما هو فيما إذا كانت بأمره، وزاد في الإصلاح على رسوله إليه. وقال في الإيضاح: وإنما قال إليه لأن رسوله إلى غيره كالأجنبي. وفي التتارخانية: يشترط التسليم عن الكفالة ولا يحتاج أن يقول عن كفالة فلان إنما يحتاج تعيينه إذا كان كفله لرجلين، ولو قال بعد قوله ورسوله وكفيله لكان أولى لأن كفيل الكفيل لو سلمه برئ كما في التتارخانية، فلو قال وبتسليم نائب الكفيل عنه لكان أحسن.
He is released by the delivery of the demanded person himself from his guarantee, and by the delivery of the agent of the guarantor and his messenger. He delivers him to the one in whose favor the guarantee is given whenever he demands him, then he delivered him to him before he demanded him and he did not accept him, he is released because the ruling of guarantee is the obligation of delivery, and it is established at the moment. And his statement 'on condition that he deliver him to him whenever he demands him' is mentioned for emphasis, not for conditioning; for he delivered him to him while being a guarantor, so he is released. And they only mentioned this issue, despite its clarity as Abu al-Layth said, to repel the misunderstanding that the guarantor is obligated to deliver him repeatedly until his right is fulfilled, because guarantee is only meant for security for the recovery of the right; so as long as he has not recovered it, he must deliver him until he recovers it. So he removed this misunderstanding by clarifying that the contract of guarantee necessitates delivery once, not repeated. So it is in Fath al-Qadir. His statement: (And by the delivery of the demanded person himself from his guarantee and by the delivery of the agent of the guarantor and his messenger) means the guarantor is released by the delivery of these because the demanded person is required to deliver himself; if he delivers himself the purpose is achieved, so there is no meaning for its continuation, like the referrer (al-muhil) when he pays the debt himself, it is valid before the claimant first, and the act of the deputy of the guarantor is like his act. And he restricted by his statement 'from his guarantee' because the guarantor is not released until the guaranteed person says 'I have delivered myself to you from the guarantee'; and if he delayed his saying 'from the guarantee' it would be better because the agent and messenger are like the guaranteed person; delivery must be from them, otherwise he is not released. And he restricted by delivery of the person because if the debtor paid the debt to the guarantor before he paid on his behalf and did not say that it is for your guarantee, it is considered repayment because it is usual and due upon him, so it is directed to that; as in al-Qunyah. And he restricted by the agent and messenger because if a stranger delivered him without the command of the guarantor and said 'I delivered to you on behalf of the guarantor', it is contingent upon acceptance; if the claimant accepts, the guarantor is released; if he remains silent, no. In al-Siraj al-Wahhaj: If the guaranteed person delivered himself to the one in whose favor the guarantee is given with the intention of guarantee, he is forced to accept so that the guarantor is released; this is if the guarantee is by command; as for without command, he is not released; so in al-Fawa'id. And this detail was not clear to me, then it became clear to me that what is meant is the command of the demanded person, and that the guarantee of the person is of two types: either by command of the demanded person or without his command; as in al-Tatarkhaniyyah: If he guaranteed a person without his command, there is no claim for the guarantor against him unless he finds him and delivers him, then he is released. Upon this, if he guarantees him without his command, he does not sin by not enabling him, so he may flee, unlike if it was by his command. And upon this, what we have mentioned of preventing him from travel is only when it is by his command. And he added in al-Islah 'to his messenger'. And he said in al-Idah: He only said 'to him' because his messenger to someone else is like a stranger. In al-Tatarkhaniyyah: Delivery from the guarantee is required, and he does not need to say 'from the guarantee of so-and-so'; he only needs to specify if he guaranteed for two men. And if he said after his statement 'and his messenger and his guarantor' it would be better because the guarantor of the guarantor if he delivers him, he is released; as in al-Tatarkhaniyyah. So if he said 'and by the delivery of the deputy of the guarantor on his behalf' it would be nicer.
Source pages p. 356

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement from the commentary explaining how a guarantor (kafil) is released from his obligation. The core ruling is that the guarantor is released by the principal (al-matlub) delivering himself, or by the agent or messenger of the guarantor delivering him. The commentary clarifies that the contract of kafala obligates a single delivery, not repeated deliveries until the debt is paid, correcting a potential misunderstanding. It then delves into details: the guarantor is not released until the principal explicitly states he is delivering himself 'from the guarantee'. If the principal pays the debt to the guarantor without specifying it is for the guarantee, it is treated as repayment of the debt. If a stranger delivers the principal without the guarantor's order, it is contingent on the claimant's acceptance. A key distinction is made between kafala bi al-amr (by the order of the principal) and kafala bighayr al-amr (without his order). In the former, the guarantor can prevent the principal from traveling; in the latter, the principal may flee and the guarantor does not sin. The passage also discusses the requirement of delivery being explicitly from the guarantee and the specification of the beneficiary if multiple.

Key Terms
كفالة (kafala): A contract of guarantee where one person (kafil) undertakes to produce another (makful) or pay a debt.
مطلوب (matlub): The principal debtor or the person whose appearance is guaranteed.
تسليم (taslim): Delivery of the principal to the claimant, which releases the guarantor.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When a stranger (not the agent of the guarantor) delivers the principal to the claimant saying 'I deliver to you on behalf of the guarantor', what happens?
A) The guarantor is immediately released.
B) The delivery is contingent on the claimant's acceptance; if he accepts, the guarantor is released.
C) The guarantor is not released under any circumstance.
D) The guarantor must also agree for release.

Key Takeaway
A guarantor is released by a single delivery of the principal or his agent, provided the delivery is explicitly from the guarantee; the contract does not require repeated deliveries.

Conditional Guarantee: Liability and Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Expansion on conditional guarantee with intra-Hanafi dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rules of conditional guarantee to determine when the guarantor becomes liable for the debt, and identify the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the appointment of a proxy when the claimant absents.

Core Idea
A conditional guarantee binds upon non-fulfillment; debt remains even if the principal dies; in absence, judge may appoint a proxy per later Hanafis.

In this lesson, we delve into the Hanafi treatment of suspended guarantees (ta'liq). The guarantor pledges to produce the principal by a deadline or else be liable for the debt. We examine the conditions that trigger liability, exceptions, and a notable dispute within the school about the role of the judge when the claimant absents. This material is from the commentary of Al-Bahr al-Raiq on Kanz al-Daqaiq.

Guided Path
1
Understand the validity of suspending a guarantee
The Hanafi school permits ta'liq in kafala based on custom (urf). The conditional statement is valid and creates a suspended obligation.
Custom (urf) is a recognized source in Hanafi fiqh for such transactions.
2
Identify when the debt becomes obligatory
When the condition (non-fulfillment) occurs—i.e., the principal is not produced or dies—the guarantor becomes liable for the debt.
Death or insanity of the principal is treated as non-fulfillment.
3
Clarify the effect of death and inability
Death of the principal triggers liability; death of the guarantor does not because his heir stands in his place. Inability of the guarantor (unless due to principal's death/insanity) prevents liability.
4
Resolve disputes over fulfillment
If the guarantor claims he brought the principal and the claimant denies, the claimant's word is taken, and the guarantor must prove fulfillment.
No oath is required from either party in such disputes.
5
Examine the intra-Hanafi dispute on proxy appointment
When the claimant absents after the guarantor brings the principal to the stipulated place, early scholars held the guarantor liable. Abu Yusuf and later scholars hold that the judge should appoint a proxy to accept delivery, especially if the claimant is obstinate.
Qadikhan adopted the later scholars' view as mufta bihi.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قال إن لم أواف به غداً فهو ضامن لما عليه فلم يواف به أو مات المطلوب ضمن المال ومن ادعى على آخر مائة يجب عليه فلم يواف به أو مات المطلوب ضمن المال لأن الكفالة بالمال معلقة بشرط عدم الموافاة وهو متعارف يصح تعليقها به فإذا وجد الشرط لزمه المال ولا يبرأ عن كفالة النفس لأنها كانت ثابتة قبلها ولا تنافيها كما لو كفلهما جملة. والمراد بقوله ولم يواف به مع قدرته عليه فإن عجز لا يلزمه إلا إذا عجز بموت المطلوب أو جنونه وموت المطلوب وإن أبطل الكفالة بالنفس فإنما هو في حق تسليمه إلى الطالب لا في حق المال وقيد بموت المطلوب لأن الكفيل لو مات لم يوجد شرط الكفالة المعلقة لأن وارثه يقوم مقامه كموت الطالب فإن الكفيل إذا سلمه إلى وارثه برئ، ولو أبرأه الطالب عن كفالة النفس فلم يواف به لا المال لفقد شرطه، ولو اختلف فقال الكفيل وافيتك به وقال الطالب لم توافني به فالقول للطالب والمال لازم على الكفيل لأن سبب وجوب المال التزام المال بالكفالة إلا أن الموافاة شرط للبراءة فلا يثبت بقول الكفيل؛ كذا في الخانية. وفي البزازية وفيما إذا علق المال بعدم الموافاة لا يصدق الكفيل على الموافاة إلا بحجة وبيانه ما ذكره في نظم الفقه: قال الكفيل دفعته إليك اليوم المشروط وأنكره الطالب فالأمر على ما كان في الابتداء ولا يمين على واحد منهما لأن كلاً منهما مدع الكفيل البراءة والطالب الوجوب ولا يمين على المدعي عندنا اهـ. وفي فروق الكرابيسي: رجل كفل بنفس رجل على أنه إن لم يواف به غداً فعليه المال فلم يوافه لكن المدعي وجد المدعى عليه ولازمه حتى الليل يلزمه المال وكذا لو تغيب الطالب فلم يجده لزمه المال هنا فصول: الثاني لو شرط على الكفيل مكاناً فجاء الكفيل بالمكفول به في ذلك المكان وتغيب الطالب لزم المال الكفيل الثالث لو اشترى بالخيار فتوارى البائع الرابع حلف ليقضين دينه اليوم فتغيب الدائن الخامس جعل أمر امرأته بيدها إن لم تصل نفقتها فتغيبت، وعند أبي يوسف ينصب القاضي قيماً في الفصلين الأخيرين لا في الأول لأن الطالب متعنت فيهما لا في الأول اهـ.
المصنف وفي الخلاصة: إذا توارى الطالب والبائع نصب القاضي وكيلاً عن الغائب قال الفقيه أبو الليث: هذا خلاف قول أصحابنا وإنما روي في بعض الروايات عن أبي يوسف ولو فعل القاضي فهو حسن. وجعل قاضيخان في فتاواه المسائل كلها على الخلاف وأن القاضي ينصب وكيلاً عن الغائب على قول المتأخرين وهو قول أبي يوسف ا هـ. ولم يصور المسألة بالألف كما في الهداية لبيان أن معلومية القدر ليست شرطاً لصحتها فإذا قال بما عليه فمهما ثبت بالبينة أنه عليه لزمه كما سيأتي؛ كذا في النهاية وكذلك لو قال الكفيل بالنفس إن لم أوافك به غداً فعلى ما أقر به المطلوب فلم يواف به غداً فأقر المطلوب أن له عليه ظاهر لأنها إذا كانت بغير أمره لا يلزم المطلوب بالحضور فليس مطالباً بالتسليم فإذا سلمه نفسه لا يبرأ الكفيل.
If he says: 'If I do not bring him tomorrow, I am a guarantor for what is upon him,' then he did not bring him, or the demanded one died, he guarantees the property. And whoever claims against another a hundred that is due upon him, and he did not bring him, or the demanded one died, he guarantees the property because the guarantee of property is suspended on the condition of non-fulfillment, and it is customary, so its suspension upon it is valid. So when the condition occurs, the property becomes obligatory upon him, and he is not released from the guarantee of the person because it was established before it and does not contradict it, just as if he guaranteed both together. And what is meant by his statement 'and he did not bring him' is while he has the ability to do so; if he is unable, it does not become obligatory, unless the inability is due to the death or insanity of the demanded one. And the death of the demanded one, although it nullifies the guarantee of the person, is only with regard to his delivery to the claimant, not with regard to the property. And he restricted it by the death of the demanded one because if the guarantor dies, the condition of the suspended guarantee does not occur because his heir stands in his place; like the death of the claimant, for if the guarantor delivers him to his heir, he is released. And if the claimant releases him from the guarantee of the person, and he did not bring him, the property is not [obligatory] because its condition is lost. And if they dispute, the guarantor saying 'I brought him to you' and the claimant saying 'you did not bring him to me', the saying is the claimant's, and the property is binding upon the guarantor because the cause of the obligation of property is the undertaking of property by the guarantee, except that the bringing is a condition for release, so it is not established by the guarantor's statement. So in al-Khaniyyah. And in al-Bazzaziyyah: When he suspends the property on non-fulfillment, the guarantor is not believed regarding the fulfillment except with evidence; and its explanation is what is mentioned in Nazm al-Fiqh: The guarantor said 'I paid it to you on the stipulated day' and the claimant denied it, the matter remains as it was initially, and there is no oath on either of them because each is a claimant: the guarantor claims release and the claimant claims obligation, and there is no oath on the claimant according to us. In Furuq al-Karabisi: A man guaranteed the person of a man on the condition that if he does not bring him tomorrow, then upon him is the property. He did not bring him, but the claimant found the defendant and accompanied him until night, so the property becomes obligatory on him. Likewise, if the claimant absented himself and he did not find him, the property becomes obligatory on him. Here are sections: The second: If he stipulated a place on the guarantor, and the guarantor brought the guaranteed person to that place, and the claimant absented himself, the property becomes obligatory on the guarantor. The third: If he bought with an option and the seller hid. The fourth: He swore to pay his debt today and the creditor absented himself. The fifth: He placed the affair of his wife in her hand if her maintenance is not paid, and she absented herself. And according to Abu Yusuf, the judge appoints a representative in the last two sections, not in the first, because the claimant is obstinate in the last two, not in the first. The author [says]: In al-Khulasah: If the claimant and the seller hide, the judge appoints a proxy for the absent one. Al-Faqih Abu al-Layth said: This contradicts the statement of our companions, and it is only narrated in some transmissions from Abu Yusuf, and if the judge does it, it is good. And Qadikhan in his Fatawa made all the issues on the difference, and that the judge appoints a proxy for the absent one according to the later scholars, which is the statement of Abu Yusuf. And he did not formulate the issue with 'alif' as in al-Hidayah to show that the knowledge of the amount is not a condition for its validity; so if he says 'for what is upon him,' whatever is established by evidence that is upon him becomes obligatory, as will come. So in al-Nihayah. And likewise if the guarantor of the person says 'If I do not bring you to him tomorrow, then upon me is what the demanded one confessed,' and he did not bring him tomorrow, and the demanded one confessed that upon him is [a debt] apparently, because if it is without his command, the demanded one is not required to attend, so he is not obligated to deliver; so if he delivers himself, the guarantor is not released.
Source pages p. 357

Commentary
This passage discusses a conditional guarantee (kafala mu'allaqa) where the guarantor says 'If I do not bring the principal tomorrow, I guarantee what is upon him.' The ruling is that if the condition is met (the principal is not produced or dies), the guarantor becomes liable for the debt. The text explains that this is valid because it is customary to suspend a guarantee on such a condition. The debt obligation does not nullify the prior guarantee of the person; both can coexist. The condition of non-fulfillment is interpreted as being within the guarantor's ability; if he is unable due to death or insanity of the principal, the debt is still owed. If the principal dies, the guarantee of the person is void regarding delivery, but the debt obligation remains. If the guarantor dies, his heir takes his place. If the claimant releases the guarantor from the personal guarantee, the conditional debt is not triggered. In case of dispute, the claimant's word is taken over the guarantor's regarding fulfillment. The text then discusses various scenarios (furus) where the claimant absents himself or conditions are not met, and mentions a difference among Hanafi scholars: Abu Yusuf holds that the judge should appoint a representative in certain cases of the claimant's absence, while other early scholars disagree. Qadikhan adopts the view of the later scholars including Abu Yusuf. Finally, the text notes that the amount of the debt need not be specified if the guarantor says 'what is upon him'; it can be proven later.

Key Terms
موافاة (muwafah): The act of bringing or producing the principal (mukful) to the claimant as promised.
تعليق (ta'liq): Suspending the obligation of the guarantee on a condition (e.g., non-fulfillment).
متعلق (muta'alliq): That which is suspended; here the debt obligation is mu'allaq (suspended) on the condition.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If the guarantor says, 'If I do not bring him tomorrow, I guarantee what is upon him,' and the principal dies before tomorrow, what is the ruling?
A) The guarantor is released from all obligations.
B) The guarantor must pay the debt because the condition is met.
C) The guarantor only pays if he was able to bring him.
D) The guarantor must bring the corpse to the claimant.

Key Takeaway
A conditional guarantee binds the guarantor to the debt upon non-fulfillment, and the Hanafi school (later view) allows the judge to appoint a proxy in cases of claimant obstinacy.

Conditional Kafalah: Surety for Person with Promise to Pay Debt
Explaining the ruling of kafalah bi al-nafs with conditional promise to pay debt

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on conditional kafalah bi al-nafs and identify when the surety becomes liable for the debt.

Core Idea
A conditional promise to pay the debt upon failure to produce the principal is valid by custom, and liability arises upon the condition's occurrence.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Raiq explains a combined guarantee: a person stands surety for another's presence (kafalah bi al-nafs) and also promises to pay the debt if he fails to bring him. The Hanafi school validates this conditional obligation based on customary practice (urf). The lesson examines the conditions, exceptions, and procedural rule in case of dispute.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two obligations
Recognize that the surety has two commitments: person-surety (kafalah bi al-nafs) and a conditional money-surety (kafalah bi al-mal) that activates upon failure to produce the principal.
The money-surety is conditional on 'adam al-muwafah' (non-fulfillment).
2
Understand the condition
The condition for money liability is that the surety fails to bring the principal despite ability. Inability due to death or insanity of the principal still triggers liability.
Other forms of inability (e.g., principal flees) may excuse if beyond surety's control? The text only mentions death/insanity as exceptions.
3
Note the effect of the surety's own death or release
If the surety dies, his heir can fulfill the person-surety. If the claimant releases the surety from person-surety before the condition occurs, the conditional money-surety is also canceled.
4
Apply the procedural rule in dispute
If the surety claims he brought the principal and the claimant denies, the claimant's word is taken, and the surety must pay the debt because the obligation is established by the undertaking, and fulfillment is a condition for discharge that the surety cannot prove by his mere claim.
This follows the principle that the claimant's denial is credible regarding non-fulfillment.
5
Summarize the full chain
Undertaking → conditional → condition occurs (failure/death/insanity) → money becomes wajib. Discharge only by proven fulfillment or release.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
به غداً فهو ضامن لما عليه فلم يواف به أو مات المطلوب ضمن المال ومن ادعى على آخر مائة يجب عليه فلم يواف به أو مات المطلوب ضمن المال لأن الكفالة بالمال معلقة بشرط عدم الموافاة وهو متعارف يصح تعليقها به فإذا وجد الشرط لزمه المال ولا يبرأ عن كفالة النفس لأنها كانت ثابتة قبلها ولا تنافيها كما لو كفلهما جملة . والمراد بقوله ولم يواف به» مع قدرته عليه فإن عجز لا يلزمه إلا إذا عجز بموت المطلوب أو جنونه وموت المطلوب وإن أبطل الكفالة بالنفس فإنما هو في حق تسليمه إلى الطالب لا في حق المال وقيد بموت المطلوب لأن الكفيل لو مات لم يوجد شرط الكفالة المعلقة لأن وارثه يقوم مقامه كموت الطالب فإن الكفيل إذا سلمه إلى وارثه برئ، ولو أبرأه الطالب عن كفالة النفس فلم يواف به لا المال لفقد شرطه، ولو اختلف فقال الكفيل وافيتك به وقال الطالب لم توافني به فالقول للطالب والمال لازم على الكفيل لأن سبب وجوب المال التزام المال بالكفالة إلا أن الموافاة شرط للبراءة فلا يثبت بقول الكفيل؛ كذا في الخانية.
If he does not bring him tomorrow, he is a guarantor for what is upon him. If he did not bring him or the sought person died, he guarantees the money. And whoever claims against another a hundred that is obligatory upon him, and he does not bring him or the sought person dies, he guarantees the money, because the guarantee for the money is suspended on the condition of non-fulfillment, and it is customary, so suspension upon it is valid. When the condition occurs, the money becomes binding upon him. And he is not freed from the guarantee of the person because it was established before it and does not contradict it, just as if he guaranteed both together. And the meaning of his saying 'and he did not bring him' is with his ability; if he is unable, it does not become binding except if he is unable due to the death of the sought person or his insanity. The death of the sought person, although it invalidates the guarantee of the person, is only regarding handing him over to the claimant, not regarding the money. And it was restricted to the death of the sought person because if the surety dies, the condition of the suspended guarantee does not occur because his heir takes his place, like the death of the claimant, for if the surety hands him over to his heir, he is freed. And if the claimant releases him from the guarantee of the person, and then he does not bring him, there is no money because its condition is lost. And if they dispute, the surety saying 'I brought him to you' and the claimant saying 'you did not bring him to me', the word is the claimant's, and the money is binding on the surety, because the cause of the obligation of money is the undertaking of money by the guarantee, except that fulfillment is a condition for discharge, so it cannot be established by the statement of the surety. This is in al-Khaniyah.
Source pages p. 357

Commentary
This passage is a ruling from the Hanafi school on kafalah bi al-nafs (surety for the person) combined with a conditional promise to pay the debt. The author explains that if a person stands surety for another's appearance with the condition that if he fails to bring him by a certain time, he will be responsible for the debt, then upon failure to bring him (or if the principal dies or goes insane), the surety becomes liable for the money. The key point is that this conditional liability is valid because it is based on custom (ta'aruf). The condition of non-fulfillment must be within the surety's ability; if he is unable due to the principal's death or insanity, liability still attaches, but other forms of inability are excused. The surety's own death does not trigger liability because his heir can fulfill. If the claimant releases the surety from the person-surety, the conditional money-surety is also nullified because its condition is gone. In case of dispute about fulfillment, the claimant's denial is believed, and the surety must pay, because the obligation arises from the undertaking, and fulfillment is a condition for discharge, which the surety cannot prove by his mere claim. This follows the principle that the claimant's word is taken in matters of debt and discharge.

Key Terms
الكفالة (al-kafalah): A contract of guarantee where a person (surety) becomes responsible for another's debt or presence.
الموافاة (al-muwafah): Bringing the principal (the person for whom surety was given) to the claimant.
متعارف (muttaarif): Customary practice; accepted by custom as valid.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If the surety for the person says to the claimant, 'I brought the principal to you,' but the claimant denies, who is believed according to the Hanafi school?
A) The surety, because he is trusted
B) The claimant, because his denial is accepted
C) Both, and the case is dismissed
D) The judge decides based on witnesses

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, a conditional promise to pay the debt upon failure to produce the principal is valid by custom, and the claimant's word is taken in disputes over fulfillment.

Surety Liability When Claimant Is Absent: Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on surety liability with claimant's absence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling on surety liability when the claimant is absent and distinguish the intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Hanifa/Sahibayn and Abu Yusuf regarding appointment of a representative.

Core Idea
When the claimant evades, the surety may be released if the judge appoints an agent — Abu Yusuf's view adopted by the later scholars.

In the previous lesson, we learned that the surety's liability for the money is triggered when the condition (like failure to produce) occurs. But what if the claimant makes it impossible for the surety to fulfill? This passage explores scenarios where the claimant is absent or difficult, and an intra-Hanafi dispute emerges. We will examine the positions and see which one became the operative view.

Guided Path
1
Identify the basic condition
The surety agrees to pay if the principal is not produced by a specified time. The condition is the non-fulfillment of the undertaking.
This is the foundation for all scenarios.
2
Review the five scenarios from Furq al-Karabisi
The passage lists five cases: (1) claimant finds the defendant and keeps him, (2) surety brings principal to agreed place but claimant absent, (3) buyer with option hides, (4) debtor swears to pay but creditor absent, (5) husband gives wife authority to appoint maintenance but she is absent. In all, the mainstream view holds the surety liable.
Scenario 1 is considered non-difficult; scenarios 4 and 5 involve a difficult claimant (mutaannit).
3
Determine the mainstream Hanafi ruling
Abu Hanifa and Muhammad al-Shaybani (Sahibayn) hold that the surety's liability is unconditional once the condition occurs, regardless of claimant's conduct.
This is the position of Zahir al-Riwayah.
4
Identify Abu Yusuf's exception and its basis
Abu Yusuf argues that in scenarios where the claimant is mutaannit (difficult, hiding, or absent), the judge should appoint a wakil to receive the principal or payment, thereby relieving the surety. His basis is preventing injustice and hardship.
Abu Yusuf differentiates between the first scenario (claimant not difficult) and the last two.
5
Trace the later adoption of Abu Yusuf's view
Later scholars (al-muta'akhkhirun), including Qadikhan, adopted Abu Yusuf's opinion as the mufta bihi. The passage notes that even though Al-Faqih Abu al-Layth considered it contrary to the mainstream, the practice became to appoint an agent.
This shows the evolution of the madhhab's practice to ease hardship.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي البزازية وفيما إذا علق المال بعدم الموافاة لا يصدق الكفيل على الموافاة إلا بحجة وبيانه ما ذكره في نظم الفقه : قال الكفيل دفعته إليك اليوم المشروط وأنكره الطالب فالأمر على ما كان في الابتداء ولا يمين على واحد منهما لأن كلاً منهما مدع الكفيل البراءة والطالب الوجوب ولا يمين على المدعي عندنا اهـ. وفي فروق الكرابيسي : رجل كفل بنفس رجل على أنه إن لم يواف به غداً فعليه المال فلم يوافه لكن المدعي وجد المدعى عليه ولازمه حتى الليل يلزمه المال وكذا لو تغيب الطالب فلم يجده لزمه المال هنا فصول: الثاني لو شرط على الكفيل مكاناً فجاء الكفيل بالمكفول به في ذلك المكان وتغيب الطالب لزم المال الكفيل الثالث لو اشترى بالخيار فتوارى البائع الرابع حلف ليقضين دينه اليوم فتغيب الدائن الخامس جعل أمر امرأته بيدها إن لم تصل نفقتها فتغيبت، وعند أبي يوسف ينصب القاضي قيماً في الفصلين الأخيرين لا في الأول لأن الطالب متعنت فيهما لا في الأول اهـ . المصنف وفي الخلاصة : إذا توارى الطالب والبائع نصب القاضي وكيلاً عن الغائب قال الفقيه أبو الليث : هذا خلاف قول أصحابنا وإنما روي في بعض الروايات عن أبي يوسف ولو فعل القاضي فهو حسن .اهـ. وجعل قاضيخان في فتاواه المسائل كلها على الخلاف وأن القاضي ينصب وكيلاً عن الغائب على قول المتأخرين وهو قول أبي يوسف ا هـ.
And in al-Bazzaziyyah: When he suspends the money on non-fulfillment, the surety is not believed on fulfillment except with proof. And its explanation is what he mentioned in Nazm al-Fiqh: The surety said 'I paid it to you today as stipulated' and the claimant denied it, then the matter remains as it was initially, and there is no oath on either of them, because each of them is a claimant: the surety claims discharge, the claimant claims obligation, and there is no oath on the claimant according to us. End. And in Furq al-Karabisi: A man stood surety for the person of a man on the condition that if he does not bring him tomorrow, he owes the money. He did not bring him, but the claimant found the defendant and kept him until night, then the money is binding. Likewise, if the claimant was absent and he did not find him, the money is binding. Here are chapters: Second: If he stipulated a place on the surety, and the surety brought the principal to that place and the claimant was absent, the money is binding on the surety. Third: If he bought with an option and the seller hid. Fourth: If he swore to pay his debt today and the creditor was absent. Fifth: If he gave his wife's matter in her hand on condition that if she does not receive her maintenance, and she became absent. According to Abu Yusuf, the judge appoints an agent in the last two chapters, not in the first, because the claimant is being difficult in those two, not in the first. End. The author: And in al-Khulasah: If the claimant and the seller hide, the judge appoints a representative for the absent one. Al-Faqih Abu al-Layth said: This contradicts the statement of our companions, and it is only narrated in some narrations from Abu Yusuf. If the judge does it, it is good. End. And Qadikhan in his Fatawa made all the matters subject to difference, and that the judge appoints a representative for the absent one according to the later scholars, and it is the saying of Abu Yusuf. End.
Source pages p. 357

Commentary
This passage discusses several scenarios and an intra-Hanafi dispute. First, in al-Bazzaziyyah, it is stated that if the surety claims he fulfilled (brought the principal) but the claimant denies, the surety is not believed without proof; this is consistent with the previous lesson. Then from Furq al-Karabisi, several cases are presented where the surety's liability for the money is triggered even if the claimant is absent or makes it difficult. In the case where the surety brings the principal to the agreed place but the claimant is absent, the surety still owes the money. The same applies if the buyer hides after a sale with option, if a debtor swears to pay but the creditor is absent, or if a husband gives his wife a right to appoint maintenance. For the last two, Abu Yusuf holds that the judge should appoint a representative (an agent) to receive payment or release the surety, because the claimant is being difficult (متعنت). Abu Hanifa and Muhammad (Sahibayn) are reported to disagree, considering the surety's liability unconditional. The author (المصنف) then cites al-Khulasah mentioning that Abu al-Layth al-Samarqandi considered the appointment of a representative as contrary to the mainstream Hanafi view, though it is reported from Abu Yusuf. Later, Qadikhan in his Fatawa adopted the view of the later scholars, which prefers Abu Yusuf's opinion, allowing the judge to appoint a representative. This shows the evolution of the madhhab's practice, where the mufta bihi eventually adopted Abu Yusuf's view for these hardship cases.

Key Terms
متعنت (mutaannit): One who makes it difficult, causes hardship by hiding or absconding.
وكيل (wakil): A representative appointed by the judge to act on behalf of the absent claimant.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the later Hanafi scholars (المتأخرون), what should the judge do if the claimant hides after the surety brings the principal to the agreed place?
A) The judge must wait for the claimant to appear
B) The judge appoints a representative (وكيل) to receive the principal
C) The surety is released without further action
D) The surety must pay the money immediately

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, when the claimant is difficult or absent, the later adopted view allows the judge to appoint an agent to receive the principal, relieving the surety of liability.

Kafalah with Unspecified Debt: When Is the Surety Liable?
Ruling on kafalah with unspecified debt and effect of acknowledgment

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on kafalah with unspecified debt and explain the effect of the principal's acknowledgment on the surety's liability when the surety denies.

Core Idea
Specifying the debt amount is not a condition for kafalah's validity, but it determines enforceability against a denying surety.

In kafalah, the amount of debt is often known, but what if it is unspecified at the time of guarantee? The Hanafi school addresses this with a nuanced rule: the contract itself is valid, but the surety's liability depends on specification and acknowledgment. This lesson unpacks that ruling through the scenarios presented by the author, focusing on the interplay between the surety's wording, the principal's acknowledgment, and the surety's denial.

Guided Path
1
Identify the key legal question
Determine whether specification of the debt amount is a condition for the validity of the kafalah contract itself or merely a factor for enforceability. The text indicates it is not a condition but a restriction to avoid dispute.
The passage uses 'qayd' (restriction) not 'shart' (condition).
2
Analyze the unspecified debt scenario
Examine the case where the surety says 'upon me what you claim' and later the claimant specifies an amount, the principal acknowledges, but the surety denies. According to the text, the surety's word is taken with an oath on knowledge, and he is not liable.
This is the core ruling: the surety's denial is accepted because the amount was not fixed at contract.
3
Distinguish specified vs unspecified
Contrast with the case where the surety names a specific amount (e.g., 'upon me the hundred'). In that case, the surety is bound by that exact amount because the debt was specified.
Specification makes the obligation clear and enforceable.
4
Extract the operative ruling (mufta bihi)
Synthesize the criterion: The surety is liable only if the debt amount was specified at the time of kafalah, or if the principal acknowledges and the surety does not deny (i.e., remains silent). If the surety denies, his denial is accepted.
This is the relied-upon position from Fatawa Qadikhan.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

His statement: 'It is restricted by mentioning the money at the time of the claim.' He meant by 'mentioning' its mention and specification, not description of its quality, that it is good or a dinar. So a man said: 'If I do not bring him to you tomorrow, then he is liable for the hundred.' He did not bring him tomorrow, so he is liable for the hundred, and he is not forced to pay five hundred; the surety is liable for what he acknowledged. And this is not like if he said: 'If I do not bring him to you tomorrow, then I am a guarantor for what you claim against him.' If he did not bring him tomorrow, then the claimant claimed money against him, he is not liable for the money. Likewise, if he said: 'If I do not bring him tomorrow, then what you claim against him is upon me.' If he did not bring him tomorrow, then the claimant claimed money against him, he is not liable. This is in Fatawa Qadikhan after reviewing a correct copy. And his statement 'If I do not hand him over to you tomorrow' is equivalent to 'If I do not bring him to you' as in al-Khaniyah. And if the surety for the person said: 'If the principal is absent from you, then I am a guarantor for what is upon him.' Then the principal went to Kufa, and the claimant did not demand him, then the surety handed him over after his return from Kufa, then the surety is liable for the money because he attached it to absence. And if he said: 'I have stood surety for the person of so-and-so; if he is absent and I do not bring him, then I am a guarantor for what is upon him.' Then he was absent before he brought him, so the money is binding. This is like if he said: 'If he is absent before I bring him to you.' And if he said: 'If he is absent and I do not bring him to you, then I am a guarantor for what is upon him.' This means that he will bring him after the absence. This is in al-Khaniyah. And in it also: If he stood surety for the person of a man on the condition that he bring him when the judge sits, and if he does not bring him, then upon him the thousand that the claimant has against him. The judge did not sit for days, and the claimant demanded and he did not bring him, then there is nothing on the surety of the money, because he suspended the guarantee of the money on non-fulfillment when the judge sits. In al-Khaniyah: 'If I do not bring him, then I have this money for you' is binding, because 'I have' when used for debt means obligation. Likewise if he said 'this money is for you'. And it is restricted by non-fulfillment to avoid what is in al-Bazzaziyyah: He stood surety for the person on condition that the claimant demand whenever he hands over; if he does not hand over, then upon him what is upon him. Then the principal died, and the claimant demanded delivery and was unable, then the money is not binding because the demand for delivery after death is invalid. So when the demand is invalid, the inability that causes liability does not occur, so it is not obligatory. End. Then he was imprisoned with right or without right, or fell ill that prevented his presence, the money becomes binding after three days. End. And in Wukalat Munyat al-Mufti: He said: 'If I bring him to you tomorrow, then upon me what is upon him.' Then he brought him, he is not liable for the money, because he made its binding conditional upon kindness to him. End. Meaning it is a suspension upon something not customary, so the surety is invalid. His statement: 'And whoever claims against another a hundred dinars, and a man says: If I do not bring him to you tomorrow, then upon him the hundred.' He did not bring him tomorrow, then upon him the hundred, because the condition exists, so the money is binding. It is restricted by mentioning the money at the time of the claim, because if a man attaches to another and says: 'I have a claim against you' without specifying it, and then a person stands surety for the person on condition that if he does not bring him tomorrow, then upon him what the claimant claims. He did not bring him, and the claimant claimed a thousand dirhams against him, and the principal confirmed it, and the surety denied it, then the word is the surety's with an oath on knowledge. End. His statement: 'It is restricted by mentioning the money at the time of the claim.' He meant by 'mentioning' its mention and specification, not description of its quality that it is good or...
Source pages p. 357

Commentary
This passage is a collection of rulings and comments from various sources on the validity of kafalah when the amount of debt is not specified at the time of contracting. The author explains that mentioning the amount is not a condition for the validity of the kafalah itself, but rather a specification to avoid disputes. The key example: if a surety says 'If I do not bring him, then upon me what the claimant claims,' and later the claimant claims a specific amount and the principal acknowledges it, but the surety denies, the surety's word is taken with an oath (on knowledge) because the amount was not fixed at the time of the contract. Conversely, if the surety says 'If I do not bring him, then upon me a hundred' (specified), he is liable for that exact amount. The author cites Fatawa Qadikhan that the mere claim without specification does not bind the surety. Various wordings are discussed: 'I am a guarantor' vs explicit promise, the effect of absence, the condition of the judge's session, etc. The overall ruling is that the surety's liability depends on the exact wording and conditions, and custom plays a role in determining what is binding. The passage ends with a note that mentioning the money is for specification, not description of its quality.

Key Terms
قيد ببيان المال (qayd bi bayan al-mal): Restriction by specification of the money: the condition that the debt amount be mentioned or identified.
إقرار المطلوب (iqrar al-matlub): Acknowledgment by the principal (the person for whom surety is given) of the debt.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A surety says: 'If I do not bring the principal tomorrow, then upon me what the claimant claims.' The principal acknowledges a debt of 1000 dirhams, but the surety denies. What is the ruling?
A) The surety must pay 1000 dirhams
B) The surety's word is taken with an oath, and he does not pay
C) The claimant must produce witnesses
D) The surety is released from all liability

Key Takeaway
For kafalah of an unspecified debt, the surety's denial of liability after the principal's acknowledgment is accepted with an oath, making him not bound unless the amount was specified at contract.

Binding vs Non-Binding Conditional Promises in Kafalah
Explaining various conditions and phrasings in kafalah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between binding and non-binding conditional promises in kafalah based on the precise wording and conventionality of the condition.

Core Idea
In kafalah, the surety's liability depends on the precise wording of the condition and whether it is conventionally used to create obligation.

The Hanafi school examines conditional promises in kafalah with great precision. A surety might say, 'If I do not bring the debtor, I am liable for his debt,' or 'I am a guarantor.' The difference in wording determines whether the surety is bound. This lesson unpacks several scenarios from the classical texts, showing how the same outcome can produce different rulings based on the phrasing.

Guided Path
1
Identify the wording of the condition
Read the conditional phrase carefully. Does it mention a specific debt? Does it use 'upon me' (عليّ) or 'I am a guarantor' (أنا ضامن) or 'I have this property for you' (عندي لك هذا المال)?
The key is whether the wording clearly indicates an obligation or merely an acknowledgement.
2
Determine if the wording is conventional for creating liability
In Hanafi fiqh, certain phrases like 'I am a guarantor' without mentioning the debt are not binding for any specific amount unless the debtor admits. Phrases like 'upon me' with a specific sum are binding. Also, conditions must be customary (mut'arraf).
Unconventional conditions, like 'If I treat him well, then I am liable', do not create liability.
3
Check the fulfillment of the condition
The condition must be fulfilled exactly as stated. For example, if the surety says 'if the judge sits' and the judge never sits, the condition is not triggered.
The occurrence or non-occurrence of the condition determines whether the surety's obligation arises.
4
Apply the ruling: binding or not
If the wording is clear and conventional, and the condition occurs, the surety is bound to pay the specified debt. Otherwise, no liability attaches.
The ruling may also depend on whether the principal admits the debt.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
دينار فقال رجل إن لم يوافك به غداً فعليه المائة فلم يواف به غداً فعليه المائة ولا يجبر على به خمسمائة كان الكفيل ضامناً لما أقر ، وليس هذا كما لو قال إن لم أوافك به غداً فأنا ضامن . ادعيت عليه فلم يواف به غداً فادعى الطالب عليه مالاً لا يلزمه المال، وكذا لو قال إن لم أوافك غداً فما ادعيت عليه فهو علي فلم يواف به غداً فادعى الطالب عليه مالاً لا يلزمه؛ كذا في فتاوى قاضيخان بعد مراجعة نسخة صحيحة. وقوله «إن لم أدفعه إليك غداً» بمنزلة «إن لم أوافك به كما في الخانية. ولو قال الكفيل بالنفس إن غاب عنك المكفول فأنا ضامن لما عليه فغاب المكفول إلى الكوفة ولم يطلبه الطالب ثم دفعه الكفيل إليه بعد رجوعه من الكوفة فالكفيل ضامن للمال لأنه علقها بالغيبة، ولو قال قد كفلت لك بنفس فلان فإن غاب ولم أوافك فأنا ضامن لما عليه فغاب قبل أن يوافي لزمه المال، وهو بمنزلة ما لو قال إن غاب قبل أن أوافيك به. ولو قال إن غاب فلم أوافك به فأنا ضامن لما عليه فهذا على أن يوافيه بعد الغيبة؛ كذا في الخانية وفيها أيضاً : ولو كفل بنفس رجل على أن يوافي به إذا جلس القاضي، فإن لم يواف به فعليه الألف التي للطالب عليه فلم يجلس القاضي أياماً وطلب المدعي ولم يأتي به فلا شيء على الكفيل من المال لأنه علق الكفالة بالمال بعدم الموافاة إذا جلس القاضي ا هـ . وقوله في الكتاب فأنا ضامن ليس بقيد ففي الخانية: إن لم أواف به فعندي لك هذا المال لزمه لأن عندي إذا استعمل في الدين يراد به الوجوب، وكذا لو قال إلى هذا المال. وقيد بعدم الموافاة للاحتراز عما في البزازية : كفل بنفسه على أنه طالبه متی سلمه فإن لم يسلمه فعليه ما عليه ومات المطلوب وطالبه بالتسليم وعجز لا يلزمه المال لأن المطالبة بالتسليم بعد الموت لا تصح فإذا لم تصح المطالبة لم يتحقق العجز الموجب للزوم المال فلا يجب ا هـ . وفي القنية : كفل بنفسه وقال إن عجزت عن تسليمه إلى ثلاثة أيام فعلي المال ثم حبس بحق أو بغير حق أو رضي مرضاً يتعذر إحضاره يلزمه المال بعد الثلاثة ا هـ . وفي وكالة منية المفتي: قال إن وافيتك به غداً فعلي ما عليه ثم وافى به لم يلزمه المال لأنه شرط لزومه إن أحسن إليه ا.هـ. يعني أنه تعليق بغير المتعارف فلم تصح الكفالة .
A dinar, so a man said: If I do not meet you with him tomorrow, then upon him is one hundred. So he did not meet him with him tomorrow, so upon him is one hundred. And he is not compelled to pay five hundred; the surety is liable for what he acknowledged. And this is not like if he said: If I do not meet you with him tomorrow, then I am a guarantor. He claimed against him, and he did not meet with him tomorrow, then the claimant claimed property against him, the property is not binding on him. Similarly, if he said: If I do not meet you tomorrow, then whatever you claim against him is upon me, then he did not meet him tomorrow, then the claimant claimed property against him, it is not binding on him. Thus in Fatawa Qadikhan after reviewing a correct copy. And his saying: 'If I do not hand it over to you tomorrow' is like 'If I do not meet you with him' as in al-Khaniyya. And if the surety for the person said: If the principal is absent from you, then I am a guarantor for what is upon him, so the principal went to Kufa and the claimant did not demand him, then the surety handed him over after his return from Kufa, the surety is liable for the property because he conditioned it on absence. And in al-Qunyah: He stood surety for the person and said: If I am unable to hand him over within three days, then upon me is the property. Then he was imprisoned by right or without right, or he fell ill in a way that makes it difficult to bring him, the property becomes binding on him after three days. And in Wakalat Munyat al-Mufti: He said: If I meet you with him tomorrow, then upon me is what is upon him. Then he met with him, the property does not become binding on him because he conditioned its necessity on [؟] good treatment. It means it is a condition in an unconventional way, so the surety is not valid.

Commentary
This passage is a collection of rulings from various fatwa collections on the topic of kafalah (suretyship). The author, al-Marghinani, explains different scenarios where a person makes a conditional promise to bring the debtor or pay the debt. The first ruling states: if a person says, 'If I do not bring the debtor tomorrow, then upon me is the debt of one hundred,' and he fails to bring him, the debt becomes binding. However, if the surety acknowledges a larger amount (e.g., five hundred), he is only liable for what he acknowledged. The author then distinguishes this from phrasing where the surety says, 'I am a guarantor' without specifying the debt; in that case, if the claimant later claims a specific amount, it is not binding on the surety. Similarly, if the surety says, 'Whatever you claim is upon me,' it is not binding unless he specifies. The phrase 'If I do not hand it over to you tomorrow' is equivalent to 'If I do not meet you with him.' Various scenarios are discussed: conditional upon absence, upon the judge's sitting, etc. The key principle is that the wording of the condition must be clear and conventional for the surety to be binding. The passage also notes that saying 'I have this property for you' implies obligation, while saying 'upon me' may not if the condition is unusual. These rulings are based on the Hanafi school and reflect the nuanced approach to determining liability in suretyship contracts.

Key Terms
كفالة (kafalah): Suretyship; a contract where one person (the surety) guarantees the presence of the debtor or payment of a debt.
ضامن (damin): Guarantor or surety; the person who becomes liable for the debt or for bringing the principal.
موافاة (muwafat): The act of meeting or bringing the debtor at the appointed time and place.
عندي (‘indi): Literally 'I have'; used in debt to indicate immediate obligation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) The surety is liable for any debt the claimant proves.
B) The surety is not liable for any specific amount unless the condition specifies the debt.
C) The surety is liable for the debt even if not specified.
D) The surety is not liable because the phrase 'I am a guarantor' is not conventional.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi kafalah, the surety's liability hinges on the precise wording of the conditional promise: specific, conventional phrasing creates binding obligation; vague or unconventional phrasing does not.

Condition of Specifying Debt at Time of Claim and Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Commentary on matn condition for debt specification and intra-Hanafi dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on specification of debt in kafalah and distinguish the views of Abu Hanifa/Abu Yusuf from Muhammad al-Shaybani.

Core Idea
The majority Hanafi view upholds suretyship if the debt is later specified, while Muhammad requires initial specification.

This passage is a commentary on a matn ruling in kafalah that states the condition of specifying the debt at the time of claim. The author then explores a subtle case where the debt was initially unspecified, leading to a dispute among the Hanafi imams. Understanding this ikhtilaf clarifies when a surety becomes liable and highlights the role of custom (urf) in Hanafi jurisprudence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the matn's condition
The matn requires that the debt be specified ('bayan al-mal') at the time of the claim (al-da'wa). This is the starting point for the dispute.
The matn's ruling applies only when the debt is already specified.
2
Recognize the fact pattern causing the dispute
A person makes a claim without specifying the amount, and a surety guarantees to bring the debtor or pay a certain sum. Later, the creditor specifies the amount. Does the surety become liable?
The vagueness ('ibham') at the moment of suretyship is the key.
3
State the position of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf
They hold that the surety is liable when the creditor later specifies the amount and demands it. Their proof: custom allows initial vagueness; the subsequent specification retroactively validates the claim.
This is the mufta bihi (operative) position.
4
State the position of Muhammad al-Shaybani
Muhammad argues the surety is not liable because the original claim was invalid (due to lack of specification), so the suretyship for the person is also invalid, and thus the suretyship for the money fails.
He emphasizes strict procedural validity.
5
Analyze the tarjih and practical outcome
The majority view is relied upon due to custom and the principle of validating contracts where possible. Practically, a surety who guarantees without knowing the exact debt may still become liable once the creditor specifies.
This ruling reflects the Hanafi tendency to uphold transactions despite minor defects.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (ومن ادعى على آخر مائة دينار فقال رجل إن لم أوافك به غدا فعليه المائة فلم يواف به غداً فعليه المائة لوجود الشرط فلزم المال. قيد ببيان المال عند الدعوى لأنه لو تعلق رجل بآخر وقال لي عليك دعوى ولم يبينها فكفله إنسان بالنفس على أنه إن لم يواف به غداً فعليه مائة دينار ففيه اختلاف قالا : إذا لم يوافه به لزمته إذا ادعاها المدعي. وقال محمد : لا تلزمه لأنه لما لم يبينها وقت الدعوى لم تصح الدعوى فلم يجب حضوره إلى مجلس القاضي فلم تصح الكفالة بالنفس فلم تصح بالمال لأنها مبنية عليها ولهما أنه يمكن تصحيحها لأن العادة جرت بالإبهام في الدعاوى في غير مجلس القضاء ثم يبينوها عنده دفعاً للحيل فصحت الدعوى والملازمة على احتمال البيان فإذا بين بعده انصرف إلى البيان أولاً فظهر الكفالة بالنفس فصحت بالمال حملاً على أن الكفيل كان يعلم خصوص المال المدعى به تصحيحاً لكلام العاقل ما أمكن والحاصل أنا لا نحكم حال صدورها بالفساد بل الأمر موقوف على ظهور الدعوى بذلك القدر فإذا ظهرت ظهر أنه إنما كفل بالقدر المدعى به. وفي الخلاصة : كفل بنفس رجل على أنه إن لم يواف به غداً فعليه ألف درهم ولم يقل التي عليه فمضى الغد ولم يواف به وفلان يقول لا شيء علي والطالب يدعي ألفاً والكفيل ينكر وجوبه على الأصيل فعلى الكفيل ألف درهم عند أبي حنيفة وأبي يوسف في قوله الأول، وفي قوله الآخر وهو قول محمد لا شيء عليه ا هـ . وهذا يقتضي أن الحاصل أن أبا حنيفة وحده، ويستفاد بها أن الألف تجب على الكفيل بمجرد دعوى المكفول له وإن كان الكفيل ينكر وجوبه ؛ كذا في فتح القدير.
His saying: (And whoever claims against another a hundred dinars, and a man says: If I do not meet you with him tomorrow, then upon him is the hundred, so he did not meet him with him tomorrow, then upon him is the hundred because the condition exists, so the money is binding. He restricted it with specification of the money at the time of the claim. Because if a man attaches himself to another and says: I have a claim against you, but does not specify it, and a person stands surety for the person on condition that if he does not meet him with him tomorrow, then upon him is a hundred dinars, there is a dispute. The two (Abu Yusuf and Abu Hanifa according to the first view) said: When he does not meet him with him, it becomes binding on him when the plaintiff claims it. And Muhammad said: It does not become binding on him, because since he did not specify it at the time of the claim, the claim is not valid, so his presence in the court is not obligatory, so the suretyship for the person is not valid, so the suretyship for the money is not valid because it is based on it. And for them (the two) that it can be corrected because custom has proceeded with vagueness in claims outside the court, then they specify them there to avoid tricks, so the claim and the obligation on the probability of specification are valid. So when he specifies afterwards, it refers back to the first specification, so the suretyship for the person appears, and thus the suretyship for the money is valid, carrying it on the basis that the surety knew the specific property claimed, to validate the speech of a rational person as much as possible. The conclusion is that we do not judge it as invalid at the time of issuance, but rather it is suspended on the appearance of the claim to that extent. So when it appears, it appears that he only stood surety for the amount claimed. And in al-Khulasa: He stood surety for the person of a man on condition that if he does not meet him with him tomorrow, then upon him is a thousand dirhams, and he did not say 'which is upon him,' so the next day passed and he did not meet him with him, and the principal says 'nothing is upon me,' and the claimant claims a thousand, and the surety denies its obligation on the principal. So upon the surety is a thousand dirhams according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf in his first opinion, and in his other opinion, which is the saying of Muhammad, nothing is upon him. Thus in Fath al-Qadir.
Source pages p. 358

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the matn statement about suretyship when a person claims a specific amount and a surety promises to bring the debtor or pay. The author notes that the matn restricts the ruling to when the debt is specified at the time of the claim. He then explains the reason: if a person claims a debt without specifying the amount, and a surety offers to guarantee for an unspecified amount, there is disagreement among the Hanafi imams. According to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf (in his first opinion), if the surety fails to bring the debtor and the plaintiff subsequently specifies the amount, the surety becomes liable. Their reasoning is that custom allows for vagueness initially, and the subsequent specification validates the claim retroactively. Imam Muhammad disagrees: since the claim was invalid for lack of specification, the suretyship for the person (to attend court) is also invalid, and consequently the suretyship for the money is invalid. The text then cites al-Khulasa, which states that according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf's first opinion, the surety is liable even if he denies the debt, while in Muhammad's view he is not. The passage ends by noting that this implies only Abu Hanifa holds this view (as per Fath al-Qadir), meaning the later opinion of Abu Yusuf aligns with Muhammad. The principle is that the surety's liability hinges on the validity of the underlying claim.

Key Terms
بيان المال (bayan al-mal): Specification of the amount of the debt (or property) at the time of the claim or suretyship.
إبهام (ibham): Vagueness or lack of specification in a claim.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the dispute in the text, if a surety says, 'If I do not bring him tomorrow, upon me is a hundred dirhams,' but the debt was not specified at the time, and later the claimant claims a hundred dirhams, what is Imam Muhammad's ruling?
A) The surety is liable because the condition is met.
B) The surety is not liable because the original claim was invalid.
C) The surety is liable only if the debtor admits.
D) The surety is liable only if he knew the exact amount.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, the surety's liability for a debt is valid even if the debt was unspecified at the time of suretyship, as long as the creditor later specifies the amount upon making the claim.

Surety for a Third Party's Debt: Istihsan vs Qiyas
Expansion of a condition in kafalah and the resulting intra-Hanafi dispute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two Hanafi positions on the validity of a surety guaranteeing a stranger's debt and identify the legal basis (istihsan vs qiyas) for each.

Core Idea
A surety can guarantee a stranger's debt by istihsan, but qiyas forbids it.

This lesson examines an internal Hanafi dispute regarding a condition in suretyship (kafalah). The matn (text of Kanz al-Daqa'iq) restricts the guaranteed debt to the principal (the person for whom the surety stands). When the surety agrees to pay a debt owed by a third party, the Hanafi imams differ: Imam Muhammad validates it by istihsan, while Imam Abu Yusuf invalidates it by qiyas. Understanding this dispute sharpens your appreciation of how the Hanafi school sometimes resolves conflicts between rigorous analogy and practical convenience.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Matn's Condition
The matn states that the property (debt) must be on the principal (the one for whom the surety stands). Locate this condition in the Arabic text.
The condition is: 'وقيد بكون المال على المكفول بالنفس'.
2
Recognize the Exceptional Scenario
Consider what happens if the surety guarantees to pay a debt that is not owed by the principal but by a third party. The text gives an example: standing surety for a man's person on condition that if he is not produced, the surety will pay what the claimant has against another person.
This scenario triggered the dispute.
3
State Imam Muhammad's Position and Its Basis
Imam Muhammad holds that such a suretyship is valid (sahih) by istihsan. Istihsan departs from strict analogy for reasons of custom and ease of transactions.
The text says: 'جاز ذلك استحساناً وهو قول محمد'.
4
State Imam Abu Yusuf's Position and Its Basis
Imam Abu Yusuf holds that it is invalid (batil) by qiyas. By analogy, since the surety cannot guarantee a debt not owed by the principal, it is akin to guaranteeing a stranger's debt without his consent.
The text says: 'وفي القياس لا يجوز وهو قول أبي يوسف'.
5
Note the Conflicting Transmission Regarding Imam Abu Hanifa
Check the conflicting report in al-Muhit that places Abu Hanifa with Abu Yusuf, reversing the typical alignment. This shows the fluidity of transmissions in early Hanafi texts.
The text says: 'وفي المحيط: جعل الخلاف على العكس وجعل أبا حنيفة مع أبي يوسف'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقيد بكون المال على المكفول بالنفس لأنه لو كان على غيره ففيه اختلاف كما لو كفل بنفس رجل على أنه إن لم يواف به في يوم كذا فعليه ما للطالب على فلان آخر جاز ذلك استحساناً وهو قول محمد، وفي القياس لا يجوز ولو قول أبي يوسف؛ ذكره قاضيخان. وفي المحيط : جعل الخلاف على العكس وجعل أبا حنيفة مع أبي يوسف. وفي السراج الوهاج وكذا لو كفل بنفس رجل على أنه إن لم يواف به غداً كان كفيلاً بنفس رجل آخر كان على هذا الاختلاف ا هـ.
And he restricted it to the property being on the principal (the one for whom the surety stands). Because if it were on someone else, there is a dispute. For example, if he stands surety for the person of a man on condition that if he does not bring him on such a day, then upon him is what the claimant has against another person, that is permissible by istihsan, and it is the saying of Muhammad. And by analogy it is not permissible, and it is the saying of Abu Yusuf. Qadikhan mentioned it. And in al-Muhit, the dispute is reversed, and it made Abu Hanifa with Abu Yusuf. And in al-Siraj al-Wahhaj: similarly, if he stands surety for the person of a man on condition that if he does not bring him tomorrow, he becomes a surety for the person of another man, then it follows this dispute.
Source pages p. 359

Commentary
This passage discusses a further condition in the matn: the debt for which the surety is liable must be the debt of the principal (the person he is guaranteeing to bring). If the surety guarantees to pay the debt of a third party (not the principal), there is disagreement among the Hanafi imams. Imam Muhammad approves of this by istihsan (juristic preference), considering it valid because it facilitates transactions and avoids difficulty. Abu Yusuf rejects it by qiyas (analogy), arguing that since the surety is not liable for the principal's own debt, he cannot be liable for a stranger's debt. The text then notes a conflicting transmission in al-Muhit, which places Abu Hanifa on the side of Abu Yusuf, indicating a reversal of the positions. The same disagreement applies if the surety guarantees to stand surety for another person's presence (i.e., becomes a surety for a surety). The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is not explicitly stated, but the mention of istihsan as Muhammad's view suggests it may be preferred for practical reasons.

Key Terms
استحسان (istihsan): Juristic preference; a method of reasoning that departs from strict analogy in favor of convenience or public interest.
قياس (qiyas): Analogical reasoning; applying the ruling of an original case to a new case based on a common effective cause.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the basis for Imam Muhammad's permissibility of a surety guaranteeing a third party's debt?
A) Qiyas (analogy)
B) Istihsan (juristic preference)
C) Ijma (consensus)
D) Nass (textual evidence)

Key Takeaway
A surety for a third party's debt is valid by istihsan (Muhammad) but invalid by qiyas (Abu Yusuf), reflecting a key intra-Hanafi methodological tension.

Kafalah — Surety
intermediate
8 min
The Surety's Liability Requires the Debtor's Admission
Critical note on a condition for surety liability

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the condition of the debtor's admission for surety liability in Hanafi law and identify the apparent conflict between Ibn Nujaym and Qadikhan on this point.

Core Idea
A surety is not bound unless the debtor admits the debt.

In the contract of kafalah (suretyship), the Hanafi school imposes a subtle but crucial condition: the surety's liability does not arise merely from the claimant's assertion. The debtor himself must acknowledge the debt. This principle protects the surety from being held liable on false claims. The commentator Ibn Nujaym, in Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq, emphasizes this condition and then notes that it appears to conflict with a statement in Qadikhan's Sharh al-Jami' al-Saghir, signalling an internal debate among Hanafi authorities.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act and its Legal Issue
Focus on the contract of kafalah (suretyship) and the specific scenario from the matn (Kanz al-Daqa'iq). The issue is under what conditions the surety becomes liable for the debt.
The matn likely presents a case where a person claims a debt from another, and a third party acts as surety.
2
Locate the Condition Stated
The text says 'لا بد في مسألة الكتاب من إقرار المدعى عليه' – it is necessary for the debtor to admit (iqrar) the debt. This is the condition being asserted.
Iqrar here means the debtor's confession that the debt is due.
3
Understand the Legal Effect of the Condition
Without the debtor's admission, the surety is not liable. The claimant's mere assertion is insufficient. This protects the surety from potential fraud or false claims.
This is a substantive requirement, not merely procedural.
4
Note the Apparent Conflict
Ibn Nujaym states that this condition 'yukhalif' (conflicts with) what is found in Qadikhan's commentary on Al-Jami' al-Saghir. This indicates a difference of interpretation among Hanafi scholars.
The exact contrary position is not fully given in the passage, but it suggests that Qadikhan may not have required the debtor's admission in the same scenario.
5
Assess the Practical Consequence (Thamarah)
The difference matters in practice: under Ibn Nujaym's ruling, if the debtor denies the debt, the surety is free. Under Qadikhan's possible view, the surety might still be liable based on the claimant's word or other evidence.
The mufta bihi (operative position) in later Hanafi fatwa is that the debtor's admission is required, as it aligns with the principle that no one can be bound by another's claim without proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا بد في مسألة الكتاب من إقرار المدعى عليه بالمائة (الخ يخالف هذا ما في شرح الجامع الصغير لقاضيخان حيث
And it is necessary in the issue of the book (the matn) that the defendant admits to the hundred. This conflicts with what is in Sharh al-Jami' al-Saghir by Qadikhan where...
Source pages p. 359

Commentary
This short passage is a critical note from the commentator (likely Ibn Nujaym) pointing out a requirement in the matn's scenario that the debtor must admit to owing the hundred dinars. He then notes that this condition differs from what is found in Qadikhan's commentary on al-Jami' al-Saghir, implying a disagreement or alternative view. The text is incomplete, so the full explanation is not available, but the point is that the surety's liability is contingent on the principal's admission of the debt, not merely the claimant's assertion. This is a refinement of the earlier ruling, ensuring that the surety is not bound by a potentially false claim.

Key Terms
إقرار (iqrar): Admission or confession by the debtor that the debt is due.
كفالة (kafalah): Suretyship or guaranty contract.
المدعى عليه (al-mudda'a alayhi): The defendant or debtor in a claim.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The claimant's oath
B) The debtor's admission (iqrar)
C) A court judgment
D) The surety's own admission

Key Takeaway
A surety's liability in Hanafi law is conditional upon the debtor's admission of the debt, protecting the surety from baseless claims, though this condition is subject to internal debate.
